<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Farina Volatica</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="polenta(n)">THe flyinge meale of the mylle, commonly called mill
stuffe: beinge not one, but the meale commonlye of
manye graynes together, muste nedes possesse suche
facultyes, as the composition maketh: (As wheat is
of temperament whot in the firste degre, but neither moi&#173;
steth nor dryeth manifestly: And barlye cold and drye in the
first degree. Yet dothe wheat flower manifestly moiste, as
also Barly meale dothe, somewhat more then the grayne: 
thoughe Polenta doe drye muche more.) But <term lang="la">Farina Volatica</term>,
is commonly colde and drye of complexion, and therfore is
conueniently put into restrictiues, and consolidatiues. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Galenus de a&#173;
linentis. li. 1. 
ae de atenuan
te Victus ra&#173;
tione</term>. </note>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Felles quium</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="gall(n)" lexeme="choler(n)">CHoler naturalle, or the gaule, called in Latyne <term lang="la">Fel</term>,
and <term lang="la">Bilis</term>, in Greke <term lang="gk">&#x03A7;&#x03BF;&#x03BB;&#x03B7;</term>: Is of all iuyces in every li&#173;

ble, according to the creatures theyr temperamentes. For
as the gaule of the Hogge, is of al other beasts the coldest:
so is the gaule of the shepe, of the gote, of the Bull. &#38;c. eue&#173;
ry one in theyr degrees whotter. 
But the gaules of fowles, (which only Lanfranke hath,
are bothe sharper and dryer, then of . iiii. foted beastes. Of 



</page>

<page no="38">
the whych also, the galles of Hennes and Pertriches, are
moste alowed in medicyn<damage>e</damage>s. The gaules of the Kite, of the
Egle, and suche like, are excedinge sharpe and biting: yea
of a grene coloure, and somtime <damage>sw</damage>orte. And therfore Ga&#173;
len doth warne the same, that Lanfranke neglecteth not:
That is, to put vnto any of them the iuyce of Fenell, <term lang="la">Mel</term>,
and <term lang="la">Opobal<damage>s</damage>amum</term>: whensoeuer you vse them to the eyes. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Fermentum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">CAlled in Greke <term lang="gk">&#x03B6;&#x03CD;&#x03BC;&#x03B7;</term>: is meanlye whot, consistinge of

nesse, and also of heate, sprunge of putrefaction: and

depthe, grosse spirites, and superfluous humores, and dyge&#173;

franke hathe so ouertly spoken therof in hys antidotarye. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="ficus(n)">Ficus. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="fig(n)">THe Figge (and also the tree,) is called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03C5;&#x03BA;&#x03B7;</term>. 
The drye Figges haue a certaine subtiltye, and doe
heate in the firste degre, or in the beginnynge of the
seconde: wherfore as well they as theyr decoction, doe con&#173;
cocte, and digest hard swellinges. But where conction is
chiefly required, they ought to be mixed with <term lang="la">Farina tr<damage>trieticea</damage></term>. 
And where rather digestion, <term lang="la">Hordeacea</term>. Fatte fygges haue
the more concoctiue power, and soure figges and doe bothe
scoure more and digest. Grene fygges are weaker yet doe
bothe <expan type="">the</expan> moyst, (which hurt the stomache) &#38;  the dry purge
the belly. But the fluxe by them moued, is sone stayed. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gkt">Syce</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Galenus de
symp. li. 8. </term></note>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="fistula(n)">Fistula. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="bosom(n)" lexeme="sinus(n)">FIstula saythe Galen, (in his boke of tumores against na&#173;
ture,) <term lang="la">Sinus est angustus &#38;  longus. &#38;c.</term> A fistule is a strey&#173;
ght and long bosome, lyke to other bosomes, whyche is
muche drawen together, and after also agayne opened. 
That caue or holowe roome in the fleshe, which I haue



</page>

<page no="39">
Englished a bosome, is there also of Galen thus defyned. 
When rotten mater, dothe excoriate bodyes, and deuyde
the conteinynge from the conteined, or s<damage>u</damage>biecte: And far&#173;
ther, it beinge emptyed howe so euer, the partes dissidente
one from an other, can not recouer their pristinate consti&#173;
tution: thys affecte is <damage>called</damage> Sinus. Whiche (if it be not with
spede cured) dothe gather to it selfe Callum, and in tyme be
commeth harde: and then can not be adglued to his partes
subiecte. &#38;c. And then doubtlesse it is no longer Sinus, but 
Fistula. </xpln>
<note type="m">Sinus. </note>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Flammula. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="spearwort(n)"><term lang="la">FLammula</term>: is one of the kindes of Ran<expan type="">un</expan>culus, (wherof ther
are very manye,) as are also the herbes, called of Lan&#173;
franke and others, <term lang="la">Apium risus</term>, and <term lang="la">Apium H&#230;morrhoid<expan type="">um</expan></term>,
and also <term lang="la">Pesmilui</term> (as above vnder <term lang="la">Apium</term>:) and hathe so exce&#173;
ding burninge, and blisteringe powre: (as haue also all the
rest,) that it exulcerateth the entraelles, but chiefly the ly&#173;
uer; and therfore is moste pernicious fro sheepe, and other
beastes feedinge theron. It is called also of some <term lang="la">Lanceola</term>,
and in Englyshe speare worte, for that the leaues therof,
haue the forme of a speare head. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Lanceola</term>. </note>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Flos&#230;ris. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">IS called in Greke <term lang="gk">&#x03C7;&#x03B1;&#x03BB;&#x03BA;&#x03BF;&#x03C5; &#x03B1;&#x03BD;&#x03B8;&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</term>: some men saith Dioscori&#173;
des haue called it <term lang="la">Claucram Veterum psegma</term>, that is the scra&#173;
ping, rubbing, or dust of old brasse naues or rivets. But
a litle after chewing the makinge therof, he saythe it com&#173;
meth of melted brasse, by pouring cold water theron forth
with as it runneth by certain conducts, but of the fornace. 
Wherby this <term lang="la">Flos aris</term> breaketh forthe, and congealeth in
many small partes, whiche of theyr formes, some haue cal&#173;
led litle graines. It is by the sentence of Galen, of more sub<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
til substance, then as <term lang="la">Vstum</term>, or <term lang="la">Squama aris</term>: doth vehem<expan type="">en</expan>tly
scoure, &#38;  also biteth much. Wherfore wyth collyries made
therof, the greate roughnesse or ruggednesse of the eye
browes are taken away, &#38;  other superfluous excrescentes. 



</page>

<page no="40">
And although it be (as you see) moste evident, that <term lang="la">Aeru&#173;
go</term> and <term lang="la">Flos aria</term> are two thinges: yet maye the one in some
cases, be circumspectly bled for the other: after the myndes
of divers learned. Loke more at <term lang="la">Viride &#230;s</term>. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Chalcuan&#173;
thos</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De symp, li. 9</term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">F&#339;niculum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="fennel(n)">FEnell, whyche the Grecians calle <term lang="gk">&#x039C;&#x03AC;&#x03C1;&#x03B1;&#x03B8;&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03B7;</term>, doothe
heate in the firste degre, and dry in the second, whyche
eaten, or the seade drunke wyth parley water, fylleth
the pappes wyth milke. Whose iuyce also healeth the web
in the eye, and sharpeneth the sighte moste commodiously. 
The roote and seade of <term lang="la">Hippomarathrium</term> doe drye more then
<term lang="la">Foeniculum domestic<expan type="">um</expan></term>: and therfore seme to stay the belly, and
also are able to breake the stone, and to heale the iaundies. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gkt">Marathron</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Galenus de
sym. med. li. 7</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Dioscorides
li. 3</term>. </note>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">F&#339;num gr&#230;cum. </form>
<xpln lang="en">IS called in Greke <term lang="gk">&#x03C4;&#x1F25;&#x03BB;&#x03B9;&#x03C2;</term>. The seade wherof beinge well
sodden, discusseth and mollifyeth, chieflye wyth some
kynde of fatnesse. The decortion therof hath discussyue,
and moderate heatinge propertye, and therfore loseth, and
maturateth: and is wonte to mitigate manye paines. It is
whotte saythe Galen in the seconde degre, and drye in the
firste. Wherby it kyndleth, and augmenteth inflammaty&#173;
ons. For the whiche cause Lanfranke hath frendlye war&#173;
ned us, to eschewe the sayinge of Fenigreke, to whotte in&#173;
flamed members. Yet doth it by his deuiding power, heale 
suche inflammations, as are more hard than whotte. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gkt">Telis</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Dioscor. li. 6</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">de sym. li. 8. 
de com. med</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">sec<expan type="">un</expan>. gen. li. 7
de com. med</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">secun. locos
li. 4</term>. </note>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Formica. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">OF this tumore called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03BC;&#x03C5;&#x03C1;&#x03BC;&#x03BA;&#x03BA;&#x03B9;&#x03B1;</term>. Auicenna,
(of whome Lanfranke semeth to take his definition
therof,) saythe: it is a pustule, that commeth out, and
causeth a pure aposteme, and is abulatyue. Whiche per&#173;



</page>

<page no="41">
happes vlcerateth, or els wyll be resolued: hauynge a sub&#173;
stance, rounde lyke a lyttle warte, inflamed, and of coloure
somwhat citrine. But Galen (rekening it with Veruca, A&#173;
crochordine, and other lyke affectes of the <emend err="(">s</emend>kinne,) teacheth
how with a holowe quille to plucke it out. Affirming also,
that some men at Rome in his tyme, vsed to byte them a&#173;
way with their teeth. Neyther of the which wayes, can in
any wyse be mete for pure apostemes, eyther vlcerated, or 
apte to be resolued. 

Of the little Ante or pismire, called <term lang="gk">&#x03BC;&#x03CD;&#x03C1;&#x03BC;&#x03BA;&#x03BE;</term> doth this tu&#173;
mor take his name, both in Greke and Latin. Of whose
egges Lanfranke maketh an oyle, whose vse and vtilitie: 
there may you fynde. </xpln>

Primi tracta&#173;
tus. </term></note>

<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Myrmex</term>. </note>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Fraxinus. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="ash(n)" lexeme="tree(n)">IT should seme that Lanfranke, taketh the rynde of ash,
to be of vertue lyke vnto myrtils, in driyng vp the bru&#173;
sed places, kepyng the same from putrefaction: If it be
not added by some other, for I haue perused some copies,
whiche haue it not. But Ashe is vertuous againste the
swolne splene, and hath dyuerse properties, agreyng with
the Mirte tree, as in learned authors may farther reade 
both olde and newe wryters. The Ashe tree is called in 
Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03BC;&#x03B5;&#x03BB;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;</term>, and in Latin <term lang="la">Fraxinus</term>. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gkt">Melia</term>. </note>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="furfur(n)">Furfur. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">BRan hath qualities variable, after the kynde of grain
that it cometh of. Howe be it, <term lang="la">Furfur</term> is commonly ta&#173;
ken for bran of wheate: whiche is sayeth Galen, not
so whotte, but dryeth more then the meale, and dothe som&#173;
what scowre. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De symp.

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="galbanum(n)">Galbanum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">GAlbanum, or as some wryte Chalbanum, after the 
Greke, which is <term lang="gk">&#x03C7;&#x03B1;&#x03BB;&#x03B2;&#x03AC;&#x03BD;&#x03B7;</term>: is sayth Dioscorides,
the liquor of a tree, called <term lang="la">Metopi<expan type="">um</expan></term>, of a co<expan type="">un</expan>trey
in Syria, where it groweth. Which name doth 



</page>

<page no="42">
the Gum also sometymes obteine: For the vnguente <term lang="gkt">Me&#173;
topion</term> is so called, of the admirti<expan type="">on</expan> of Galbanum therwith. And 
he sayeth that it is adulterate with hulled beanes, resin, &#38;
ammoniake. Diosco. <term lang="la">lib. 1. cap. 6</term> &#38;  <term lang="la">lib. <damage>5</damage>. cap. 79. </term>
It ought to haue <term lang="la">Similitudinem Thuris, uel Ammoniaci</term>. It ta&#173;
keth his name <term lang="la">ab Albedine</term> (sayeth Antonius Musa Brasauolus:)
and therfore the whiter is the better, and contrariwyse,
the worse. Wherfore the Galbanum that we comm<expan type="">on</expan>ly haue,
may well be iudged adulterate, or else the dregges &#38;  drosse
of right Galbanum. It heateth after Galen, in the end of the
seconde degree, or in the beginning of the thirde, and dri&#173;
eth in the beginning of the second. Wherby it hath com&#173;
forting, mollifying, and degestiue powre, aswageth paine,
and is put in maturatiues: and in the prickes or hurtes of
sinewes, it helpeth not a lytle to mitigate payne, if the ner&#173;
ues be not bare, for it hath as haue all other mollificatiues
some putrefiying propertie. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gkt">Chalbane</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De symp. li. 8</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De com. med. 
secun. genera. 
lib. 3. 5. &#38;. 7. </term></note>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Gall&#230;</form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="oak-apple(n)"><term lang="la">GAlla</term> (saieth Dioscorides,) is a fructe of the Oke, wher&#173;
of the lesse is rugged, without holes, and as it weare
but half growne, &#38;  called <term lang="gk">&#x039F;&#x03BC;&#x03C6;&#x03B1;<damage>&#x03BA;</damage>&#x03AF;&#x03C4;&#x03B9;&#x03C2;</term>, and is the best:
The other (which is greter) is plain, light, &#38;  perced through
with holes. They are to vs well knowne: as well by the 
vse of perfect Galles, which we receiue from other regi<expan type="">on</expan>s. 
as also that we see th<expan type="">em</expan> yearly growing plentifully in Kent:
(which are ther of <expan type="">the</expan> co<expan type="">om</expan>m<expan type="">on</expan> sort called oke appuls). Though
they be never indurate. And whether ther be several kin&#173;
des of okes, wherof (as maister Turner reasoneth,) some
beare Galles, and some not. I can not saye: For with us,
fewe okes for the most yeres are without. 
Howe be it, of maister D. Cunningham I am through
his most friendly conference informed that he hath by ob&#173;
seruation founde on okes growing about Norwich, (wher
they are in certayne wooddes very plentifull,) three kindes
of fructes: the Acorne, the Oke appull, and the Galle: All



</page>

<page no="43">
the whiche I haue also by obseruati<expan type="">on</expan> founde in Kent. The
Acorne, and the Appull, growing on the sprigges or bran&#173;
<emend err="r">c</emend>hes, but the Galle, on the leaues of the tree. The Galle
(saieth he) waxeth harde, and the outward couering wryn&#173;
kled, and hath been by triall proued good (besyde the natiue
adstringent vertue,) to make inke, and blacke colours. Af&#173;
firming also, that the appul is harde in Julie, but hath gro&#173;
wing on it a softe substance, not unlike to masse, inwardly
more massy, &#38;  very stiptike: within the which also are those
thinges found, that are in the Galle. Wherof also some old
experte husbandmen of Kent, haue no smal opini<expan type="">on</expan> to iudge
by them the sequell of the yere. For they breaking them a&#173;
bout their time of withering, doe finde in them some liuing
thing. As sometime an Ante, wherof they iudge plenty of
grayne: sometime a whyte worme lyke a gentill, wherof
they prognostricate moreine in of beastes: And sometime a
spyder, wherof they presage the pestilence, or some other
lyke sicknes, to folowe among men. Whiche thing also the
learned haue not omitted to note, though not in the same 
order. For Matthiolus upon Dioscorides hath these wordes. 
<term lang="la">Illus sibi peculiare maiores Gali&#230; uendicauere, ut quot annis aut an&#173;
none fertilitatem, aut sterilitatem, aut pestilentem auram pr&#230;n<expan type="">um</expan>cient:
Nam si rumpantur integra (&#38; qu&#230; perforat&#230; non sunt,) aut museam,
aut araneum, aut uermiculum exponeunt. Si muscauolat futuri belli, si
repit uermiculus, <damage>annon&#230; penuria</damage>: si curritaraneus, pestitentium mor&#173;
borum pr&#230;sagium est. Neque culquam mirum uideatur, quod ex omni&#173;
bus Gallis ista oriantur animalculatetenim ego huius rei s&#230;pius feci pe&#173;
riculum, nullamque unquam suam c<expan type="">on</expan>sequutus prius n<expan type="">on</expan> perforatam, qu&#230;
ex tribus animalibus unum intra se non continerat. Nam foramine con&#173;
specto facile iudicium sumi potest, iam animal exi<emend err="i"></emend>sse. Dicamus igitur li&#173;
cet, qu&#230;rcum &#38; fructum, &#38;  animal gignere. Cuius rei haud ignari ue&#173;
teres illi patres, non sine causa quercum dixer<expan type="">un</expan>t loui summo esse dicat<expan type="">am</expan></term>. 
Which may in effecte thus be Englished. This thynge
haue the greatter Galles chaleged peculiar to the selues
that they can yerely forchewe, eyther plentye or scarcitie
of victualle, or a pestilentialle ayre; for if they be broken in
sondre, namely those that are not already through pearced. 



</page>

<page no="44">
They conteine either a flie, a spyder, or a worme. If a flye
be fo<expan type="">un</expan>de, it is a prognostication of warre to folow: if a cre&#173;
pyng worme, of the scarcitie of victuall: but if a runnynge
spyder, of pestilente sicknesses. Neyther let any man mer&#173;
ueile that out of Galles those little liuyng thynges should
apeare: for this thyng haue I often proued, neither I
eyer fynde any, (being not before pearced <expan type="">with</expan>holes,) which
conteyned not within it selfe one of the fornamed ani&#173;
malles: for the hole beinge sene, it is easy to iudge that they
are nowe gone forth. Wherfore we may saye, that the oke
bryngeth forth frute, and lyuing thynges. Of the whiche,
the olde fathers not being ignorant, haue sayde (not with&#173;
out cause) that the oke was dedicated to the hyghe Ju&#173;
piter. 

<term lang="la">Galla</term> (sayeth Galen) which is called <term lang="la">Omphacitis</term>, is a sower 
medicine and of substance for the most part earthy &#38;  colde:
wherby it drieth and repercusseth fluxions, constreyneth &#38;
fasteneth lose and feble partes. It coleth in the seconde de&#173;
gree, and drieth in the thyrde. The other (which is great, 
yellow, and softe) drieth also, but that so muche the lesse as
it hath lesse sower or sharpe qualities. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">In primum. 
Dioscoridis. 
lib. Cap. Ve&#173;
ro. 124.</term> <term lang="gk">&#x03BA;&#x03B7;&#x03B9;&#x03C2;</term>
. i. Galla. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De simplici<expan type="">um</expan>
facultatibus
lib. 7. </term></note>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Garyophilli</form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="clove(n)"><term lang="la">GAryophylli</term>, (others write <term lang="la">Caryophylli</term>,) in Greke <term lang="gk">&#x0393;&#x03B1;&#x03C1;&#x03C5;&#x03BF;&#173;
&#x03C6;&#x03CD;&#x03BB;&#x03BB;&#x03BF;&#x03B9;, &#x03BA;&#x03B1;&#x03B9; &#x03C7;&#x03B1;&#x03C1;&#x03C5;&#x03BF;&#x03C6;&#x03CD;&#x03BB;&#x03BB;&#x03BF;&#x03B9;</term>: so called, sayth Paulus Aegi&#173;

</page>

<page no="45">
hath neither Dioscorides nor Galen: but Plinie maketh m<expan type="">en</expan>&#173;
tion therof, saiyng. There is a frute in India called <term lang="la">Garyophil

reddishe vpon blacke, headed like a nayle, with foure den&#173;
ticles spredde lyke a starre: within the whiche lyeth bow&#173;
lyng out a litle rounde button. The tree is of heyght three
cubites, hauing litle thick leaues, &#38;  a broade woody roote. 
Cloues are whotte and drie in the thirde degree, very
aromatike, and haue almoste the vertue of pepper, but
dooe hurt the stomache lesse, &#38;  are good against poyson. In 
lynimentes, they haue vertue to consume, dissolue, comfort
sinewes, and asswage payne, specially commyng of colde
matter. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Gentiana. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="gentian(n)">GEntian is an herbe: called of the Grecians <term lang="gk">&#x03B3;&#x03B5;&#x03BD;&#x03C4;&#x03B9;&#x03B1;&#x03BD;&#x1F74;</term>,

extenuate, and scoure away. Wherby it helpeth obstructi&#173;
ons, and is drunke <term lang="la">In Vino, cum ruta &#38;  pipere</term> againste the
daunger of venemous woundes: and is somtymes put into

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Glandul&#230;. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="glandule(n)"><term lang="la">GAlenus in lib. 13. &#38;  . 14. De methodo medendi</term>, defineth Glan&#173;
dules &#38;  their natures double, saiying. Some Glandu&#173;
les there are, placed in the myddes of the vesselles,
and of their branches, to defende their strengthe, whose vse



</page>

<page no="46">
is not great. Other there are (whose vse is greater,) made 
to receiue, and make spitle, milke, seade, &#38;c. (as thou maiest
farther see in myne anatomy, neither are they otherwyse
cured, when they are affected <term lang="la">Cum scirrho</term>, then other partes
of the body: But the other require the parte, with also the 
corrupti<expan type="">on</expan> to be taken vtterly away. They are partes most
apte to receiue fluxions, as well for their weaknes and lo&#173;
senes of substance, as also because among all other partes,
thei haue (except the alteratiue) almost no natural facultie:
and therfor are most swiftly taken, of the tumores <term lang="la">Bubones</term>. 

<term lang="la">Glandul&#230;</term> in Lanfranke, are to be vnderstanded of the first
sorte of Glandules conteined in the vessels, being affected
of <term lang="gkt">Phygethlon</term>: but chiefly after his minde, of humores phleg&#173;
matike. For if by mel<expan type="">an</expan>choly they become scirrhous, he cal&#173;
leth them <term lang="la">Scophulas</term>, but Galen nameth them <term lang="la">Strumas</term>, and
in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03C7;&#x03BF;&#x03B9;&#x03C1;&#x03AC;&#x03B4;&#x03B5;&#x03C2;</term>. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Scrophula</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Struma</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gkt">Chocras</term>. </note>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Glycyrrhiza. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="liquorice(n)"><term lang="gk">&#x0393;&#x03BB;&#x03C5;&#x03C5;&#x03CD;&#x03C1;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;&#x03B6;&#x03B1;</term>, which is <term lang="la">Radix dulcis</term>, and we call it liquo&#173;
rice, After the Apothecaries who call it <term lang="la">Liqueriti<expan type="">am</expan></term>, is indue&#173;
ed with a certeine warming propertie, and yet more colde
then man his nature: and muste needes also be moderatlye
moyste, as it is moderatly sweete. And therfore to staye
thyrste it is moste mete, and smotheth or softeneth the
roughnes or ruggednes, as well of the bladder, as of the 
sharpe artery. It is conuenient for the heate of the breste,
stomache, and liuer: And drunke <term lang="la">Cum passo</term>, healeth the pei&#173;
nes of the renes. The pouder of the roote also dried, hea&#173;
leth sayeth Dioscorides, the webbe of the eye. </xpln>
<note type="m">Galenus <term lang="la">De 
simp. med. li. 6</term></note>
<note type="m">Diosc. <term lang="la">lib. 3. </term></note>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Grana paradisi. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="cardamom(n)"><term lang="la">GRana paradisi</term> are commonly taken for <term lang="la">Cardamomo</term>, and
are so vsually solde of the Apothecaries. Not with&#173;
standynge others am<expan type="">on</expan>g the learned, thinke the com&#173;



</page>

<page no="47">
mon lyttle graynes, to be <term lang="la">Grana paradisi</term>. 
It is a spice of whotte &#38;  drye nature, &#38;  odoriferous of smel. 
Whiche I could neuer fynde so perfectly descrybed, of any
man, as of Rychard Eden, where he wryteth of the twoo
first viages of our Englyshemen to Guinea, saiynge that
our men might haue lade their shippes with those graines,
by the ryuer Sesto. These Graynes (sayeth he,) growe and
come forth, in a fruyte muche lyke a fygge: wiche (when
they are gathered,) are as redde as bloude. They growe
not paste a foote and a halfe, or twoo foote from the gro&#173;
unde: And as the fygge hath in it many graynes, so hathe
this fruyte. Through the whiche the inhabitantes there
make holes, wherin they put strynges, and so hange them
to drye, that the sayde Graynes may be the more easely
taken out: as all seades and graynes, may most aptlye be
taken from theyr coddes vessicles or eares, when they be
very drye. These last haue been taken for <term lang="la">Cardamomo minori</term>,
and the former (sold commonly for <term lang="la">Granis paradisi</term>,) for <term lang="la">Car&#173;
damomo maiori</term>. And this is the opinion of the later Phisiti&#173;
ens: for Brasauolus noteth three sortes of seedes, which haue
been of olde among the Apothecaries under this name 
<term lang="la">Cardamomum</term>. The one is the trianguled grayne, whiche
they sell as is aboue sayde, both for <term lang="la">Cardamomo</term>, and <term lang="la">Grano pa&#173;
radisi</term>. The seconde he calleth <term lang="la">Meligettan</term>. The thyrde (sayeth
he) is <term lang="la">Cardamomum</term> of the auncientes, is 
to vs vtterly vnknowne. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Cardamom<expan type="">um</expan></term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Meligetta</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Nigella Ci&#173;
trina</term>. </note>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Gummi Arabicum</form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="acacia gum(n)"><term lang="la">GVmmi Arabicum</term> founde (and also so called) of the Arabi&#173;
ans, because it is there moste plentifull, and also <term lang="la">Babi&#173;
lonicum</term> &#38;  <term lang="la">Sarasenicum</term>, vp<expan type="">on</expan> lyke reason, is <expan type="">the</expan> teares of the
thorny tree called Acacia &#38;  <term lang="la">Spina Aegyptia</term>, <emend err="">(</emend>described befor in <expan type="">the</expan> 



</page>

<page no="48">
letter A) and therfore may it be called <term lang="la">Gummi Acaci&#230;</term>, or <term lang="la">Gum&#173;
mi Spin&#230; Aegyptia</term>, Though the Gumme that is vnder that
name commonly solde, be the gumme sumetime of the bit&#173;
ter almonde tree, whiche is nexte of goodnes to the beste,
sometyme of the Cherry or prune trees, and sometyme of
the Elme: and this is the cause why it is fo<expan type="">un</expan>de of so diuers
colours. It is (as the tree wherof it c<expan type="">om</expan>meth) of cooling and 
drying facultie, without sharpenes of byting: And therfore
a commodious lenitiue medicine, for the grefes and peines 
of the eyes. </xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="haematite(n)">H&#230;matites. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="bloodstone(n)"><term lang="gk">&#38;#x0303B1`;&#x03B9;&#x03BC;&#x03B1;&#x03C4;&#x03AF;&#x03BA;&#x03C2;</term>, <term lang="la">id est apis sanguinaria</term>, in English the bloud stone. 
So called sayeth Galen, of the lykenes in coloure that it 
hath with bloude: beyng of so muche coldnesse, as it hathe
adstriction. Wherfore it is founde a medicine profitable to
the eyes: and may be vsed alone, to the roughnes of the 
eye browes, if it come of inflammati<expan type="">on</expan>. And thus muche of
his faculties efficient, whiche are by reason knowne. For 
the faculties that it hath after the propertie of the whole 
substance, are neyther knowne by reason, nor methode,
but onely by experience. Wherby it stayeth the fluxe of
bloude, the wounde beyng onely touched therwith. It is
conuenient for all vlcers, and the poulder therof represseth 
excrescentes. </xpln>
<note type="m">Gal. <term lang="la">De symp. 
lib. 9. </term></note>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Hedera. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="ivy(n)">IVye (of the Grecians <term lang="gk">&#x039A;&#x03B9;&#x03C3;&#x03C3;&#x03CC;&#x03C2;</term>,) is after Dioscorides of . iii. 
kindes, namely <term lang="la">Candida</term> (which Plinie calleth <term lang="la">foemina</term>,) <term lang="la">Ni&#173;
gra</term>, (of Plinie <term lang="la">mas</term>,) and <term lang="la">Helix</term>. Wherof the white reioy&#173;



</page>

<page no="49">
ceth in watery places, the blacke to growe vpon walles or
trees, and <term lang="la">Helix</term> barenly crepeth on the grounde. 
It consisteth (by the sentence of Galen) of contrary qua&#173;
lities: as of a certeyne adstryngente substance, whiche is
colde and earthy, hauing also some sharpnes: whiche to bee
whotte euen the taste proueth. And farther if it be grene,
it obteineth a warme and watery substance. 
The grene leaues therfore boyled in wyne, doe glewe
together and brynge to healynge greate and maligne vl&#173;
cers, and vlcers made with fyre: though by experience, we
see them also attractiue, and apte to kepe long open, suche
sores as we would not haue healed. And being boyled with 
vynegar, they helpe the sycke of the splene. The tares or
gumme therof kylleth nittes, and maketh balde, and is so
whotte, that it priuily burneth. Whiche yet I haue found,
to be a meruaylous desiccatiue, and with oyle to asswage
peyne. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gkt">Cissos</term>,</note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">lib. 2. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De symp. 
lib. 7. </term></note>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Hermodactylus. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="Mercury's finger(n)">THough that kynde of <term lang="gk">&#x0395;&#x03C6;&#x03BA;&#x03BC;&#x03BC;&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03BC;</term>, (so called because it
kylleth in one daye hym that eateth the roote ther&#173;
of) that is also called <term lang="gk">&#x039A;&#x03BF;&#x03BB;&#x03C7;&#x03B9;&#x03B7;&#x1F78;&#x03B7;</term>, (of the contrey <term lang="la">Colchis</term>,
where it did plentifully growe) haue emong many learned
men, borne the name of <term lang="la">Hermodactylus</term>, and may be one of 
the worste or nocyue kyndes of the same, accordyng to the
descripti<expan type="">on</expan>s of Auicenna, Serapio, Mesues, &#38;  other Arabi<expan type="">an</expan>s: yet
it is not the <term lang="la">Hermodactylus</term> that is solde of the Apothecaries. 
First because the roote of this is rounde on al partes, black
or reddyshe, and when it is drye rugged, or wrynkled:
whose stalke goeth forth at a rifte or creaste, in the vpper
or smallest ende. That of the Apothecaries is rounde on the 
one syde, and flatte on the other: whyte, and smothe, and
without rifte: but hath on the flatte syde, a certayne sha&#173;
lowe dinte, as it weare a cloue cleauynge to the syde of a 
stalke. Wherin it very well agreeth, with <term lang="la">Hermodactylo</term>,



</page>

<page no="50">
as Paulus Aegineta descrybeth it. The same also doth Cordus
inferre, because <term lang="la">Colchicum</term> strangleth, whiche <term lang="la">Hermodactyli</term>
doe not. Who affirmeth <term lang="la">Hermodactylus</term> to growe in mowne 
medowes, and sonny places. 

Mesues makyng twoo kyndes of <term lang="la">Hermodactili</term>, sayeth. It is 
the roote of an herbe that groweth on the hylles, wherof
the one is of a fynger length, the other rounde: and this is
eyther whyte, redde, or blacke. Amonge the whiche, that
whiche is white, bothe within and without, is the beste: as 
the redde and the blacke are euell. Whiche (sayeth Serapio),
doe strangle, and therfore may one of these be that kynde
of <term lang="la">Colchicum</term>, or wylde saffron, that groweth in certeyne
gardyns in Englande. 
But who so woulde note more exactly, Mesues order of
deuision of <term lang="la">Hermodactilus</term>, may beholde it thus gathered.

1 Very whyte both

harde and softe. 
All others howe muche so&#173;

the beste. 
2 Redde. 
3 Blacke. 
Bothe the whiche are euell. 



</page>

<page no="51">

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="herpes(n)">Herpes. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="gk">&#x0395;&#x03C1;&#x03C0;&#x03B7;&#x03C2;</term>, is one of the tumores against nature, that are re&#173;
ferred to Erysipelas, and an affecte of exulceration of the
skynne, springing, (by the testimony of Galen) of the same
cause: as of bilious excrementes, setlyng in some place. Sa&#173;
uing that the humore therof, is thinner and subtiler then 
of Erysipelas, wherin also they differ. It is sayeth he, of two
kindes. For when the subst<expan type="">an</expan>ce is grosse, it perceth through
the skynne with his vlcerations, euen to the fleshe, and is 
called <term lang="la">Herpes exedens</term>: whiche rightly in qualitie resembleth
the serpente, (wherof it hath his name) in crepyng, and de&#173;
uouring the partes adiacent, and is of the kynde of Erysipe&#173;
las vlcerate. But when it is ingendred of choler not so
whotte and sharpe, or (as to some it semeth not all without 
reason) myngled with some phlegme: it maketh no vlcer,
but litle pustules like the graines of <term lang="la">Milium</term>, and therfore is
called Herpes. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De methodo
medendi. lib. 1. 
2. &#38;. 4. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De tumorib. 
pr&#230;ter natur<expan type="">am</expan></term></note>

dens</term>. </note>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Hordeum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="barley(n)" lexeme="summer barley(n)" lexeme="big barley(n)" lexeme="winter barley(n)" lexeme="naked barley(n)" lexeme="Dutch barley(n)" lexeme="polenta(n)">BArley is called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x039A;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;&#x03B8;&#x03B7;</term>, and therof are there
dyuerse kyndes. As <term lang="la">Hordeum distichum</term>, wiche is oure
sommer barley. <term lang="la">Hordeum tetrastichon</term>, bigge barley. <term lang="la">Hor&#173;
deum polystichum</term>, of some wynter barley. <term lang="la">Hordeum Hexastich<expan type="">um</expan></term>, 
whiche D. Turner calleth duche barley. <term lang="gk">&#x0393;&#x03C5;&#x03BC;&#x03BD;&#x03CC;&#x03BA;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;&#x03B8;&#x03BF;&#x03B7;</term>. i. <term lang="la">Hor&#173;
deum nudum, uel corticis expers</term>, naked barlye, &#38;c. Whiche far&#173;
ther to dilate, here is neyther place nor nede. 
Barley coleth and drieth (after Galen) in the first degree,
wherin it differeth from wheate, and hath also a little ab&#173;
stersion, and in meates excelleth beanes: because it is not
wyndy. Barley bread nourysheth lesse then wheate, but
prouoketh the belly more: and is vnto men of smal or no
exercyse, of large nuryshment. So is also Polenta, which is
of nature more drye: although <term lang="la">Ptisana</term>, doe naturally moyst. 
And in <term lang="la">Libro de attenuante uictus ratione</term>, he saieth: that emong
all other breades, barley is vnhurtefull to a sclender diete. 
Barley meale outwardly resolueth, mollifieth, and matu&#173;
rateth, chiefly whotte affectes, and cholericke tumores. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gkt">Crithe. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gkt">Gymnocrith<expan type="">on</expan>. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De alimentis. 
lib. 1. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De sym. medi. 
fac. lib. 7. </term></note>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="hyoscyamus(n)">Hyosciamus. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="henbane(n)">HEenbane is called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03A5;&#x03BF;&#x03C3;&#x03BA;&#x03CD;&#x03B1;&#x03BC;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>, in Latin
<term lang="la">Hiosciamus, Apollinaris, Faba suilla, Altercum</term>: of Apuleus
<term lang="la">Symphoniaca</term>: of others also <term lang="la">Fabulum</term>, and <term lang="la">Eabulonia</term>: and of
some <term lang="la">Cassilago</term>, or <term lang="la">Caniculata</term>: of the Apothecaries <term lang="la">Iusquiamus</term>. 
Of Henbane there are three kyndes: the blacke, the ye&#173;
lowe, and the whyte. The blacke is our common kynde:
Whose seade causeth madnes, and is not allowed. The ye&#173;
lowe groweth in my garden, the seade wherof was geuen
me, of maister Roger Lee Doctor of phisicke: whiche is bet&#173;
ter then the blacke, but yet to be eschued as hurtfull. 
That whose flower and seade is whyte, I neuer sawe as
yet. It is only allowed holsome, bey<emend err="m">n</emend>g colde in the thyrde



</page>

degree, and is a narcotike, or stupefactiue medicine: asswa&#173;

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="hyposarca(n)">Hyposarca. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="gk">&#x0391;&#x03BD;&#x03B1;&#x03C3;&#x03AC;&#x03C1;&#x03B1;&#x03BA;</term>, called also <term lang="la">Sarcites</term>, Hyposarca, and <term lang="la">Leucophleg&#173;
matias</term>, is that kynde of <term lang="la">Hydrops</term>, whiche (spreadyng it

nites</term>,) possesseth the whole habite of the body, with muche
moisture: though the other two dooe yet sende affectes, to
other partes then they possesse, whiche is the belye. As to
the great arteries, <term lang="la">Ascytes</term>, colyng, stretching and aggraua&#173;
ting them, and <term lang="la">Tympanites</term>, filling th<expan type="">em</expan> with wynde. Whose
coates <term lang="la">Anasarca</term> dothe lose &#38;  mollifie. The Hydropsy cometh
diuerse wayes. As by the obstruction and refrigeration of
the lyuer, or by <term lang="la">Pylorus</term> and <term lang="la">Mesente&#173;
rium</term>, or of some one of the smalle guttes: of the immoderate
fluxe of the <term lang="la">H&#230;morrhoides</term>, or of <term lang="la">Menses</term>, and of the suppression
of the same: and sometime of the vntymly drynking of cold
water, coolyng the lyuer: Without some vnnaturall affec&#173;
tion wherof, there can spryng no Hydropsie, sayeth Galen. 
<term lang="la">De locis affectis. lib. 5. cap. 6. </term></xpln>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Icteritia. </form>

</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Infusio. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">Commyng of <term lang="la">Infundo</term>, to powre in, or hylle on, signifieth



</page>

<page no="55">
stepyng or laiyng in fuse: and sometyme the liquor, wherin
thynges are or haue ben infused. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Intensio. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="la">Intensio</term> is, a stretching, streigning, or bending. But here 
it is a meanynge, wyl, intent, or diligence: &#38;  commeth of <term lang="la">In&#173;
tendo</term>, signifiyng as well to indeuoure, to looke diligently, or 
to intende: as to stretche, streygne, bende, or inforce. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Intubum. </form>
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="cichorium(n)">Or <term lang="la">Intybum</term> is sufficiently spoken of at <term lang="la">Cichorium</term>. </xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Iposarca. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="hyposarca(n)">Also at Hyposarca. </xpln>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="iris(n)">Iris. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="fleur-de-lis(n)">OF the Greci<expan type="">an</expan>s likewise <term lang="gk">&#x0399;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;&#x03C2;</term>, which name <expan type="">the</expan> Apothe&#173;
caries also reteyne, Is to vs commonly knowne, and
called in Englysh blew flowre deluce. whose flowre
(though blew do beare therin <expan type="">the</expan> greatest vewe) is of many
colours, lyke the raynbowe: wherof sayeth Dioscorides, it 
taketh his name. Whose roote is sharpe, and whotte and
drye after Mesus in the thyrde degree. 

It scoureth concocteth, resolueth, thinneth, openeth, and
taketh away the obstructions of the lyuer, splene, &#38;  other
vicious partes: Healing or amendyng, the affectes caused
of them: as hydropsies, tumores, hardnes, payne, and suche
lyke. It aswageth paynes, and purgeth grosse phlegme &#38;
choler mixed therwith, and also whayey waters, and that
gentilly. It helpeth muche with sweete wyne the olde
coughe, caused of grosse and toughe humores, and also
the difficultie of breathynge sprunge therof. Beynge
boyled and outwardly applyed, it mollyfyeth, and dige&#173;
stethe scrofules, olde harnesses of the Ioyntes and 



</page>

<page no="56">
sinewes, &#38;c. It purgeth with hony, drye vlcers, and filleth 
them: so doth it also fistules, and hollowe vlcers. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Lib. 2. de
symp. medica. 
purgant. ca. 8. </term></note>
</wordentry>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="L">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">
Lacertus. </form>
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="la">Lacertus</term>, or <term lang="la">Lacerta</term>, is a worme or litle beaste, hauynge
foure feete, and formed in body like our este or ewght
(which therfore some iudge a kynde of the same,) of
coloure in germany ashye or blacke, and of a spanne or of a
foote lengthe at the moste. In Italy longer, of coloure
grene, &#38;  venemous, but yet moste frendly to man, (whose
face he delyghteth to beholde,) enemy to serpentes: and for
man his cause, (whose spittle he wyll lycke when he fyn&#173;
deth hym sleaping, vsing it as a defence) he fighteth against
serpentes, and therfore obteyneth worthilye the name
<term lang="gk">&#x039F;&#x03C6;&#x03B9;&#x03CC;&#x03BC;&#x03B1;&#x03C7;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>. <term lang="la">Almanzoar</term>, describinge the same medicine for
the eyes, that Lanfranke dothe, hath <term lang="la">Stellio</term>, because it hath spottes on the skynne
lyke to starres. 

The dunge of the Lizarde, is a medicine proffitable for
the eyes, as to sharpen the syght, &#38;  to take awaye the itche
and the webbe in the same. The head of the Lizarde,
(as sayeth Dioscorides) brused and layde to, draweth out all
prickes or stynges fixed in the flesh, &#38;  taketh away spottes,
and the wartes called <term lang="la">Formicas</term>, and <term lang="la">Acrochordonas</term>. The ly&#173;
uer of the same put into holowe teethe, taketh away their
peynes. The Lizarde broken, and layde to the stroke of a
Scorpion, easeth the same. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Ophiomachos</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Lib. 1. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">&#x0391;&#x03BA;&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03C7;&#x03BF;&#x03C1;&#173;
&#x03B4;&#x03C9;&#x03BC;</term>. i. <term lang="la">Ve&#173;
ruca pensilis. </term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Lac muliebre. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="milk(n)">MYlke called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03B3;&#x03AC;&#x03BB;&#x03B1;</term>, (whose substance primi&#173;
tiue is bloude:) is of temperament, meane betweene
bloud and phlegme, consisting of three substances dyuerse,



</page>

<page no="57">
and in effectes contrary. Namely whayey, whiche is colde
and moiste, and loseth the bely: Curdie, whiche is tough,
and byndeth the same: and fatty, wherof butter is made,
and vsed (sayeth Galen, <term lang="la">Lib. 3. De alimentis</term>,) in colde contreis
for oyle. Of qualities as variable as the bodies ar dyuers,
wherin it is made: As cowe mylke is most grosse and fatte,
(whiche healeth the lang="it"<term>Dysenteria</term>.) Camels mylke leane and
moste liquyde. But of all the thynnest and most whayey is
asses mylke: And therfore to drye affectes is moste conue&#173;
nient: The meane betwene these holdeth goates mylke: 
whiche taken without hony is perylous, because if it con&#173;
geyle in the stomache it suffocateth. Galenus <term lang="la">De Euchymia, &#38;  
Cacochymia</term>) Thicker then whiche is shepes mylke, &#38;c. 

Mylke generally nurysheth, maketh good iuyce, &#38;  pur&#173;
geth the bely moderatly, is profitable for the breste, and
healeth the vlcers of the lunges: but to the head that is not
very fyrme it is vnprofitable, and the vse therof to the
teethe very hurtfull. But of all other womans mylke, (as
of the same nature) is to vs most famyliar, and to phthisick
persons an excellent medicine: conuenient also for sharpe
and bytyng fluxions of the eyes, and inflammations of the
same, and to the vlcers of the panicle <term lang="la">Cornea</term>. It mitigateth
the inflammations of the mouthe, throte, and <term lang="la">Vuula</term>, and ea&#173;
seth the squincie: and is geuen as a helpe to them that haue
taken corrodyng venyms. 

The best mylke is very whyte, and without sensible
temperament: that is hauing no odor or little, and the same
pleasant: but hauing in taste some swetenes, lyke holsome
bloude. As that which is in any wyse thervnto contrari, is 
alwayes euell. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Gala</term>. </note>
<note type="m">Galenus <term lang="la">De 
sanitate tu<expan type="">en</expan>da
lib. 1. &#38;c. 5. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De marasmo. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De methodo
medendi. lib. 7</term></note> 
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De sym. med. 
lib. 4. &#38;. 10. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De compo. se&#173;
cun loca. lib. 4</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De Theriaca
ad pisonem</term>. </note>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Lactuca. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="lettice(n)" lexeme="endive(n)" lexeme="cabbage(n)"><term lang="gk">&#x0398;&#x03C1;&#x03AF;&#x03B4;&#x03B1;&#x03BE;</term> is of kynde firste double, as <term lang="la">Lactuca satiuaeme="lettice(n)"</term>, or <term lang="la">Hor&#173;

Cabbeginge. 

Letuce cooleth and moisteth by the testimony of Galen,
according to the temperament, of fountaine water, and
therfore is commodious to whotte inflammati<expan type="">on</expan>s, and <term>Ery&#173;
sipelata</term> of the worste kyndes. It cooleth the stomache, and
stayeth thyrste: and therfore sayeth Galen, that he vsed it
in his youth to coole with, When choler infested his sto&#173;
mache, And in age, as a helpe against wakfulnes: For the
seade prouoketh sleape, and stayeth 
<term lang="la">Genitur&#230; profluuium</term>. The
iuice therof neyther resisteth concoction, nor byndeth the 
belly: But the large drynkyng therof is very dangerous. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="laudanum(n)">Laudanum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="labdanum(n)"><term lang="en">LApdanum</term> as we barbarously call it, is called of
Dioscorides in Greke <term lang="gk">&#x03BB;&#x03AC;&#x03B4;&#x03B1;&#x03BD;&#x03BF;&#x03B7;</term>, of Galen <term lang="gk">&#x039A;&#x03B9;&#x03C3;&#x03B8;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>, and
in Latin <term lang="la">Ladanum</term>: but more commonly of the Apo&#173;
thecaries <term lang="en">Laudanum</term>. Galen affirmeth <term lang="la">Ladanum</term> to be whotte
beyonde the firste degree, of a subtyle substance: and hathe
vertue to mollifie, resolue moderatly, to digeste, and con&#173;
cocte harde thynges, to amende the deformitie of scarres,
to disolue the duration of the matrixe, and to amende other
vices therof. It stayeth the faulyng of heare, but yet is not
able to heale <term>Alopecias</term>, or <term>Opthalmia</term>, which require stronger
medicines. Being drunke with wyne, it stoppeth the belly 
and prouoketh vryne. 

It is the Gumme of a certeyne shrubbe or bushe, called
<term lang="en">Cistus</term>, or <term lang="gk">&#x03BB;&#x1F27;&#x03B4;&#x03BF;&#x03B7;</term>, whiche maister Turner called therfore <term lang="la">Ci&#173;
stus ladanifera</term>: and is gathered by drawynge ropes, or suche
like thyngs, through the bushes: or els as saith Galen from
the beardes of gotes, which they haue gathered by feding
among the same. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Lad<damage>a</damage>non</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Cisthos</term>. </note>

<page no="59">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Laurus. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="laurel(n)" lexeme="bay(n)" lexeme="tree(n)">THe Laurell or Bay tree, is called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x0394;&#x03AC;&#x03C6;&#x03BD;&#x03B7;</term>,
and in Latin <term lang="la">Laurus</term>. The leaues wherof doe vehe&#173;
mently heate &#38;  drye, but the beries more vehemently: and
therfore doe helpe the diseases of the lunges, caused of colde
and reumatyke matter, paynes of the eares also, and deaf&#173;
nes. The rynde of the roote, is neyther so sharpe nor so
whotte, but more bitter, and hath also some adstriction: and
therfore is with swete wyne profitable to the breste, and
breaketh the stone. The leaues doe sensibly bynde, offende
the stomache, and prouoke vomite. Brused and aplyed,
they help the stingyng of waspes or bees: And <term lang="la">Cum polenta
&#38; pane</term>, asswage inflammations. 

Other kyndes of this there are, as <term lang="la">Nerium</term>, <term lang="la">Daphnoides</term>, whiche we call laurialle: but because
they are for the more parte rare vnto vs, and not seruyng
to any thyng wrytten in this boke: I here omitte to speake
any more of them. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Daphne</term>. </note>
<note type="m">Dioscor. <term lang="la">lib. 1. </term></note>
<note type="m">Galenus <term lang="la">de symp. lib. 6. </term></note>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Lenticula. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="lentil(n)" lexeme="pulse(n)"><term lang="gk">&#x03A6;&#x03B1;&#x03BA;&#x03CC;&#x03C2; &#x1F22; &#x03A6;&#x03B1;&#x03BA;&#x1F74;</term>, Latine, <term lang="la">Lens, &#38;  Lenticula</term>, and in Englyshe
Lentilles, Is a kynde of pulse. whose grayne sayeth Fuch&#173;
sius, is of all other the leaste: of temperamente meane be&#173;
twene whotte and colde, but drie in the seconde degree. Of
substance grosse, earthly, austere, and myghtly adstring<expan type="">en</expan>t. 
Wherfore both their substance and seconde decoction, sup&#173;
presseth the fluxe of the belly, and comforteth the stomache:
though <expan type="">the</expan> iuice yet be losing, &#38;  the first decoction doe purge
the same. It is of lytle iuice, and of nurishment grosse and
melancholyke, to melancholyke persones dangerous,
and bredeth on them <term lang="la">Elephantiasim</term>, &#38;  <term lang="la">Cancrum</term>, and dulleth
the syghte. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="lepra(n)">Lepra. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="la">LEpra</term> sayeth Auicen, is as it weare a <term lang="la">Cancer commuitis toto
corpori</term>, that is a Cancer common to al the body: wher&#173;
in he sheweth him him selfe to meane, that lothly af&#173;
fecte, whiche the Grecians call <term lang="gk">&#x0395;&#x03BB;&#x03AD;&#x03C6;&#x03B1;&#x03BD;&#x03C4;&#x03B1; &#x1F94; &#x0395;&#x03BB;&#x03B5;&#x03C6;&#x03B1;&#x03BD;&#x03C4;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;&#x03C3;&#x03B9;&#x03B7;</term>, 
and some <term lang="gk">&#x039B;&#x03B5;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;&#x03C4;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;&#x03C3;&#x03B9;&#x03B7;</term>, of the greatnes of the disease, ma&#173;
kyng the skinne lose, rugged, and of diuerse colours lyke
the skynne of the Elephante: and is of Galen thus descri&#173;
bed. <term lang="gk">Elephas</term> sayeth he, is a melancholicke affecte, hauing his
beginnyng of melancholicke bloud: but in processe of tyme,
blacke choler superaboundeth that bloud in quantitie, and 
then doth the body stynke, and semeth filthie, deformed,
and the fleshe blacke, and sometymes is exulcerate. Of the 
same kynde also is <term lang="la">Lepra</term>, and commeth of a lyke
cause, but is more common to the skynne, &#38;  is not alwayes
so vniuersall: Whiche some (of the lykenes and communi&#173;
tie of the one with the other) haue called <term lang="gk">&#x03A5;&#x03CE;&#x03C1;&#x03B1;&#x03B7;</term>, <term lang="la">id est scabiem</term>,
but not without confusion. Guido de Cauliaco, and others of
that age, haue rekoned foure kyndes of <term lang="la">Lepra</term>, for the which 
resorte to Serpigo. </xpln>

<form lang="la">Leucophlegmatia. </form>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Lilium. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="lily(n)"><term lang="gk">&#x039A;&#x03A1;&#x03AF;&#x03BD;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan>, &#x03BA;&#x03C1;&#x03AF;&#x03BD;&#x03BF;&#x03B7;, &#x03B7;&#x03B1;&#x03B7;&#x03BB;&#x03AD;&#x03B9;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;&#x03BF;&#x03B7;</term>, Latine <term lang="la">Lilium, atque Rosa iunonis</term>,
wherof Dioscorides, <term lang="la">Lib. 3.</term> mencioned two kyndes: as 
<term lang="en">Albion</term>, and <term lang="la">Croceum</term>. The leaues &#38;  rootes of the white
lilyes (for that chiefly is vsed in medicine,) doe drye, digeste,
and moderatly scoure away: and therfore <term lang="la">Cum olco rosato</term> are



</page>

<page no="61">
good for burnynges of fyre. The floures therof consiste of
a mixed temperament: as of a substance partly subtil, part&#173;
ly earthy, partly watery, and that temperate. Wherof it
foloweth the oyle made of them, to bee of digestinge and
mollifying facultie, without byting: and therfore is moste
conuenient, for the hardnesses of mother, inflammations
of the coddes, and apostemes. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="linum(n)">Linum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="flax(n)" lexeme="fenugreek(n)">FLaxe, whiche is named in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03BB;&#x03AF;&#x03BD;&#x03B7;</term>, (as well as the
seade therof called <term lang="gk">&#x03BB;&#x03B9;&#x03BD;&#x03CC;&#x03C3;&#x03C0;&#x03B5;&#x03C1;&#x03BC;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, <term lang="la">id est semen lini</term>,) is of tem<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
perament whotte in the firste degree, and meane be&#173;
tweene drye and moyste, obteininge the same faculties
that Fenigreke hath, and is in meate flatuous: yet doth it
scatter and deuide, and with boyled hony mollyfieth bothe
inwardly and outwardly harde inflammations: and be&#173;
yng rawe, taketh away the frecles and litle swellynges of
the face. Their brothe leadeth out excrementes, and profi&#173;
teth as well against the gnawyng of the guttes, as also a&#173;
gainst inflammations and other diseases of the mother. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Linon</term>. </note>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Lithargyrus. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">DIoscorides in his fifte boke and . 62. Chapiter (treating
of <term lang="la">Lithargyros</term>), noteth three kyndes therof. One to be
made of a certeine Leady sande in the fornaces, an other or
siluer, and the thyrde of leade. We of a common opinion
also recken three sortes, as of leade, siluer, &#38;  of golde: which 
two last are inded both one thing: (though we haue of a 
long tyme by reason of the name) thought <term lang="la">Lithargyrum aure<expan type="">um</expan></term>. 
to come onely of golde. For <term lang="gk">&#x03BB;&#x03B9;&#x03B8;&#x03AC;&#x03C1;&#x03B3;&#x03C5;&#x03C1;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>, is by common con&#173;
sent interpreted <term lang="la">Spuma argenti</term>, and commeth thus: as out of 
Brasauolus, and MatthiolusI haue gathered. 

When the finers of metalles (hauing taken a masse of
siluer out of his owne veyne,) woulde trie it from the me&#173;



</page>

<page no="62">
talles, myxed therwith, (whiche are lead and brasse:) They
put it into a certeyn fornace, being first wrapped in plates
of lead, or into muche lead already melted, in the fornace or 
meltyng pitte: whiche is prepared of ashes after their ma&#173;
ner. On the whiche (beinge melted together) ryseth a cer&#173;
teyne spume, whiche is gathered together with an Iron
instrument, and is called <term lang="la">Spuma argenti</term>. And the same (if
the greater store of brasse therin doe geue vnto it a goul&#173;
den coloure,) is called <term lang="gk">&#x03C7;&#x03C1;&#x03C5;&#x03C3;&#x03B9;&#x03C4;&#x03B9;&#x03C2;</term>, and vulgarly in Latin, by
the imitation of the greke, <term lang="la">Lithargyrum aureum</term>, and that only
for the coloure: and yet is it not to be doubted, but that of
goulde there ryseth also a Spume in the triyng, whiche I
thynke may moste worthily be called <term lang="la">Lithargyrum auri</term>. An
other friuolous varietie of opinions there is, about the sole
or symple namyng of the same: for the common opinion is
that <term lang="la">Lithargyrum</term> so wrytten in receptes, without any other
addition of difference, ough to be taken for litarge of lead. 
Platearius woulde haue it taken for the litarge of goulde:
but the ancient and true interpretation, is aboue shewed. 

<term lang="la">Lythargyros</term> dryeth by the wytness of Galen, moste mo&#173;
deratly, consistynge in a certeyne measure, bothe of qua&#173;
lities and faculties: yet dothe it meanly bynde and scoure,
and is by ryght as a meane amonge other metalles. And
therfore vse we it, as the matter or substance of others:
none other wyse then wexe, among thynges lyquide. It 
healeth gaulynges or chafinges: and beynge broken with
wyne, auayleth againste vlcers, that are for their moi&#173;
sture harde to heale: and that without gnawynge, if it bee
washed. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Lithargyros</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Chrysitis</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De symp. li. 9. </term>
<term lang="la">De com. med. 
secun. genera. 
lib. 1. </term></note>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Lumbrici. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="pulse(n)" lexeme="fig(n)" lexeme="bean(n)">Looke at <term lang="la">Vermes terrestres. </term>



</page>

<page no="63">
<term lang="la">Lupinus</term> 
CAlled in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03B4;&#x03AD;&#x03C1;&#x03BC;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>, is a kynde of pulse, descri&#173;
bed of maister Turner at large, (who calleth it <term lang="en">figge
bene)</term> whose immesurable bitternes sheweth it to be
whotte and drye, and hath vertue digestiue, and scowryng
away. Beyng watered before, they lose their bytternesse,
and may be eaten: but they are of grosse nuryshement. 
They kyll wormes rather layde to, or eaten: they purge
also the lyuer and the splene, and drawe out <term lang="la">Menses &#38;  foe&#173;
tum, cum myrrha &#38;  melle.</term> The meale of <term lang="la">Lupines</term> boyled in vi&#173;
neger <term lang="la">Cum posca aut oxymelite</term>, digesteth blewe hurtes, wen&#173;
nes, and scrofules: and mondyfieth scabbes, scalles of the
head, and all maligne vlcers, without mordication: and 
purgeth the skynne of frecles, spottes, and blewe markes. 
And thus muche of <term lang="la">Syluestris</term>,
whiche I haue growynge in my garden at Maydstone. 
whose vertues are lyke the former, but they doe all these
thynges more strongly. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Thermos</term>. </note>
<note type="m">Galen. <term lang="la">de sym. 
lib. 6. </term></note>
<lemenote>
"Figge bene" antedates the earliest OED citation (1657). 
</lemenote>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="lycium(n)">Lycium. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">IS the Iuice of a thorny tree, growyng chiefly in Capado&#173;
cia and Lycia, of three cubites heyght, (made by boylynge
the infusion of the rootes and braunches therof, to the
thycknes of hony,) called in Greeke <term lang="gk"> &#x03C0;&#x03C5;&#x03BE;&#x03AC;&#x03BA;&#x03B1;&#x03BD;&#x03B8;&#x03B1; &#x1F94; &#x03BB;&#x03CD;&#x03BA;&#x03B9;&#x03BF;&#x03B7;</term>,
and of Theophrastus <term lang="gk">&#x039F;&#x03BD;&#x03CC;&#x03C0;&#x03C5;&#x03BE;<expan type="0+sigma">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>, <term lang="la">hoc est Buxus asininus</term>, which we
may call an <term lang="en">asse boxe tree</term>: hauynge leaues lyke the boxe
tree, with a lyght, faste, and bitter frute, like pepper: a pale
coloured rynde, (not vnlyke to washed <term lang="la">Lycium</term>,) and many 
croked rootes. 

<term lang="la">Lycium</term>, (that liquid medicine,) sayeth Galen, digesteth,
and dryeth in the seconde degree, heateth moderatly, and
consisteth of dyuers substances: as one of thinne partes,
whotte and digestynge, an other earthlye and colde. 



</page>

<page no="64">
Wherof it hath not a little adstriction, and is vsed to the 
blewe markes of bruses, to the infl<expan type="">am</expan>mations of the mouthe
and fundam<expan type="">en</expan>t, to exulcerations, teters, rottennes, froward
and stubbern vlcers, to chafinges, to sanious runnynge
eares, and to nayle wheles: and putteth from the eyes, the
dulnes of syghte. It is conuenient both in drynke and Cli&#173;
sters, for the fluxe of the belly and <term lang="la">Dysenteria</term>: and is geuen
for the coughe, and spitting of bloud. And thus farre of the 
true thing. For the <term lang="la">Lycium</term> that is now vsed and sold of the
Apothecaries, is of the learned iudged conterfeite, &#38;  decei&#173;
uable: for that it is not apte to burne, neyther yeldeth a
redde spume when it is quenched, as <term lang="la">Lycium</term> shoulde. And
where <term lang="la">Lycium</term> should be blacke without, and redde within
It is blacke both without and within. 

Another kinde of <term lang="la">Lycium</term> also doth Dioscorides menti<expan type="">on</expan>,
called <term lang="la">Lycium Indicum</term>, made of a plant called <term lang="la">
Lonchitis</term>: which
Dodon&#230;us affirmeth to be solde of the Apothecaries, vnder 
the name of <term lang="la">Sanguinis draconis</term>, for the which looke vnder the
same title. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Pyxacantha</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Lycion</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Onopyxos</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Dioscor. lib. 1. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De symp. me. 
facult. lib. 7. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Lonchitis</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Sanguis dra&#173;
conis</term>. </note>

<form lang="en">Malua. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="mallow(n)"><term lang="gk">&#x039C;&#x0391;&#x03BB;&#x03AC;&#x03C7;&#x03B7;</term>, so called of the Grecians (of <term lang="gk">&#x039C;&#x03B1;&#x03BB;&#x03AC;&#x03C4;&#x03C4;&#x03B5;&#x03B9;&#x03B7;</term>, <term lang="la">Id est
mollire</term>,) because it mollifieth the belly, (and Varro con&#173;
t<expan type="">en</expan>deth also it to be called <term lang="la">Maluam, quasi moluam</term>, for the cause
aboue sayde) is of Dioscorides and Galen deuided into two
kyndes: namely <term lang="la">Hortensis</term>, (for it is planted and groweth in 
gardines,<emend err="(">)</emend> and <term lang="la">Syluestris</term> so called not because it groweth in
wooddes or roughe places, but wildly of it selfe in laye, and
vntitled groundes. And so of a great number moe. Hereof
there are two kyndes: whervnto some doe also reken <term>Al&#173;
th&#230;a</term>, or <term lang="la">Malua uiscus</term>, called of <term lang="la">Actius</term> <term lang="gk">&#x0394;&#x03B5;&#x03BD;&#x03B4;&#x03C1;&#x03CC;&#x03BC;&#x03B1;&#x03BB;&#x03AC;&#x03C7;&#x03B7;</term> and of
Galen <term lang="gk">&#x1F05;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;&#x03C3;&#x03B1;&#x03BB;&#x03B8;&#x03B4;&#x03B9;&#x03B1;</term>: wherof vnder <term lang="en">Alth&#230;a</term>. 

<term lang="la">Malua agrestis</term>, (sayeth Galen) hath some digestinge, and
lyght mollifyng facultie. But <term lang="la">Hortensis</term> is apter to be eat<expan type="">en</expan>,
and frendly to the belly, but yet hurtfull to the stomache,



</page>

<page no="65">
and howe muche the more it hath of watery moysture, so
muche the weaker are the vertues therof. So is the sede
also so muche the stronger, as it is dryer, but <term lang="en">Alth&#230;a</term> dothe
of all other digeste moste effectuously. Of temperamente
he sayeth it is whotte, whiche the Arabians seme to deny. 
Malowes sayth Dioscorides, both healeth the stynging of 
bees and waspes, and withstandeth the same, and is good
for the griefes of the mother, the guttes, and the bladder:
and beyng drunke in tyme, is a remedy against al venims. </xpln>

<form lang="la">Malum punicum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="pomegranate(n)">THe p<expan type="">om</expan>granate is <expan type="">the</expan> frute of a tree, called in greke <term lang="gk">&#x03A1;&#x03BF;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;, &#x03BA;&#x03B1;&#x03B9; &#x03C1;&#x03CC;&#x03B1;</term>, in Latin <term lang="la">Malus punica, seu granata</term>, and is of twoo

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Malum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="apple(n)">Appuls are called <term lang="gk">&#x039C;&#x1F95;&#x03BB;&#x03B1;</term>, and <term lang="la">Mala</term>, (as the apple tree is



</page>

<page no="66">
called <term lang="gk">&#x03BC;&#x03B7;&#x03BB;&#x03AD;&#x03B1;</term>, and <term lang="la">Malus</term>) and are generally of two kyndes:
(wherof somwhat at <term lang="la">Agresta</term>) otherwise
infinite, and of dyuers natures (as are also their leaues and
ryndes. ) The appuls that are adstringente, haue a colde
and earthy iuice, and doe coole inflammations of heate and
moysture. The sharpe ones are bothe colde and subtyl, and
incysiue: of meane temperamente are the swete ones,
whiche doe inclyne to heate, as those that are watery and
without taste doe bende to coldenes. Of these vsed accor&#173;
ding to their qualities, may the distemperatures of the sto&#173;
mache be cured: as with the meanly sharpe and with very
tarte appuls, you may roborate the lose &#38;  feynte stomache,
glewe together woundes, and repercusse the fluxe of great
inflammations: but suche as are more watery, doe put a&#173;
waye lesse inflamations, that doe but nowe begynne. 
They are generally of harde digestion and ill iuice, but
may bee geuen after meate to suche as lacke apetyte, and
concocte slowly, to suche also as are vexed with womyting,
Diarrhoea, and Dysenteria. Whervnto very tarte appulles
are moste commodious. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en">Manna. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="en">Manna</term> so called of the Arabians, is sayeth Mesues the
dewe of a vapor, lyfted by and concocted in a tem&#173;
perate and fertille ayre, fallyng (in the twye lyghte
tymes sayeth Matthiolus) through plesant aspectes vpon cer&#173;
teyne plantes and stones, wherof there are twoo differen&#173;
ces. That whiche falleth vpon stones, is coagulated round
lyke droppes or sedes, whiche oughte to bee newe, swete,
whytyshe, or at least a citrinyshe colour. That whiche fal&#173;
leth on plantes, bothe taketh of them some qualitie, and 
hath myred therwith some of their leaues and floures,
though not muche: wherof that whiche is newe, and why&#173;
tyshe is beste. Of this doubtlesse dyd Galen wryte, <term lang="la">In Lib. 3. 
De alimentis</term>: where he sayeth, that in the mounte Libanus,



</page>

<page no="67">
men vse yearly to spread skynnes on the grounde, and af&#173;
ter they beate the trees, gathering vp that whiche falleth, 
and fille pottes with the hony: whiche they call <term lang="gk">&#x03B4;&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03C3;&#x03BF;&#x03BC;&#x03AD;
&#x03BB;&#x03B9;, &#x1F94; &#x03B1;&#x03B5;&#x03C1;&#x03CC;&#x03BC;&#x03B5;&#x03BB;&#x03B9;</term>, <term lang="la">Hoc est mel roscidum, &#38;  mel aerium</term>. Though Aue&#173;
rois Cordubensis affirme (not without the opinion of dyuers
great clerkes of late dayes) Manna to haue ben vnknowne
to Galen, and it to haue comde in vse synce hys tyme, be&#173;
cause in dede in all Galens workes, there is no mention
therof vnder that name: Who mente by Manna (and also
Dioscorides with other olde Grecians) a farre other thing,
as hereafter will apeare. But Mesues procedynge sayeth,
that it somwhat excedeth the meane in heate, scoureth and
smotheth the throte, the breste, and the stomache: purgeth
choler gentilly, and quencheth thyrste: and mixed wyth
strong medicines, it bettereth their acti<expan type="">on</expan>s. And thus much
of <term lang="la">Marina Arabum</term> (who call it also <term lang="la">Thereniabin) uel Recentium</term>, na&#173;
med also <term lang="la">Manna Orientalis</term>: for <term lang="la">Manna Gr&#230;corum</term>, called also <term lang="la">Man&#173;
na Thuris</term>, are certeyne fragmentes taken out of pure fran&#173;
kincense, whiche Galen calleth the drosse of the same, affir&#173;
ming it to be of the same nature, how be it somewhat more
adstringent, by reason of the frankincense ryndes that are
somwhat myngled therwith, wherof also at Thus. somwhat
more. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Drosomelid</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Mel Roscidum
uel reruleni<expan type="">um</expan></term></note>

mel atherium
siue mellago</term>. </note>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="mandragora(n)" lexeme="lettuce(n)" lexeme="mandrake(n)">Mandragora</form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="gk">&#x039C;&#x0391;&#x03BD;&#x03B4;&#x03C1;&#x03B1;&#x03B3;&#x03CC;&#x03C1;&#x03B1;&#x03C2;</term>, is also called <term lang="gk">&#x039A;&#x03B9;&#x03C1;&#x03BA;&#x03B1;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;</term> (saith Fuchsius a <term lang="la">Circe</term>,
because it is thought to worke amorous affectes, &#38;  of

haue Fuchsius and maister doctor Turner (our contrey&#173;
man,) right well detected. Herof after Dioscorides, there
are twoo kyndes: as the male, called <term lang="la">Mandragoras mas, uel 
Candida,</term> and also <term lang="la">Mandragoras morion</term>, whyche is with vs not
verye rare, And the female called <term lang="la">Mandragoras foemina</term>,

It cooleth, by the testimonie of Galen, in the thyrde ex&#173;
cesse, though saith he in the appuls ther is not a little heate
and moisture: whiche therfore prouoke depe sleapes. The
rynde of the roote doth bothe coole and drye. The leaues
of mandrage are profitable, as sayeth Dioscorides, for the
inflammations and other affectes of the eyes, caused of vl&#173;
cers in them, or in other partes howe so euer: and mete to
deuide euery harde tumore. It is of suche mollifiynge ver&#173;
tues, that Iuory (as some thynke) beyng boyled with the 
roote therof, may be made softe, and apte to be wrought. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Massacunia. </form>
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="la">Massacunia. </term>, (for so fynde I it wrytten in the prynted la&#173;
tin copy, and interpreted the fylinge or scarpynge of
earthen pottes glased,) is written of Almanzoar (who for <term lang="la">Al&#173;
bula in oculo</term>, maketh the same recept) Masacuna, and Pandacta&#173;
rius wryteth it <term lang="la">Massacuna</term>: Who sayeth it is a coloure wher&#173;
with earthen vessels are nealed, and called also of them <term>Pe&#173;
tanum</term>. Shewyng moreouer the opinion of diuers, as that 
some saye it is the water of glasen vesselles, and others
that it is glasse not perfectly boiled, and is the mater wher
of glasse is made, and vulgarly called <term lang="la">Massa cocta</term>, &#38;c. Whose
vertues, because the mater is vncerteyne, and the autori&#173;
ties scant probable, (for in Galen, Dioscorides, and other 
auncientes I fynde it not) I leave to silence. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="mastic(n)">Mastiche. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="gk">&#x039C;&#x0391;<expan type="">&#x03C3;&#x03C4;</expan>&#x03AF;&#x03C7;&#x03BA;</term> comm<emend err="l&#38;o+_;"><expan type="">on</expan>l</emend>y called <term lang="la">Mastix</term>, and of Dioscorides <term lang="la">Resi&#173;
na Lentiscus</term>, (called also in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03C7;&#x03B9;&#x03BD;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>) is of tempe&#173;
rament whotte and drye in the seconde degree: Wherof
Galen maketh two kyndes, namely 
<term lang="la">Candida</term>, &#38; <term lang="la">Nigra</term>. The



</page>

<page no="69">
whyte mastike sayeth he, (callynge it also <term lang="la">Mastiche Chia</term>, be&#173;
cause the beste and fayrest groweth plentifully in <term lang="la">Chio)</term>) doth
bynde and mollifie: and therfore is proffitable, for the in&#173;
flammations of the stomache, the belly, the lyuer, and the
bowelles: To the olde coughe also, and as sayeth Dioscori&#173;
des to the reiecti<expan type="">on</expan>s of bloude. It is frendly to the stomache,
but moueth belchyng, and is vsed to scoure the teethe and
the face. 

The blacke masticke (called <term lang="la">Mastiche Aegiptia</term>) dryeth more
but byndeth lesse, &#38;  therfore is the meter for suche thinges
as require stronger digestion, by euaporation. </xpln>
<note type="m">Dioscor. <term lang="la">lib. 1. </term></note>
<note type="m">Galen. <term lang="la">de sym. 
faculta. lib. 7. </term>
Chius is an 
Isle of the 
sea Aegeum. </note>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Mel. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="honey(n)">IS called of the Greekes <term lang="gk">&#x03BC;&#x03AD;&#x03BB;&#x03B9;</term>, whiche some thynke to be
the humore of the ayre purging it selfe, for it is someti&#173;
mes founde to cleaue vpon mens heare and apparell,
that are abrode very rathe. wherfore Plinie iudgeth it the 
swette of heauen, or els a certeine slymines or spittle of the 
starres. And therof are thre kindes: as <term lang="la">Mel anthinii, uel Ver&#173;
num</term>, which is gathered in the spring: <term lang="la">Mel hor&#230;um, aut astiuum</term>,
gathered in the sommer: and <term lang="la">Ericeum</term>, whiche is smally este&#173;
med, for that it is gathered in Autumne, when <term lang="en">Erica</term> onely
bloweth in the wooddes. The best hony is gathered in som<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
mer, of Thyme and suche other odiferus plantes, whiche
heateth and dryeth in the seconde degree, and mundifieth. 
Others eyther allowe or disprayse it, in respecte of the place 
where it is gathered: praysinge <term lang="la">Mel atticum</term> for the beste,



</page>

<page no="70">
next to that <term lang="la">Mel Siculum</term>, and chiefly that whiche is called
<term lang="la">Hybleum</term> of Hybla, a citie of Sicilia, and that because of the
plentie of Thyme growynge there. But <term lang="la">Mel Sardoum</term> &#38; 
<term lang="la">Ponticum</term>, are (as euell and hurtfull) abiected. 

Galenus. <term lang="la">lib. 3. De alimentorum facultatibus</term>, sayeth that it is
founde on the leaues of plantes, but neyther can it sayeth
he be sayde, eyther any iuice, frute, or parte of the same. 

And here must you take Galen to speake of the primi&#173;
tiue matter, or first substance therof. For if there shoulde
not be graunted to hony suche a varietie of qualities, accor&#173;
dyng to the plantes wheron it is, As Mesues noteth in 
<term lang="en">Manna</term>: Why should it haue ben estemed the worthyer, or 
the vyler, for the plantes wheron it is founde. 

Hony not well boyled, bredeth certeyn wyndes in the
stomache and bowells: but beyng well clarified, it is vn&#173;
wyndie and moueth vryne. It taken without the admyx&#173;
tion of water, nourysheth weakely, but emptieth the belly
aptly, and eaten largely it moueth vomite: beyng boyled
without water, it neither causeth vomite, nor loseth the
belly. To olde and colde persons, it is moste commodious:
In yonge and whotter persons it turneth into choler. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Meli. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Mel Vernum. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Mel astiuum. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Mel Ericeum. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term>Erica. </term></note>
<note type="m">Galenus <term lang="la">de
symp. medi. 
fac. li. 4. &#38;. 7</term></note>

<form lang="en" lexeme="melilot(n)">Melilotus. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">THe herbe that we in Englande vse for Melilote, lac&#173;

gia</term>. It is whotte and drye in the fyrste degree. Galen saieth
it hath more heate then colde, and is somwhat adstringent,
but yet doth it digeste and concocte: And the emplaster
called <term lang="la">Meliotum</term>, is potente in mollifiyng hardnesses, by

<form lang="la">Mica panis. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="bread-crumb(n)">CRommes of bread are generally resolutiue: but that

lyght corne, well leuened, and moderatly baked, is moste
easye to c<expan type="">on</expan>cocte, so is it of power outwardly to digeste and
deuyde: and of consequence if it varie from these poyntes, it
is the vnfitter for the one and the other. </xpln>
