<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="mummia(n)">Mumia. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="">OF <term lang="ar">Mumia</term> (so called of the Arabians. ) There is an vn&#173;
certeine varietie of opinions, for diuers great lear&#173;
ned men thinke it to be <term lang="gk">&#x03A0;&#x03B9;&#x03C3;&#x03C3;&#x03AC;&#x03C3;&#x03C6;&#x03B1;&#x03BB;&#x03C4;&#x03BF;&#x03B7;</term> <term>Dioscoridis</term>, be&#173;
cause that as Dioscorides sayeth, <term lang="gkt">Pissasphaltos</term> is driuen by
lande floudes from certeyn hylles of Apolonia, called <term lang="la">Ceraunii
montes</term>, and being after cast vp on the ryuer bankes: is by
heate baked into Cloddes, whiche doe smell lyke pytche,
myred with <term lang="en">Bitumine</term>, wherof it hathe that name. Euen so
and with the same wordes, hath Serapio descrybed his <term>Mu&#173;
miam</term>. But differing from this is that which the most of the 
Arabians doe mention: who affirme it to resulte of the
imbaumyng or spicery of dead bodyes at their burialles, as
<term lang="la">Ex Croco, Aloe, myrrha, &#38;  Balsamo</term>, beyng coagulated &#38;  growne
together (with the fatte and moysture of the corps) into a
But as this composition is very costly, and therfore vsed
for the bodies of the nobles: so are men of some lesse repu&#173;
tation imbaulmed with Aloe, myrhe, and saffron: neyther
of the whiche bodies are easely spoyled of their tombes and
spicerie, namely by strangers. Howe be it the poorer sorte,
(whiche are the greatest number, the openinge also and re&#173;
mouyng of whose bodies is the more tollerable) are inclo&#173;
sed with <term lang="la">Pissaphalto</term> only, because it is of lesse price: for they



</page>

<page no="73">
vse customably to imbaulme all bodies. 

But that whiche is nowe among our Apothecaries ex&#173;
tant, varieth from all these substances, and is the very flesh
of mans body, as it weare burned to a cole: for both whole
armes and whole legges, haue been here not rarly seene,
being dryed as blacke as cole. Wherfore it must nedes 
be thought, that eyther the merchantes bryng from thence
whole buried bodies, or very partes of the same: or els that
it becommeth so, by that meanes that dyuers merchantes
make reporte of , (if it be worthy credite. ) Who saye, that in
the farther partes of Aegypte, great driftes of lyghte and 
moste subtyle sande, are at certeyn tymes remoued (by the
change of wyndes) from place vnto place, and therby row&#173;
led vp into huge heapes lyke great mountaynes: and that
often tymes bothe men and beastes, happening in the way,
are violently swalowed vp and buried therein: so remay&#173;
ning vntyll by a lyke tempeste from the obiect coastes, it be
agayne turned ouer, and all such buried thinges are again
vncouered: beynge by the whotte and drye nature of the
sande so dryed, and preserued from putrifaction. All the
whiche howe true it is or may be, for the lacke of experi<expan type="">en</expan>ce
I am not able vndoubtfully to saye, neyther can I (through
lykelyhodes) yelde for the same a better defence, then that
baked or dryed sande, hath been proued to preserue frutes
longtyme vnputrefied: wyshinge that some man of more
experience therin, would make the matter more manifest. 
But certeinly it is mans fleshe, eyther thus or otherwyse 
dryed into a cole. 

It is vsed as well in outwarde plasters, as inwarde
drinkes, to confounde broken bones and veynes, and to di&#173;
solue congeiled bloud, and stayeth the fluxe of the same. It
helpeth <term lang="la">Hemicraniam</term> &#38;  <term lang="la">Paralisin</term>, and the peynes of the heade
that come of cold without humores. For it is of heating and
driyng facultie in the seconde degre, as sayeth Matthiolus: to
whom I remitte the explication of all other his eximiouse
vertues. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="en">Bitumen</term>. </note>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="myrobalan(n)">Myrobalanus. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">THe etymology of this worde <term lang="gk"> &#x039C;&#x03C5;&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03B2;&#x03AC;&#x03BB;&#x03B1;&#x03BD;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>, requireth
it to be called <term lang="la">Glans odorata, siue unguentaria</term>: though this
name <term lang="la">Glans unguentaria</term> be much more fitly applyed to
<term lang="gk">&#x03B2;&#x03AC;&#x03BB;&#x03B1;&#x03BD;&#x03BF;&#x03B7; &#x03BC;&#x03C5;&#x03C1;&#x03B5;&#x03C8;&#x03B9;&#x03BA;&#x03B7;&#x03B7;</term>: for from it differeth <term lang="la">Myrobalanos</term>, in figu&#173;
re, colour, and faculties, and ought rather sayeth Syluius, to
be accompted of the kynde of appuls or prunes. 

Of Myrobalanes there are fiue kyndes. wherof these
these thre <term lang="la">Citre&#230;</term> <term lang="la">Negr&#230;</term> &#38; <term lang="la">Cepul&#230;</term>, are thought of some saith
Mesues to be the frutes of one tree, whose propertie is to 
beare twyse yearly: whose first frutes are <term lang="la">Citre&#230;</term>, beyng ga&#173;
thered vnrype, and <term lang="la">Negr&#230;</term> or <term lang="la">Ind&#230;</term>, gathered when they are
rype. The seconde and laste frutes are <term lang="la">Cepul&#230;</term>. (These all
are colde in the first degree, and drye in the seconde. ) But
Andreas Marinus vpon Mesue, sayeth plainly that they are . v. 
destincted fructes, of so many seueral trees, and hath lyuely
paynted out three of them: whiche also many other haue
thought before, because thei vary in faculties. Loe so boun&#173;
tifull hath God ben vnto this our age, as to open merciful&#173;
ly vnto vs (with the landes so longe vnknowne) manye
thynges, that to the worthy fathers weare eyther vnkno&#173;
wne, or muche in doubte: For so doth Marinus confesse hym
selfe to obteyne this certeintie. They are benigne or gen&#173;
till medicines, and doe strengthen the harte, the stomache,
the lyuer, and the rest of the body. 

1 <term lang="la">Myrobalam Citre&#230;</term> (which are of colour inclining to grene,
great, weightie, massie, and within when they are broken,



</page>

<page no="75">
gummy,) do purge choler aboue the rests: and therfore are 
holsome for whotte and bilious natures, &#38;<term lang="la"> cum omphacio, uel
aqua rosarum, uel succo foenicula</term> they scoure the eyes, and take
away their inflammations: their poulder also stayeth the
fluxe of them, &#38;<term lang="la"> cum mastiche</term> dryeth vlcers. 

2 <term lang="la">Myrobalani Cepul&#230;</term> are blackishe, inclining to rednes, and
are phlegme purgers, namely fr<expan type="">om</expan> the stomache, they str<expan type="">en</expan>g&#173;
then also the same &#38; the spirites rational, &#38; sharpen <expan type="">the</expan> sight. 

3 <term lang="la">Myrobalani Nigr&#230;</term>, are of colour blacke, greate, flesshye, and
grosse, whose vertues are to purge melancholy, and choler
adust: and therfore are profitable for tr<expan type="">em</expan>bling, pensiuenes,
leprosies, quartanes, and other melancholick affectes. But
they are all apte to brede obstructions. 

4 <term lang="la">Myrobalani Emblic&#230;</term> do cole s<expan type="">om</expan>what, &#38; drie in <expan type="">the</expan> first degree. 

5 <term lang="la">Myrobalani Belleric&#230;</term> are gentill, and doe strengthen, of t<expan type="">em</expan>&#173;
perament lyke the first three: They both doe purge putre&#173;
fied phlegme from the stomache, and strengthen the same,
The brayne also, the harte, the lyuer, the synewes, and re&#173;
laxed partes. &#38;c. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="myrrh(n)">Myrrha. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="en">MYrrha</term>, whiche the Grekes call <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03BC;&#x03AF;&#x03C1;&#x03BD;&#x03B1;&#x03B7;</term>, heateth and
dryeth in the seconde degree: and therfore glueth
freshe woundes, especially of the head: Hauing also
much bitternes, wherby it killeth wormes. It hath moreo&#173;
uer a moderate abstertion: by reason wherof, it is mixed <expan type="w+c">with</expan>
medicines made for the eyes, for the olde cough, &#38; for pein&#173;
full breathing. It hath also power to comfort and to defend
from putrefaction, and to expell superfluities. It m<expan type="">un</expan>difieth
rotten vlcers, and prouoketh sleape. Howe be it the vse of
Myrrhe is not altogether hurtles, bothe for that the onely
smell therof causeth head ache: and also because in the best
myrrhe is founde <term lang="la">Opocarpasum</term>, a thyng sayeth Galen, verye
hurtfull and deadly, and hath kylled many vnwittynglye
takyng it with myrrhe. 



</page>

<page no="76">
<term lang="en">Myrrha</term> is the teares or droppyng of a tree growynge in
Arabia, not vnlike to <term lang="la">Spin&#230; &#230;gioti&#230;</term>, wherof ther are dyuers
kyndes. <term lang="la">Pediasmos</term> beyng fatty, from the whiche beyng pres&#173;
sed, commeth <term lang="la">Stacte Gabirea</term>, whiche is most fatte, and swea&#173;
teth out <term lang="la">Stacten</term> habundantly. Then <term lang="la">Troglodytica(n)</term>, whiche is
of a grenyshe colour, shyning and byting: whiche is the best
and is also of twoo sortes. The fourth is <term lang="la">Caucalis</term>, whiche is
very olde, blacke, and parched: And this is thought of some
to be it, that is commonly solde vnto vs. The fifthe and
worst is <term lang="la">Ergasima</term>: And the sixt <term lang="la">Aminea</term>, not wel approued. An
other kynde also there is, called <term lang="la">Myrrha Boeotica</term>, because it
c<expan type="">om</expan>meth from the roote of a tree, growyng in Boeotia. </xpln>
<note type="m">Smirna. </note>
<note type="m">Galenus  <term lang="la">de
symp. medi. 
lib. 8. </term></note>
<note type="m">So was 
myrrhe in Ga
len his time
adulterate. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De antidotis. 
lib. 1. </term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="mirtus(n)">Myrtus. </form> 
<xpln lexeme="myrtle(n)" lexeme="myrt-tree(n)">MYrtles are of an earthly cold propertie, as Galen af&#173;
firmeth, hauyng yet some subtiltie and heate, wher&#173;
through they drye myghtily: and therfore are verye
profitable to helpe the restoring of broken bones, and stre&#173;
igned ioyntes, and also to drye vp bruses, kepynge therby
the places from putrefaction, and apostemations. The
Mirte tree, is called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03BC;&#x03C5;&#x03C1;&#x03C3;&#x03AF;&#x03BD;&#x03B7;</term>, and in Latin <term lang="la">Mirtus</term>. 
The frutes or beries wherof, doe the Apothecaries calle
<term lang="la">Myrcillos</term>. </xpln>

lib. 8. </term></note>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Nodi. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="knot(n)" lexeme="pottage(n)" lexeme="pulse(n)"><term lang="la">Nodus</term> signifieth a knotte, and is here to be vnder&#173;
standed of certeine kyndes of tumores, whiche in
roundnes, (and somtyme hardnes,) resemble a
knotte: comming principally of phlegme, and are
chiefly referred to the three speciall tumores, called of Ga&#173;
len <term lang="gk">&#x1F04;&#x03B8;&#x03B5;&#x03C1;&#x03C9;&#x03BC;&#x03B1;</term>, <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03C4;&#x03B5;&#x03AC;&#x03C4;&#x03C9;&#x03BC;&#x03B1;</term>, and <term lang="gk">&#x039C;&#x03B5;&#x03BB;&#x03B9;&#x03BA;&#x03BA;&#x03C1;&#x1F76;&#x03C2;</term>, so called of the sub&#173;
stances like a whyte potagie confection (called <term lang="en">romane
Puls</term>) fatte, or
hony, conteined in them. And somtime the nerues become
knotty, but that differeth from <term lang="la">Nodo</term> sayeth Avicen, in that
it is not euery waye mobyle or seperable. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="oedema(n)">Oedema. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="gk">&#x039F;&#x0399;&#x03B4;&#x03B7;&#x03BC;&#x03B1;</term>, is one of the foure chiefe and principall
symple tumores agaynst nature, softe, lose, and
without payne, spryngyng of thynne phlegme or vaporous spirites, &#38;  is the same that in the tyme 
of Lanfranke as well as before and since, was called <term lang="la">Vndi&#173;
mia</term>. which (when it hath adioyned partly therwith, any of
the other three) is called eyther <term lang="gk">&#x1F44;&#x03B9;&#x03B4;&#x03B7;&#x03BC;&#x03B1; &#x03C6;&#x03BB;&#x03B5;&#x03B3;&#x03BC;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;&#x03CE;&#x03B4;&#x03B5;&#x03C2; </term>. <term lang="la">i. Oe&#173;
dema phlegmonosum, uel Oedema inflammationis particeps</term>, of bloud:
<term lang="gk">&#x039F;&#x03B9;&#x03B4;&#x03B7;&#x03C0;&#x03BC;&#x03B1;&#x03B5;&#x03C1;&#x03C5;&#x03C3; &#x03B9;&#x03AD;&#x03BB;&#x03B1;&#x03C4;&#x03CE;&#x03C4;&#x03B5;&#x03C2;</term>. <term lang="la">i. Oedema erysipelatosum, aut sacri ignis
particeps</term>, if choler be his partener, or <term lang="gk">&#x039F;&#x03B9;&#x03B4;&#x03B7;&#x03BC;&#x03B1; &#x03C3;&#x03BA;&#x03B9;&#x03B3;&#x03B3;&#x03C1;&#x03C9;&#x03B4;&#x03B5;&#x03C2;</term>, <term lang="la">hoc
est oedema induratum, siue duritiei particeps</term>, when melancholie is
adioyned. More at <term lang="la">Vndimia</term>. </xpln>

moribus pr&#230;&#173;
ter naturam</term>. </note>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="oleum(n)">Oleum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="olive oil(n)">OYle which the Grecians cal <term lang="gk">&#x0395;&#x03BB;&#x03B1;&#x1F35;&#x03B9;&#x03BF;&#x03B7;</term>, is the iuce of O&#173;
lyues named <term lang="la">Oliu&#230;</term>, whiche are the frutes of the tree,
Olea in greke <term lang="gk">&#x0395;&#x03BB;&#x03B1;&#x03B9;&#x03B1;</term>, c<expan type="">on</expan>sisting of . iii. subst<expan type="">an</expan>ces (as doth
mylke,) as of grosse dregges, called <term lang="la">Amurca</term>, of an aery essen&#173;
ce, &#38;  of a whayey substance. Oyle is of temperam<expan type="">en</expan>t meane
as it weare, betwene whotte and colde drye and moyste:
yet doth it rather inclyne to heate and moisture. But from 
this meane may it three wayes varie, as firste <term lang="la">Omphacinum</term>
(made of vnripe Oliues) is somwhat colde and adstringent:
as the oyle of through rype olyues (which they cal <term lang="la">Drupas</term>)
doth moderatly heate and moyste. Secondly newe oyle is 
by comparation colde: so swete oyle kept vntyl it be olde,
is whotte and euaporatyue. Thirdly is doth moste aptly or 
redely receyue the qualities, of whatso euer by arte is ther



</page>

<page no="78">
with composed: be they whotte or colde, &#38;c. And therfore
sayeth Galen, is it worthily accompted, the matter of all 
other medicines. 

It moueth the belly and the reddyshe wheye, beyng ta&#173;
ken a way (as by washynge or els howsoeuer) the reste is
made whyter, and without byting. The vncti<expan type="">on</expan>s therwith
are rather to be vsed to whole bodies th<expan type="">en</expan> vnto plethorikes,
or to men possessed with rawe humores. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Elaon</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">El&#230;a</term>. 
</note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Drupas. </term></note> 
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De symp. me. 
fac. li. 2</term></note>

<form lang="en" lexeme="olibanum(n)">Olibanum(n). </form> 
<xpln lang="en">OLibamon sayeth Platearius, is whotte and drie in the 
seconde degree, and is none other thyng but <term lang="la">Thus</term>.

thought of dyuers men, (not without great reason, to bee

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="ophthalmia(n)">Ophthalmia</form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="gk">&#x039F;&#x03A6;&#x03B8;&#x03B1;&#x03BB;&#x03BC;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;</term>, in Latin <term lang="la">Lippitudo</term>. Is the inflammation of that panicle that couereth all the other panicles &#38;
partes of the eye, called <term lang="la">Tunica adnata</term> (of Galen <term lang="la">Agna&#173;
ta</term>) and <term lang="la">Coberens</term>, and is of twoo sortes. <term lang="la">Vera</term>, comminge of a 
cause antecedent or interiore, as of fulnes in all the body,
of the influxion of sharpe humores, or of grosse and flatu&#173;
lente spirites: &#38; <term lang="la">nonuera</term>, whiche commeth of a cause proca&#173;
tarctyke or exterior, as of a stroke, duste, smoke, &#38;c. Suche
a difference of <term lang="la">Obtalmia</term> (for so he calleth it) maketh Lanfran&#173;
cus as thou seest, calling it <term lang="la">Parua</term>, <term lang="la">magna</term>, or <term lang="la">maxima</term>. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="opium(n)">Opium</form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="la">&#x039F;&#x03C0;&#x03B9;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, <term lang="la">Id est lacrima papaueris</term>, whiche is the iuice of <term lang="la">Papaue&#173;



</page>

<page no="79">
ris nigri</term>, brought by arte into a masse, muste nedes be lyke
in temperament to the popye, wherof it is made: and is
sayeth Galen the myghtiest among narcotike medicines,
and causeth dead sleape, but ought rarly, in great extremi&#173;
ties (and then warly) to be vsed. For it strangleth, and ve&#173;
hement paynes are often eased therby (beyng vsed alone)
for some small tyme: after the whiche it returneth more
violently then before. But in euery doubte of this and his
vse: let maister Turner be to you a sufficient satisfier. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="opopanax(n)">Opopanax. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="gk">&#x039F;&#x03C0;&#x03BF;&#x03C0;&#x03AC;&#x03BD;&#x03B1;&#x03BE;</term>, <term lang="la">Succus panace</term>, that is the iuice of the herbe <term lang="gk">&#x03A0;&#x03AC;
&#x03BD;&#x03B1;&#x03BA;&#x03B5;&#x03C2; &#x1F25;&#x03C1;&#x03AC;&#x03BA;&#x03BB;&#x03B5;&#x03B9;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, or <term lang="la">Panax</term> there are o&#173;
ther two kyndes, <term lang="la">Asclepium</term> &#38; <term lang="la">Cheironium</term>. It is an herbe
(sayeth Dioscorides) growyng in Boeotia and Arcadia, with
rough herby coloured leaues, liyng on the grounde, inden&#173;
ted with fyue diuisions muche lyke to fygge leaues, a high
stalke, as hath <term lang="la">Ferula</term>, whiche is white throughe a certeine
mosines, and also beset with little leaues: hauynge moreo&#173;
uer a toppe lyke dylle, a yellowe flowre, and a sede feruent 
and odoriferous: with many rootes also, springing fr<expan type="">om</expan> one
beginnyng, beyng whyte, of heauy sauoure, thyck rynded,
and bytter tasted. <term lang="la">Opopanax</term> sayeth Galen, doth both digeste
and mollyfie: and therin excelleth <term lang="la">Chalbanum</term>. It heateth in
the thyrde degree, and drieth in the seconde, (as dothe also
the rynde of the roote, though lesse then the iuice) and that 
without adstringencie: and therfore is vsed to stuberne vl&#173;
cers, and as an incarnatiue to broken bones. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Orificium. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">ORificium, is the orifice, mouthe, or entrance of anye
thinge that is holowe. Wherfore <term lang="la">Orificium sinus, uel
fistule&#230;</term>, is the orifice or entrance, of a fistule or holow
vlcer. &#38;c. </xpln></wordentry>



</page>

<page no="80">
<wordentry type="h"><form lang="en" lexeme="orobus(n)">Orobus. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="bitter(a)" lexeme="vetch(n)" lexeme="pulse(n)">SO called of the Apothecaries, folowynge the Greeke,
(which is <term lang="gk">&#x039F;&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03B2;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>, and the Latine <term lang="la">Eruum</term>) is englyshed
of D. Turner, a bitter fitche: who treateth therof at
large. It is a certein pulse, and a foode (for his vnpleasant&#173;
nes) meter for bullockes then for men, but are vsed in me&#173;
dicines that purge grosse humores, from the lunges and
the breste. The whyte bitter fitches, are less medicinable
 then the yelowe or the pale. <term lang="la">Eruum</term> dryeth in the thyrde de&#173;
gree fully, and heateth in the first: and farther, howe bitter
so euer it be, in the same measure doth it cutte insonder,
scowre, and open obstructions: beynge twyse sodden, it lo&#173;
seth his vnsauorines, and also his scowring and incisiue po&#173;
wer, and so dryeth without great bytternesse. It is diure&#173;
tike and (copiously taken) causeth pyssyng of bloud. Diosco&#173;
rides sayeth, that of them by parchyng and gryndinge is
made a meale, whiche with hony mundifieth vlcers, ke&#173;
pyng dyuers angrye sortes of them, from crepyng and cor&#173;
rodyng: being made into paste with wyne, it healeth vene&#173;
mous bytinges, and scowreth the skynne of freckles and 
spottes. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Eruum. </term></note>
<note type="m">Galenus <term lang="la">De a&#173;
limentis. lib. 1. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De attenu<expan type="">an</expan>te
uictus ratione</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De symp. me. 
facult. lib. 8. </term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Ossa combusta. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">OF burned bones thus sayeth Galen. They drye and 
digeste, and chiefly as some saye, the bones of a man. 
I haue knowne certeyne of our contreymen, that by
geuyng mans bones burned in drinke, haue cured in many
<term lang="la">Epilepsiam</term> and <term lang="la">Arthritin</term>, all this Galen. A great desiccatiue
they must nedes be, for that burnynge addeth to the bone
more drynes, whiche was before of his owne nature colde
and drye. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De sym. med. 
facult. li. 11. </term></note>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="ovum(n)">Oua. </form> 
<xpln lexeme="egg(n)">EGges sayth Galen in his boke last cited, are not partes
of animals, but answer in proportion, to thynges su&#173;



</page>

<page no="81">
perfluous or more then nedeful. They are made for propa&#173;
gation and perpetuitie, and are profitable, as for foode. 
Whervnto the egges of Phesantes and hennes, are the
beste: As of the Goose and the Ostriche, are the worste. 
Egges that are meanly sodden, (called therfore <term lang="la">Tremula</term>) are
for nourishment best of all other. <term lang="la">Sorbilia oua</term> (which be sodde
in water tyll it be whyte) doe nuryshe the lesse, but are easyer
to put downe, and smothe the roughnes of the sharpe ar&#173;
terie. Sodden egges are of harde concoction, of grosse nou&#173;
rishment, and are hardly put ouer: but grosser and more
fumous iuice doe rosted egges make, and tary longer in the
stomache. But fried egges are euery way of worste nurish&#173;
ment, and doe corrupte other meates eaten with them. 
Lastly, egges prepared with oyle, wyne, and the sauce <term lang="la">Garo</term>,
and after meanly baked or rosted, and called <term lang="la">Oua suffocata</term>,
are better then sodde egges or rosted, and are colder then
the equall temperament. 

The whyte of the egge is accompted of the number of
medicines not byting, and is vsed not onely to the eyes, but
also to all other thynges that require gentyll medicines:
as <term lang="la">ad Vlcera c<expan type="">on</expan>tumacia &#38;  pudendor<expan type="">um</expan> ac fedis</term>: so is also the yolke,
(whiche aswageth payne. The whole egge is vsed <term lang="la">Cum oleo
Ros&#230;to</term>, for inflammations: but in all these thynges oughte
newe egges to be taken, and the olde refused. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Ouum. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Galen. de ali&#173;
mentis. lib. 3. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Oua tremula</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Ouum sorbi&#173;
lium</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De euchymia
et cacochymia</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Oua suffocata</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Albumen ovi</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Oui <damage>uel ellus</damage></term>. </note>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Oua formicarum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="egg(n)">SO vnspeakable is the careful loue of our merciful God
towardes his creatures, that he maketh <expan type="">the</expan> litle Ante &#38;
hir egges, medicines for their health. Thei ar foode for
Beares, and their egges (sayeth Plinie) are to them a hol&#173;
some medicine, when they are greued. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="oxalis(n)">Oxalis. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="gk">&#x039F;&#x03BE;&#x03B1;&#x03BB;&#x03AF;&#x03C2;</term>, so called as well of the Apothecaries as of the



</page>

<page no="82">
Grecians, of the sharpe taste that it hath, is one of the . iiii. 
kyndes of <term lang="la">Rumecis</term>, or <term lang="la">Lapathi</term> in Dioscorides, called also of
some <term lang="gk">&#x03B1;&#x03BD;&#x03B1;&#x03BE;&#x03C5;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;&#x03C2;</term>, and commonly <term lang="la">Acetosa</term>, and is of two kyndes,
<term lang="la">Maior</term> and <term lang="la">Minor</term>, the first knowne to all, the other not so. 

It cooleth with a pleasant sharpnes, and is therfore with
vs in great vse. The iuyce therof cooleth &#38;  represseth chole&#173;
ricke inflammati<expan type="">on</expan>s of the stomache and the liuer, and pre&#173;
serueth from pestilent infections if the leaues be chawed,
and the iuice swallowed downe: as haue dyuers learned
men of late practise writen, by whose iudgement it is of t<expan type="">em</expan>&#173;
perament colde and drye in the seconde degree. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Papauer. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="poppy(n)">OF Popy with the Grecians called <term lang="gk">&#x03BC;&#x03AE;&#x03BA;&#x03C9;&#x03BD;</term>, there are 
many kyndes, as <term lang="gk">&#x03AE;&#x03BC;&#x03B5;&#x03C1;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term> <term lang="la">.i. satiuum</term> <term lang="gk">&#x1F04;&#x03B3;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term> <term lang="la">i. syluestre</term>, 
<term lang="gk">&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;&#x03C2;</term> <term lang="la">i. fluidum uel erraticum</term>, <term lang="gk">&#x039A;&#x03B5;&#x03C1;&#x03B1;&#x03C4;&#x1FD6;&#x03C4;&#x03B9;&#x03C2;</term> <term lang="la">cornutum uel cor&#173;
niculatum</term>, <term lang="gk">&#x03B1;&#x03C6;&#x03C1;&#x03CC;&#x03B4;&#x03B5;&#x03C2;</term> <term lang="la">Spumcum &#38;  Herculeum</term>: of euerye of the 
whiche there are also diuers kyndes, whiche here it were
to longe to discusse. They do all coole. 

The whyte seade of <term lang="la">Papaueris Satiui</term>, prouoketh sleape
moderatly, and therfore it is eaten in bread and with hony. 
But of the wylde ones, the seade of <term lang="la">Papaueris fluidi</term>, called al&#173;
so <term lang="la">Rhoeas</term> (because his floures doe sodaynly falle,) doth coole
more myghtyly, and therfore can not be vsed alone with&#173;
out hurte. That of the kynde of <term lang="la">Papaueris syluestri</term>, or <term lang="la">wylde
Popye</term>, whiche hath a fittynge bowle, hathe in the same a
blacke medycinable seade, whiche cooleth myghtylye: but
the seade that is founde in the longe wylde popye bowles,
is of all other the metest for medicine, somniferous, and
stoppeth suche spyttinges, as the cough bryngeth from the 
lunges and the brest, and is conuenient for Catarrhes 
and thynne destillations from the head. It cooleth so muche,
that it may induce bothe dead sleapes, and death also, vn&#173;
warly vsed: for it cooleth in the fourth and last degree. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Mecon emeros</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Mecon agrios</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Mecon rhoeas</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Mec<expan type="">on</expan> ceratitis </term></note>

tum</term>. <term lang="la">Mecon&#173;
aphrodes</term>. <term lang="la">i. 
Papauer spu&#173;
meum</term>. </note>

<page no="83">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Parietaria. </form> 
<xpln lexeme="partridge(n)">VUlgarly <term lang="la">Muralium</term>, because it deliteth to growe on 
stone walles: Of some <term lang="la">Vrceolaris</term>, because it serueth
well to scowre glasen vessels. It is called in Greeke 
<term lang="gk">&#x0395;&#x03BB;&#x03BE;&#x03AF;&#x03BD;&#x03B7;</term> sayeth Fuchsius, of the rough seade therof, which wyl
hange on mens clothes, and <term lang="gk">&#x03C0;&#x03B5;&#x03C1;&#x03B4;&#x03B9;&#x03BA;&#x03AF;&#x03BF;&#x03B7; 
&#x03BA;&#x03B1;&#x03AF; &#x03C0;&#x03B5;&#x03C1;&#x03B4;&#x03B9;&#x03BA;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;&#x03C2;</term>, be&#173;
cause the byrde <term lang="la">Perdix</term> (whiche is englyshed the <term lang="la">Partriche</term>,)
deliteth to fede theron. It constreigneth or bindeth strong&#173;
ly, and scoureth awaye with colde moisture: and therfore
healeth whotte inflammations, from the begynnyng to the
state, and is also layde in <term lang="la">Cataplasmatibus ad Phygethlon</term> in the
begynning. The iuice therof dropped into the eares, hel&#173;
peth their phlegmonous paynes. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Muralium</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Vrceolaris. </term></note>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Passula. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">CAlled also <term lang="la">Vua passa</term>, and in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03B1;<expan type="">&#x03C3;&#x03C4;</expan>&#x03B1;&#x03C6;&#x03B9;&#x03C2;, &#x03AE; <expan type="">&#x03C3;&#x03C4;</expan>&#x03B1;&#x03C6;&#x03B9;&#x03C2;
&#x03AE;&#x03BC;&#x03B5;&#x03C1;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>, with vs <term lang="la">reysons</term>, ar grapes prepared by arte
as are fygges: and therfore (sayeth Galen) haue <term lang="la">Vu&#230;
pass&#230;</term>, the same affinitie with grapes, that <term lang="la">Caric&#230;</term> haue with
figges. Whiche beynge made of tame or tylled grapes,
haue concoctiue, adstringente, and meane digestiue facul&#173;
tie: but the wylde are vehement sharpe, and therfore doe
purge and scoure the head myghtyly. Grapes doe not 
exquisitly maturate in colde regions, muche lesse reysons:
and as they beyng swete, are the whotter, so are the sharpe
ones colder, whiche doe roborate the stomache, and con<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
stipate the belly, and that, the tarter the more: among the
whyche, the reysons that are of a meane swetnes, keepe a
meane constitution. In these as there is a moderatiue ver&#173;
tue, so haue they also a meane clensinge facultie: wherby
they put awaye the small gnawynges of the mouthe of
the stomache. If they be not adstryngent, they helpe not
the lyuer or <expan type="">the</expan> splene, but the affectes of the brest &#38;  lunges. 



</page>

<page no="84">
Of reysons they are beste that are fatte, and haue a tender
skynne: whiche if they be colde are made the better, by ta&#173;
kyng from them their graynes, when they haue been ste&#173;
ped in water. Though in Cilicia there doe growe reysons,
bothe full and small, without graynes. </xpln>

Staphis eme&#173;
ros</term>. </note>
<note type="m">Dried or pre&#173;
pared fygges. </note>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="phlegmon(n)">Phlegmone. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="phlegm(n)"><term lang="gk">&#x03A6;&#x03BB;&#x03B5;&#x03B3;&#x03BC;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;&#x1F74;</term>, <term lang="la">Id est inflammatio, uel collectio</term> <term lang="gk">&#x03B1;&#x03C0;&#x03BF;&#x03C4;&#x03BF;&#x03C5;&#x03C6;&#x03BB;&#x03B5;&#x03B3;&#x03BC;&#x03BF;&#x03C5;</term>, <term lang="la">hoc
est a sanguine dicta</term>, written moste comm<expan type="">on</expan>ly hithervnto (with
muche rudenes) <term lang="la">Flegmon</term>, is properly a symple tumore (as 
Galen sayeth) and an affecte of the fleshie partes, comming
of a greater fluxe of bloude then they nede, or can natural&#173;
ly susteyne. Whiche sweateth through the coates of the
veynes lyke a dewe: wherin they gather together to them
selues a tumore, with heate, rednes, stretching, resisting,
and pulsatiue or beating payne: whiche is propre to great
inflammations. And thus much of the simple tumore. For
when so euer it chaunceth to haue some portion of anye of
the other three humerall tumores adioyned therwith:
there resulteth a compounde name, suche as the mixture 
requireth, as choler therwith c<expan type="">on</expan>current, maketh the name 
<term lang="gk">&#x03C6;&#x03BB;&#x03B5;&#x03B3;&#x03BC;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;&#x1F74; &#x03B5;&#x03C1;&#x03C5;&#x03C3;&#x03B9;&#x03C0;&#x03B5;&#x03BB;&#x03B1;&#x03C4;&#x03CE;&#x03B4;&#x1F74;&#x03C2;</term>, <term lang="la">quasi inflammatio ignis <emend err="srcra">sacra</emend> seuig&#173;
nit&#230; rubedinis particeps</term>. Phlegme coupled therwith, nameth
it <term lang="gk">&#x03A6;&#x03BB;&#x03B5;&#x03B3;&#x03BC;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;&#x1F74; &#x03CC;&#x03B9;&#x03B4;&#x03B7;&#x03BC;&#x03B1;&#x03C4;&#x1F7C;&#x03B4;&#x03AE;&#x03C2;</term>, <term lang="la">id est inflammatio laxa, uel Oedma&#173;
tis particeps</term>: But melancholy therwith, is called <term lang="gk">&#x03A6;&#x03BB;&#x03B5;&#x03B3;&#x03BC;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;&#x1F74;
&#x03C3;&#x03BA;&#x03B9;&#x03C1;&#x03C1;&#x03CE;&#x03B4;&#x03B7;&#x03C2;</term>, in Latin <term lang="la">inflammatio dura, siue duritiei consors</term>, as of
the reste. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">&#x03A6;&#x03BB;&#x03B5;&#x03B3;&#x03BC;&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</term>, 
<term lang="la">enim sanguis
est. </term></note>

pr&#230;ter natur<expan type="">am</expan></term></note>

<form lang="en">Pilileporis. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">Seyng that heares (whose cause efficient as Galen wit&#173;



</page>

<page no="85">
nesseth, is of grosse and slymy superfluities) is colder &#38; drier
then any other parte of the bodie, yea then the bones: By
the mindes of Aristotle, C<expan type="">on</expan>stantinus, Galenus, and Albertus Mag&#173;

drie, and therfore restraining and bynding) whiche is of all

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Pini Cortex. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="pine tree(n)">THe rynde of the Pine tree, (called in Greke <term lang="gk">&#x03C0;&#x03AF;&#x03C4;&#x03C5;&#x03C2;</term>, and
in Latin <term lang="la">Pinus</term>,) brused and layde to, is profitable by the 
testimonie of Dioscorides, for chafinges and vlcers that are
ouer all the body: a<emend err="u">n</emend>d also for burnynges <term lang="la">cum Manna &#38; Spu&#173;
ma argenti</term>. Beyng vsed <term lang="la">cum Serat myrtino</term>, it healeth suche vl&#173;
cers of delicate persons as refuse sharpnes. Brused <term lang="la">cum 
atrimento sutorio</term>, it restrayneth crepyng vlcers: and <term lang="la">in suffitu, 
&#38; partus &#38; secundas eiicit</term>. The leaues of the same brused and
applied, doe mitigate and represse inflammati<expan type="">on</expan>s. They ease
with vineger the totheake: and a dragme weighte of the
same druncke in <term lang="la">Mulsa</term>, is profitable for m<expan type="">en</expan> diseased in the li&#173;
uer. The ryndes of the appuls or nuttes of the Pine tree, 
dooe in drinke as doe the leaues. Wherfore Lanfrancus, by
<term lang="la">Cortice pini</term>, must be vnderstanded to meane the rynde of the
tree, whose properties doe beste agree with his intente. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Pitys</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Lib. 1. </term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="pepper(n)">Piper. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">OF the Grecians called <term lang="gk">&#x03C0;&#x03AD;&#x03C0;&#x03B5;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;</term>, is of thre kyndes, be&#173;
side <term lang="la">Piper siluestre</term>, as <term lang="la">Longum</term>, <term lang="la">Album</term>, &#38;  <term lang="la">Nigrum</term>. Which



</page>

<page no="86">
all (sayth Galen) doe heate and drye, and are vsed (as in <term lang="la">Dic&#173;
trion peperon</term>,) to digeste rawe humores: and drunke <term lang="la">Cum 
Vino</term>, doe helpe the stomache cooled by colde alimentes. 
Black pepper is to be chosen, that is neyther sclender, rug&#173;
ged, nor hauing a grosse rynde: whiche twoo notes doe also
commende the whyte. The long pepper ought to be found,
without holes, and that neither by brusing nor steping wyl
be resolued, but will kepe still the nature of pepper. 

And where as the olde wryters (ledde thervnto, as it
should seme, rather by heresaye then experience,) Esteme
them al the frutes of one tree, gathered at seuerall seasons:
The opinion of the later wryters, for dyuerse considerati&#173;
ons thervnto repugning: I referre you to Matthiolus and 
to maister D. Turner, his worthy worke, wherin he hath
not only made hereof out of eche sorte large demonstra&#173;
tions, but also moste learnedly hath he explicated manye
doubtes: whiche vnknowne, myghte through ignorance
haue wrought many euels (as no doubte) long tyme here
tofore they haue done. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Peperi. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De symplici. 
lib. 8. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De sanitate
tuenda. lib. 4. </term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="pistacia(n)">Pistacia. </form>
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="gk">&#x03C9;&#x03B9;<expan type="">&#x03C3;&#x03C4;</expan>&#x03AC;&#x03BA;&#x03B9;&#x03B1; </term>, so named bothe in Greeke and Latine, and
vulgarly <term lang="la">Fistici</term>, are the frutes of a tree, growyng as sayeth

<form lang="la">Pisum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="bean(n)" lexeme="pease(n)"><term lang="gk">&#x03C0;&#x03AF;&#x03C3;&#x03BF;&#x039D;
</term> (sayeth Galen) doth in his whole substance resem&#173;
ble the beane, (if I may so englyshe <term lang="la">Faba</term>,) and are in lyke
maner taken. They are not so wyndy, and doe in al things
excelle beanes, saue that they haue not so muche scowring
power, and therfore doe tary longer in the belly. 

But to know how <term lang="la">Pisum</term>, (wherof neyther Dioscori&#173;
des nor Galen haue made description. ) may be our peason
or no: haue recourse as before to maister D. Turner. For
to repete that here, whiche an other hathe already so com&#173;
modiously sayde: would but make my booke vnnedefully
to swelle, and with knowne thynges fatigate the reader. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Pix. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="pitch(n)" lexeme="stone-pitch(n)">NAmed in Greke <term lang="gk">&#x03C0;&#x03AF;&#x03C3;&#x03C3;&#x03B1;</term>, is after Dioscorides of three
sortes <term lang="la">Liquida</term>, <term lang="la">Viscosa</term>, and <term lang="la">Arida</term>. <term lang="la">Pix liquida</term> (sayeth he,)
is gathered out of the fattest portions of the trees <term lang="la">Picea</term> and
<term lang="la">Pinus</term>, whiche of some was called <term lang="gk">&#x0394;&#x03AC;&#x03C2;</term>, in Latine <term lang="la">Teda</term>, affir&#173;
ming it to be a vice or Canker of the trees afore saide: con&#173;
foundyng the vanitie of some that haue counted <term lang="la">Teda</term> a tree
speciall. 

Hereof the beste is sincere lyghte, and shining, whiche
by boylynge waxeth thycke, and is called <term lang="gk">&#x03C0;&#x03B1;&#x03BB;&#x03AF;&#x03BC;&#x03C0;&#x03C3;&#x03C3;&#x03B1;</term>, <term lang="la">Id est
spissa pix uel Pix bis cocta</term>: wherof one parte waxeth clammy,
and is called <term lang="gk">&#x03B2;&#x03BF;&#x03C3;&#x03BA;&#x03AC;&#x03C2;</term>, <term lang="la">quasi pix Viscosa</term>, the other is drye, and 
named <term lang="gk">&#x039A;&#x03BF;&#x03BB;&#x03BF;&#x03C6;&#x03C9;&#x03BD;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;</term>, (of the citie Colophon of Libia or Gr&#230;cia,
from whence it was wonte to be brought,) In Latine <term lang="la">Pix
Arida</term>, and commonly <term lang="la">Pix Gr&#230;ca</term>, whiche oughte to bee pure,
fatte, odoriferous, resinous, of reddyshe coloure, and hea&#173;
teth and drieth, by the sentence of Galen in the seconde de&#173;
gree: but doth more drie then heate. The liquid contrarily
doth more heate then drie, hauynge also subtill partes, 



</page>

<page no="88">
and therfore helpeth asthmatike persons. They haue ab&#173;
stersiue concoctiue, and digestiue powre, as in taste a lyght
sharpnes and bitternes: so beyng mixed with wexe, they
take away leprosy nayles, and scoure <term lang="la">Lichenas</term>. They also
beyng put in <term lang="la">Cataplasmatis</term>, doe concocte harde tumores: and
to all these is <term lang="la">Pix liquida</term> the better, and filleth vp the holow&#173;
nes of vlcers: but the drye is more apte to glewe together
woundes. 

But Peter Martyr, in his Decades of the Spanyardes
their voyages and gestes in the west Indies, sheweth of a 
harder and stronger kynde of pyche: (and therefore better
for shippes,) flowyng out of a roche in Hispaniola. Whiche (or
the lyke thynge) myght be the cause of the name in that
whiche we commonly call <term lang="la">stone pyche</term>. In the whiche Isle
sayeth he, there is also pyche made of twoo seuerall trees:
the one is the Pyne tree, (commonly knowne,) the other is
called in that countrey language <term lang="un">Copeid.</term> The leaues wher&#173;
of is a spanne broade, almoste rounde, thicker then double
parchment, merueylous tough, and is apte to be wrytten
on with wyers of metall or woode, and that on both sydes. 
And whyle the leafe is fresh and newe, it shelueth the let&#173;
ters whyte vpon grene: but when it is olde and drye, it be&#173;
commeth harde and whyte, like writing tables of woodde,
and the letters yelowe: for it wyll neuer lose them, by wa&#173;
shyng or any other wayes, saue only by fyre. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Lib 3. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Pissa. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Picea.</term> <term lang="la">Pinus</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Das</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Palim pissa</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Boscas</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Colophonia</term>. </note><note type="m"><term lang="la">Pix arida. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De sym. lib. 8. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Copeia. </term></note>
</wordentry>

<form lang="la">Plaga. </form>

<form lang="la">Plantago. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="plantain(n)">IS of the Grecians called <term lang="gk">&#x0391;&#x03C1;&#x03BD;&#x03CC;&#x03B3;&#x03BB;&#x03C9;&#x03C3;&#x03C3;&#x03BF;&#x03B7;</term>, <term lang="la">quasi agnina lingua</term>,
and is (though Dioscorides mencion but two,) of dyuers
kyndes, as <term lang="la">Maior</term>, <term lang="la">media</term>, &#38; <term lang="la">minor</term>, <term lang="la">Aquatica</term> also and <term lang="la">Marina</term>:
Wherof <term lang="la">Plantago maior</term>, whiche Lanfranke meaneth, bothe
cooleth and dryeth in the seconde excesse, and hathe some
sowernes by reason of hir earthly partes. The medicines
that are both colde and adstryngente, are mete for rebelli&#173;
ous vlcers, rottenes, and fluxions, and therfore for <term lang="la">Dysente&#173;
riis</term>: for they staye the flowynges of bloude, and coole things
burned. They heale woundes, both new and old, and glew
together their holownes. But of all other suche medicines
planteyne is chiefe, or at the least seconde to none, by rea&#173;
son of his meane and conuenient temperamente, for it is 
drye without bitynge, and colde without stupefaction. The
seade and the rootes are of lyke faculties, sauyng that they
are dryer, but not so colde. The seade is subtyll &#38;  the roote
grosse. The leaues also dryed are subtill, and of lesse coo&#173;
lyng. The rootes are vsed for paynes of the teethe. The
leaues also (but muche more the seades) are geuen for the
obstructions of the lyuer and splene. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Arnoglossos</term> 
<term lang="la">uel</term> <term lang="la">arnogloss<expan type="">on</expan></term></note>
<note type="m">Galenus
<term lang="la">De symp. me. 
facult. lib. 6. </term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Plumbum vstum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">LEade is called in Greke <term lang="gk">&#x039C;&#x03CC;&#x03BB;&#x03C5;&#x03B2;&#x03B4;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>, and is of coolyng
facultie, hauing moreouer, not only much moiste sub&#173;

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Porus sarcodes. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">THis Greeke worde <term lang="gk">&#x03A0;&#x03CC;&#x03C1;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>, permitteth dyuers inter&#173;
pretations, and is taken for a waye, a passage, a tracte,



</page>

<page no="90">
a iorney, a caue, a bosome, a couered hole, a vessell, (and <term lang="gk">&#x03C0;&#x03CC;&#173;
&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03B9;</term> plurally are small sweatyng holes,) for lucre also, som&#173;
tyme for money, and lastly, (which is nyghest our purpose)
it is a stony hardnes. And <term lang="la">Poros</term>,
(for <term lang="la">Tophus</term> is also and that more proprely a stone: whiche
Vergill in his Georgikes termeth <term lang="gk">Tophus Scaber</term>. ) Wherof
Galen thus sayeth. When came phlegmatike humores, by
liyng long hydde in the lymmes or ioyntes, doe becomme
grosser and more viscous: ther are of th<expan type="">em</expan> ingendred <term lang="la">Tophi</term>,
and <term lang="la">Calli</term>. Wherfore this substance repletiue, the worke of 
nature, of the grosse nurishment of the bone (commyng as
a lygature betwene the endes of the broken bones, of sub&#173;
stance harde, but not britle) may well be called <term lang="gk">&#x03C0;&#x03CC;&#x03C1;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan> &#x03C3;&#x03B1;&#x03C1;&#173;
&#x03BA;&#x03CE;&#x03B4;&#x03B7;&#x03C2;</term>, <term lang="la">quasi duritres Carnosus</term>, as it is of Galen called <term lang="la">Callus</term>. 
Whiche also somtyme sygnifieth the circulare or quilly
hardnes of fistules, and is comm<expan type="">on</expan>ly taken for the hardnes
both of the palmes of the handes, and soles of the fete, in&#173;
gendred by labore or exercise. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Poros</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Tophus</term>,
A stone which
is apte to be
resolued into sande. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De com. med. 
secun. loc. li. 10</term></note>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="portulaca(n)">Portulaca. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="la">POrtulaca</term>, called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03B1;&#x03BD;&#x03B4;&#x03C1;&#x03B1;&#x03C7;&#x03BD;&#x1F04;</term>, is of two kindes. 
<term lang="la">Hortensis</term> (which Lanfranke calleth <term lang="la">Domestica</term>), and <term lang="la">Syl&#173;
uestris</term>: and is after Galen, of a colde &#38;  watery tempe&#173;
ramente, and coolyng in the thyrde degree, and moystynge
in the seconde: hauynge also some tartnes, and therfore
stayeth whotte and bilious fluxions, and by a certeyn clam&#173;
mynes that it hath without bytynge, it easeth the teethe
when they are on edge. It helpeth greatly the burnyng
heates of the belly, layde to the waste or the mouthe of the 
stomache, and that in hectike feuers. And (because it is re&#173;
stryngente) is profitably geuen to <term lang="la">Dysentericis</term>, <term lang="la">muliebri pro&#173;
fluuio</term>, and to reiections of bloude: but to these the iuice is 
muche more efficatious then the herbe. 

An other herbe there is also, called <term lang="la">Portulaca marina</term>, only 
of the likenes that the leaues therof haue with porcelane,



</page>

<page no="91">
whiche yet (in other pointes vnlyke) is not to be numbred
with these. Neyther ought I here to ouerpasse, that the 
thyrde lytle kynde of <term lang="la">Sedum</term>, called <term lang="la">Illecebra</term> is of some Gre&#173;
cians named <term lang="gk">&#x1F04;&#x03BD;&#x03B4;&#x03C1;&#x03AC;&#x03C7;&#x03BD;&#x03B7; &#x03AC;&#x03B3;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;&#x1F04;</term></xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Andrachne</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De symp. li. 6. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Illecebra</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Andrachne. </term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Agria</term>. </note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Pruna. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">AVicenna. <term lang="la">lib. 4. Fen. 3. tract. 1.</term> describeth <term lang="la">Prunam</term>, and <term lang="la">Ignem
persicum</term>, bothe in one chapiter, not without some
confusion of the one with the other, and bothe with 
<term lang="la">Formica</term>. First estemyng both the names, to serue for euery
blysteryng, corrosiue, and eschare makynge pustule. And a
little after he sayeth, that <term lang="la">Pruna</term> is that, whiche maketh the
place blacke as a cole, without moisture, hauynge a small
emmence lyke a lupine, somtyme with a pustule and som&#173;
tyme not: hauyng itche ouer all. And somtyme dothe <term lang="la">Ignis
persicus</term> or <term lang="la">Pruna</term>, more larglye blyster, and purge suche a
quitture, as a place burned or cautrized doth. The place be&#173;
ing ashe coloured, blacke, or leady, and compassed aboute
with a vehement inflammation without perfecte rednes. 
And agayne, <term lang="la">Ignis persicus</term> is the more sharpe, and of the
swyfter apparition, and motion: <term lang="la">Pruna</term> the slower, and lying
deper. The beginnyng of both is of burned choler myxed
with melancholie, (and therof commeth the blacke exchare
in both. ) <term lang="la">Ignis persicus</term> consisting of the vehementer choler,
and <term lang="la">Pruna</term> of the myghtier melancholye. If they chaunce in
the fleshe, they are the soner resolued: but if in the sinewes,
they sticke the faster, and resolue the slowlyer. Thus farre
Auicen. and yet confessyng after, that the name of eyther,
may be geuen to both: and affirmeth them often to come of
a pestilentiall feuer. 

Nowe <term lang="la">Pruna</term> and <term lang="la">Carbunculus</term>, in the qualitie of names as
you see differ not: and howe muche their efficient causes,
signes, and propreties doe varie: After these descriptions
therof, by comparing them together, it is easy to see. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Ignis persicus. </term></note></wordentry>



</page>

<page no="92">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Prunum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="plum tree(n)" lexeme="damson tree(n)" lexeme="prune(n)">THe plumme tree or <term lang="la">Damsen tree</term>, called in Greeke,
<term lang="gk">&#x03BA;&#x03BF;&#x03BA;&#x03BA;&#x03CD;&#x03BC;&#x03B7;&#x03BB;&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</term>, and <term lang="la">Prunus</term> is of many kyndes, both tame
and wilde: wherof maister Turner hath egregiously
wrytten. The prune (called <term lang="gk">&#x03BA;&#x03BF;&#x03BA;&#x03BA;&#x03CD;&#x03BC;&#x03B7;&#x03BB;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>,) is a fructe sayeth 
Galen, of small nuryshment, but doth meanly moyste and
coole the belly, and by his moisture and slimines, subdueth
the same, and that the better if it bee boyled <term lang="la">cum Melicrato</term>:
But this doe the newe moyst ones, more then the olde and
drye. The damascen prunes also, (which are acounted best)
lesse then the Spanysh prunes, whiche are praysed nexte. 
The best prunes are great &#38; lose, hauing some adstriction. 

The wylde prunes or plummes, are euidently adstrin&#173;
gent, and therfore doe staye the belly, and are conuenient,
for the inflammations of <term lang="la">Columella</term>, and other partes of the
throte. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="gkt">Pyrum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="pear(n)">PEares are called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03AC;&#x03C0;&#x03B9;&#x03B1;</term>, (as the tree is called

dried, and after boyled, are vsed in wynter for meate.

therfore doe they glewe together woundes. Which to doe

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Quartana febris. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="quartan(n)" lexeme="tertian(n)" lexeme="quotidian(n)">THe quartane feuer, is either interpolate, and named
<term lang="la">Quartana intermittens, uel exquisita</term>, or continuall, &#38;  therof
called <term lang="la">Q<emend err="n">u</emend>artana continens</term>, or <term lang="la">continua</term>. The first is ingen&#173;



</page>

<page no="93">
dred of a melancholike humore putrefied, or a splenatyke
affecte, whose rigor is in the begynning not vehement, but
lyke the colde that men commonly fele in moste vehement
frostes. Whiche yet in processe of tyme augmenteth euen
to the state, with colde so vehement, as if the bones would
breake, but not pungitiue as in the tertian. The pulse is
slowe and rare: The augmentation, vigor, and mouyng of
of heate, are contrary to the tertian. The humore kyndleth
slowly lyke to a stone, or other lyke colde and drye thynge,
whiche is the cause of so muche longer intermission therin,
then in the Quotidiane. 

<term lang="la">Quartana continua</term> (whiche is rarly sene) is ingendred of me&#173;
lancholy, putrefied in the vesselles, euen as is <term lang="la">Q<emend err="n">u</emend>otidiana con&#173;
tinens</term>, or <term lang="la">continua</term>, caused of phlegme in like maner putrefied
wherin they both differ from the lyke named intermittent
feuers, and in that they are neuer without a feuerous ha&#173;
bite, vntyl they cease for all: though yet they haue some re&#173;
mission betwene euery fitte, and therin differ they from
the feuers <term lang="la">Synochis</term>, wherin there is no remission but conti&#173;
nuall burnyng. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="realgar(n)">Realgar. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">A Most vehement corrosiue medicine, and is made (as
is sayde <term lang="la">In opere pandactarum</term>) <term lang="la">ex sulphure, Calce uiua, &#38;  au&#173;
ripigmento</term>: whiche mortifieth fistules, &#38;  corrodeth de&#173;
plie. And is also called <term lang="la">Soricoria</term>, because it killeth rattes, as it
doth also the wormes of horses. 

But Brassauolus <term lang="la">in examine suo symplicium</term>, sayeth, that they
are deceiued that thinke the true <term lang="la">Risagallum</term> (for so is it more
fitly named) a thing artificiall, for it is founde sayeth he, in 
the same myne with <term lang="la">Auripigmento</term>, and is of the same kynde:
beyng in dede a medicine hurtfull and venemous. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">

natiues, as in restoring or renewing of flesh, bone. &#38;c. 
and commeth of <term lang="la">Regigno</term>, to regender or brede againe. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">

ryseth of <term lang="la">Repercutio</term> or <term lang="la">Repello</term>, to constreygne or dryue

<page no="95">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="resin(n)">Resina. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="rosin(n)"><term lang="la">REsina</term>, whiche the Grecians call <term lang="gk">&#x03C1;&#x03B7;&#x03C4;&#x03AF;&#x03BD;&#x03B7;</term>, signifieth the
oyly teares of trees, whiche wyll burne with fyre,
and easily be commixed with oyle, and moste hardly
with water. As <term lang="la">Gummi</term> signifieth the watery teares of the
same: for in the fyre it wyll crackell, and wyll in water be
easily disolued, but not so in oyle. 

Resines doe al heate and drye. Wherof <term lang="la">Resina terebinthina</term>,
is of Dioscorides called the beste: whiche also Galen appro&#173;
ueth, begynnyng with <term lang="la">Resina lentiscina</term>, and geueth to it the
firste place, saiyng. Besyde the litle adstriction that is ther&#173;
in, (wherby it is so commodious to the weaknes and in&#173;
flammations of the stomache, the liuer, and the belly,) it al&#173;
so dryeth without bytinge: for it is verye subtyle without
sharpnes. Of <term lang="la">Terebinthin&#230; resin&#230;</term> he sayeth, that it is prefer&#173;
red to the reste: hauyng a manifest adstriction, but not lyke 
to mastyke. Howe be it, it hath adioyned a certeyne bitter&#173;
nes, and therfore digesteth more then mastike, &#38;c. of dy&#173;
uers other. But this worde <term lang="la">Resina</term>, so written alone, is al&#173;
wayes taken for our common Rosin. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Rheti<damage>n</damage>e</term>. </note>

<page no="96">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la" lexeme="rigor(n)">Rigor. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="gk">&#x03A1;&#x03AF;&#x03B3;&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</term> is that swifte and frequent motion, (contrary to
<term lang="la">Palpitationi</term>,) whose motion is litle, softe, &#38;  rare, whiche Ga&#173;
len calleth compounde, as of a cause against nature and of 
a facultie animall (as of the vertue expulsiue) procurynge a 
sensible coldnes, and an vnequall and inuoluntary mouing
of the whole body, wherin it differeth from <term lang="la">Horore</term> (whiche 
is an vniuersal affecte of the skynne only,) and from <term lang="la">Tremore</term>
whiche possesseth somme one member, and may by the wil
be resisted. 

But that rigor that is wonte to come on sicke persons, 
and called <term lang="la">Rigor Morbosus</term>, is by the opinion of Hippocrates, a
certeyne peynfull coldnes, with an vnequall mouinge or
shakyng of all the body. This may dyuersly chaunce, euen
to whole bodyes as of heate, or of colde, or of a viscouse hu&#173;
more, (to the first coolyng is a remedy, to the second heate,
to the thirde concoction or euacuation, or both. ) Somtyme
of the heryng or seyng of horryble and fearfull syghtes, or 
noyses, phlegme also bredde without a feuer by idlenes, ill
order of diete, and entryng the bathe after dynner: to the
olde auncientes vnknowne. And though it be euident, it to 
come of a natiue heate, yet commeth it also dyuersly, as 
well of colde viscous phlegme as of whotte and sharpe hu&#173;
mores, and sometyme of a iuyce myxed of heate and colde,
as in quartanes, but in mixed compounde feuers, the rigor
is neuer vehement. It to chaunce to a weake body, in a c<expan type="">on</expan>&#173;
tinuall feuer is an euyl signe: and also when it wyl not at
all, or hardly be put away. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Rhigos. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Palpitatio. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De sympto. 
causis. lib. 2. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De palpita&#173;
tione tr<expan type="">em</expan>. c<expan type="">om</expan>. 
&#38; rigore. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Horor. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Tremor. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De Crisibus. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De differ<expan type="">en</expan>tiis
febrium. lib. 2. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De morborum

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rose(n)">Rosa. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="rose(n)">IS named in greke of the pleasant sauour therof <term lang="gk">&#x03A1;&#x03BF;&#x03B4;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>
<term lang="la">quasi bene olens</term>, &#38; is deuyded into kyndes diuers, wherof
the auncientes haue lefte no memory. The powre of roses



</page>

<page no="97">
consisteth sayeth Galen of a whotte &#38; a watery substance
together: And also of two other qualities, as bitternes and
byndyng. The flower wherof is more adstringent then it
selfe is, and therfore desiccatyue. Mesues appoynteth them
the faculties of coolyng in the first degree, and driyng in
the seconde. The iuyce of Roses sayth he, whiche is seue&#173;
red from the earthy substance therof, heateth nyghe to the
firste degree. It openeth, mundifieth, resolueth: and pur&#173;
geth gently by experience (sayeth Syluius,) both choler and
water, though the olde wryters saye not so muche. Lyke 
properties to these hath <term lang="la">Syrupus</term> and <term lang="la">Vnguentum rosarum</term>. 
They strengthen the harte, the stomache, the lyuer, and
the retentyue facultie. They are good in bilious feuers, to
coole inflammations, to mitigate the paynes made by th<expan type="">em</expan>,
and prouoke sleape. They moue sternutati<expan type="">on</expan>s, and are hurt&#173;
full for catarrhous persons: but they bynde and strengthen
the <term lang="la">Vuula</term> and the throte, and take awaye surfetyng. The
whyte roses doe purge little or nothyng, but doe bynde and
strengthen more then the redde. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Rhodon</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De sym. lib. 8. </term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Rumex acutus. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="rumex(n)">CAlled in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x1F44;&#x03BE;&#x03C5;&#x03BB;&#x03AC;&#x03C0;&#x03B1;&#x03B8;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, is one of the kyndes of <term lang="gk">&#x03BB;&#x03B1;&#x03C0;&#x03B1;&#x03B8;&#x03BF;&#x03C5;</term>: so called <term lang="gk">&#x03B1;&#x03C0;&#x03BF; &#x03C4;&#x03BF;&#x03C5; &#x03BB;&#x03B1;&#x03C0;&#x03AC;&#x03B6;&#x03C9;</term>, <term lang="la">quod uacuat uentrem</term>,
because the leaues of eche kynde therof doe lose and
emptie the belly. The kyndes of <term lang="la">Rumex</term> are foure, <term lang="la">Rumex
acutus</term>, wherof here. <term lang="la">Rumex satiuus</term>, called for his greatnes
<term lang="gk">&#x03B9;&#x03B7;&#x03C0;&#x03BF;&#x03BB;&#x03AC;&#x03C0;&#x03B1;&#x03B8;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, &#38;  of other fondly <term lang="la">Rhabarbarum</term>. <term lang="la">Rumex syluestris</term>, 
called also <term lang="gk">&#x03B1;&#x03BD;&#x03B1;&#x03BE;&#x03C5;&#x03C1;&#x1F76;&#x03C2;</term>, &#38; <term lang="gk">&#x1F30;&#x03BE;&#x03B1;&#x03BB;&#x03B9;&#x03C2;</term>, wherof afore. The fourth hath 
leaues lyke planten. About the which kyndes, because ther
is some controuersie rysen, in that Aetius describeth his <term lang="la">Oxi&#173;
lapathon</term>, otherwyse then Dioscorides doth, appoynting his
sharpnes to consiste rather in taste then in the harde and
sharpe substance of the leafes pointe: and that to speake of
it here so largly as D. Turner hath done, would vnnecessa&#173;
rily augmente this briefe worke: I sendeth thyther. 



</page>

<page no="98">
Of <term lang="la">Rumex acutus</term>, Galen saith, that it doth (of a mixed tem<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
perament) both digeste &#38; repercusse: The seade wherof is of
so manifest adstriction, that it staieth <expan type="">the</expan> <term lang="la">Dysenteria</term>, &#38;  the fluxe
of the wombe. The rusticall women, (lystyng when they
are with chylde) and gredy boyes, doe vse nowe and then
to eate it rawe: whiche nurysheth but litle more then <term lang="la">La&#173;
patho</term>. But that the roote of <term lang="la">Lapathi acuti</term> doth maturate, I
fynde not in any other autentike author: and therfore is 
Lanfr<expan type="">an</expan>kes experience only to be trusted vnto. Or els per&#173;
haps he myght meane some other herbe: but what, it is 
doubtfull. They prayse it in the healyng and dryuing away
of dyuers apostmes: Whiche to doe it semeth of them ra&#173;
ther to be gathered by deuydinge, then by maturation. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Oxylapathon. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Lapathon. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Hippolapath<expan type="">on</expan></term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Anaxyris</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Oxalis</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De symp. me. 
fac. li. 7. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De alimentis. 
lib. 2. </term></note>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="rue(n)">Ruta. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">RVe is called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03A0;&#x03B5;&#x03B3;&#x03B1;&#x03BD;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, because by the
great heate and drynes therof, it coagulateth sede,
for <term lang="gk">&#x03C0;&#x1F21;&#x03B3;&#x03BD;&#x03C5;&#x03B4;&#x03B1;&#x03B5;</term> (<term lang="la">id est coagulare, uel ut glaciem c<expan type="">on</expan>trahere</term>) sig&#173;
nifieth to congeyle or coagulate, <term lang="la">Teste Plutarcho</term>. It is of two
kyndes, <term lang="la">Hortensis</term> &#38; <term lang="la">syluestris</term>: wherof the one is as rare, as
the other is common. It is in taste both sharpe and bitter,
kylleth wormes, and is of temperamente whotte in the
thyrde degree. The wylde Rue in the fourthe, and dryeth
myghtely: and therfore is of power to deuide and cut inson<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
der grosse and tough humores, and to moue vryne. And
is good for paynes of the ioyntes, and the dropsie. 

The iuyce therof heated in a pomegranate rynde, is put in
to the eares to ease their peynes: It also sharpeneth the
eye sighte. Eaten rawe or condite with salt, &#38; layde to <term lang="la">cum 
melle &#38;  succo foeniculi</term>, it putteth away their dimnes: and mit&#173;
tigateth their peynes, beyng layde to <term lang="la">cum Polenta</term>. It is mor&#173;
ouer of subtil partes, and destroyeth wyndes: and therfore
pursueth strongly the inflammations of Colon, and other
interior partes, &#38;  restreyneth venereous appetites: beside
the incomparable vertue that it hath against poyson, and
all venemous woundes. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Peganon. </term></note>
<note type="m">Dioscor.  <term lang="la">lib. 3. </term></note>
<note type="m">Galen.  <term lang="la">de sym. 
lib. 8. </term></note></wordentry>
</wordgroup1>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Sabina. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="savin(n)">SAuyn is that continuall shrubbe, that is cal&#173;
led in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03B2;&#x03C1;&#x03AC;&#x03B8;&#x03C5;</term> <term lang="la">seu</term> <term lang="gk">&#x03B2;&#x03C1;&#x03AC;&#x03B8;&#x03C5;&#x03C2;</term>. And therof are
there two kindes after Dioscorides. The one with
leaues lyke to <term lang="la">Cupresso</term>, to vs well knowne: The
other resembleth in forme, the leaues of the Tamariske
tree, and is here rare. 

It heateth (by the sentence of Galen) and dryeth in the
seconde degree, Consisting of moste subtill partes, of taste
lyke the Cipers tree, but that it hath more sharpnes, and
lesse adstriction: And therfore is whotter, and dothe more
myghtily digeste. It stayeth spreadyng or eatynge sores, a&#173;
swageth inflammations, and breaketh Carbuncles: But
for his great heate and drynes, is vnmete to heale wo<expan type="">un</expan>des. 
Being drunke with wyne: <term lang="la">Menses, Vrinasque prouocat</term> by his
subtill substance, and dryueth out euen bloud by the vrine. 
<term lang="la">Foctum etiam uiuentem interficit, &#38; mortuum educit. </term></xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gkt">Brathy</term>. <term lang="la">uel</term> 
<term lang="gkt">Brathys. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De symplici. 
lib. 6. </term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Saccharum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="sugar(n)" lexeme="sugar-candy(n)"><term lang="la">SAccharum</term>, is called of the moste auncient Archigenes, and
other olde wryters <term lang="la">Sal Indicus</term>, and <term lang="la">Mel arundineum.</term> 

Dioscorides, <term lang="la">lib. 2. cap. 74.</term> Hauing spoken of dyuers
kyndes of hony: sayeth. There is also an other kynde of c<expan type="">on</expan>&#173;
creted or hardened hony, which they call <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03AC;&#x03BA;&#x03C7;&#x03B1;&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, founde
in <term lang="la">India &#38;  Arabia felici</term>, in the redes, coacted or fastened like
salte, and breaketh betwene the teethe as salte doth. It is
mete for the belly, and profitable to the stomache, and hel&#173;
peth the griefes of the reynes &#38; bladder: being layde to, it
putteth away <expan type="">the</expan> causes of darknes or dimnes of <expan type="">the</expan> eye sight. 

Galen in his . vii. boke of the faculties of simple medicins,
in the chapter of <term lang="la">Mel</term>, sayth. But <term lang="la">Saccharon</term> whiche is brought
from India and Arabia Felice, groweth together or becom&#173;
meth harde on redes, and is a kynde of hony: not so swete
as ours, but of like vertues, in scouryng &#38; digesting. And 
in that it is no enemy to the stomache as is ours, nor cau&#173;
seth thirste: so muche differeth it fr<expan type="">om</expan> our hony in substance. 



</page>

<page no="100">
Plinie sayeth, it is hony gathered together as bygge as
hasell nuttes, whyte lyke gummes, of great vse in medi&#173;
cine. 

Alexander Aphrodisaus sayeth, that whiche the Indians cal
<term lang="la">Saccharum</term>, is the curdes or heauy partes of hony, the sonne
thyckenyng or hardenyng the dewes, and conuertyng th<expan type="">em</expan> 
into the swetnes of hony. By these and lyke authorities,
doth Fuchsius seme very learnedly to proue our sugar not to 
be <term lang="la">Saccharum Candum</term>, or <term lang="la">Salem Indum</term> of the auncientes: affir&#173;
ming our sugar to be none other, but the iuyce of a plante
of a proper kynde, well brused and pressed out, (and is then
of colour, rather blacke or reddyshe, then whyte,) and by
heate of the fyre, in boyling and purifiyng or scumming, it
hardeneth and becommeth sugar: and the more it is boy&#173;
led and purified, the whyter it is. Ioannes Manardus sayeth he,
a man of most sharpe iudgement. <term lang="la">Lib. 2. Epistolarum suarum
medicinalium</term> wryteth, that by the Portugales and Sicilians
he knewe the plante, from the which sugar is pressed: and
sayeth that it is sowed of seade in moyste places, and is out&#173;
wardly lyke a great rede, but inwardly moste vnlyke: as
full of pulpe, heauy, softe, and ful of iuyce. A rede contrary&#173;
wyse is harde, lyght and empty. 

Nowe seyng that the sugar of the auncientes, came of a 
naturall dewe sayeth he, and the mater of hony, fyrst made
glewye by the colde of the nyghte, and after harde by the
heate of the sunne: whyte by nature, and breakynge be&#173;
twene the tethe lyke salte, (Ours being softe, and may as
hony be swalowed, tyll by fyre it be hardened. ) That han&#173;
gynge vpon redes, and after Galen, shoulde not moue 
thyrste so muche as hony, whiche ours doth: It can not be 
oure sugar. 

But contrary to this are the myndes of Brasauolus and
Matthiolus, who denye <term lang="la">Saccharum</term>, of the auncientes to be any
kynde of hony, or to come of the ayre: because what part of 
the ayre so euer (saye they) doth coagulate by colde, the same
wyll by the heate of the sunne not harden, but euaporate



</page>

<page no="101">
or vanyshe awaye, as wyll <term lang="la">Manna recentium</term>: whiche neuer
waxeth so harde, that it wyl breake betwene the teethe like
salte, but wyll rather be chawed as it were wexe. 

Who also iudge that plante (about whiche Fuchsius 
thus argueth) to haue been of his exterior forme called <term lang="la">Ha&#173;
rundo</term>, though it be not holowe or emptie as a rede, &#38;  doe cal
it <term lang="la">Harundin<expan type="">em</expan> Sacchariferam</term>: confessyng yet with Fuchsius, that
our sugar is <term lang="la">Factitium quoddam, non naturale</term>, and of the same
iuyce made perfecte by boylyng, as before. But <term lang="la">Sal Indus</term> (saye 
they) is of the same substance, and for very fulnes issueth or
sweateth out, at certeine ryftes of the same, and chiefly a&#173;
bout the ioyntes or knobbes: where (saye they) it is by the
heate of the sunne hardened and whytened. 

Claymyng also herin Plinies former authoritie to leane
with them, in that he lykeneth it to gummes: Noting also 
that the auncientes did neuer saye, that their <term lang="la">Saccharon</term> dyd
fasten or growe together on the leaues, as dewes are most
apte to doe. 

Brasauolus also semeth to proue, that the makynge of su&#173;
gar of the iuyce of Canes, and of the rootes of the same,
was in vse before the tymes Galen and Dioscorides, by the
testimonies Marcus Varro, Statius Papinus, and Strabo: who ly&#173;
ued al before Galen. But Matthiolus alloweth not the swete
pressed out liquores, whiche they mention, to be of necessi&#173;
tie <term lang="la">Saccharon</term>: because the INdians pressed out suche iuyces
from the rootes of dyuers plantes and trees, as well as of
Canes. Neyther yet (though it be called <term lang="la">Saccharum Tabarzeth</term> 
or <term lang="la">Saccharum Candum</term>) is it our sugar Candy: whiche is not
brought from India. But the Venetians immitating na&#173;
ture as muche as may bee, doe make it, by boylynge oure
whyte sugar foure or fyue tymes, puttinge redes therin,
wheron it may hange lyke lumpes: And wrappe it in cot&#173;
ten, as though it came from the easterly regions. Neyther
knowe I any thyng at this daye extant with vs, that may
so well by lykened to <term lang="la">Saccharum Arundineum</term>, as may the why&#173;
test sugar Candy, that we haue amongest vs: for the other



</page>

<page no="102">
is also not naturall, but artificiall: nor white but yelowysh. 
And that iudge I Lanfranke to meane by <term lang="la">Saccharum rubrum</term>. 

Loe, thus doe we se, how this our infortunate and mise&#173;
rable age, is not only bereft of this &#38;  many other Iewels
and commodities: but also (whiche worse is) so drowned in
obliuion of the same, that what they are or weare, we are
either vtterly ignorant or very vncerteine. 
</xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Sal Indicus</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Mel arundine<expan type="">um</expan>. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Saccharon</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Saccharum</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Tabarzeth. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Candum. uel
Candidum. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Harundo Sac&#173;
charifera. </term></note>
