"Saccharum" has a different sense in OED, a form of honey. 
</lemenote>
</wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="sal(n)">Sal. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="salt(n)" lexeme="opium(n)">SAlt, generally called <term lang="la">Sal</term>, (wherof there are dyuers 
kindes,) is also in Greke in lyke maner called <term lang="gk">&#x1F05;&#x03BB;&#x03B5;&#x03C2;</term>, and 
is made partly by arte, of sea water in the bayes, dryed
through the heate of the sunne, and called <term lang="la">Marinus</term>: and of
certeyne salt lakes by boylyng (whiche is called <term lang="la">Lacustris siue
Stagneus</term>: and partly natural, digged out of the earth, and
is therfore called <term lang="la">Fossilis</term>, whiche doe both alyke drye migh&#173;
tyly, but differ in this. That <term lang="la">Sal Fossilis</term> is of substance more
compacte, grosse, and more adstringent: and beynge put in
water, wyll not disolue as the other two sortes wyll. They
haue also an obscure adstriction, wherby they thycken and
bynde suche fleshe as is salted therwith: for it wasteth a&#173;
way moyste substances, and byndeth the solid partes. For
the whiche <term lang="la">Sal Fossilis</term> is best: next good to it is <term lang="la">Sal Marinus</term>. 
The thyrd rome holdeth <expan type="">the</expan> salt that is made in the welles,
&#38; salte pooles. Of all the whiche, we haue only the vse of <term lang="la">Sa&#173;
lis Marini</term> &#38; <term lang="la">Lacustris</term>. Howbeit, in some partes of Calabria &#38;
Germania, and in all Pannonia, <term lang="la">Sal Fossilius</term> is their vsuall salte. 
For the whiche (sayeth Brasauolus) they digge &#38; vndermyne
certeyne hilles, of two myles in lengthe, estemyng that for 
beste whiche is whyte, salt, and shyning: but that whiche
is moste bright and glistering like to Crystall, doe the A&#173;
pothecaries (folowing the Arabians) therfore (or els for the 
rarenes of the same) call <term lang="la">Salem Gemin&#230;</term>. Whiche for a tru<emend err="">t</emend>he 
Manardus doth auouche: affirming him selfe to haue sene in 
Pannonia dyuers lumpes or cloddes of digged salte, shynynge
so bryght, that suche as sawe it, thought it to haue ben very
Christall. This sayeth Matthiolus, beyng cast into the fyre,
neither cracketh nor leapeth out, as the other sortes wyll:



</page>

<page no="103">
but gloweth as Iron therin. 

Salte sayeth Dioscorides, dothe bynde, scowre awaye,
purge gentlye, scatter abroade, represse, and extenuate. 
But in that, one more th<expan type="">en</expan> an other as before. It defendeth
from putrefaction, and is put in medicynes that mundyfie
scabbes. It represseth <expan type="">the</expan> excrescentes of the eies, &#38;  c<expan type="">on</expan>sumeth
the webbes in the same, and taketh awaye other fleshye
eminences. It put in Clysters, loseth toughe and glasye
phlegme from the belly. Outwardly applyed, it putteth
away werynes, helpeth hydropicall tumores, and in fo&#173;
mentations dothe mitigate paynes: <term lang="la">Cum oleo &#38;  aceto</term>, it sta&#173;
yeth itche, &#38;  easeth the quincie, <term lang="la">cum melle, aceto, &#38;  oleo</term>. It is 
drunke <term lang="la">cum Oximeli</term>, after <term lang="la">Opium</term> and musheromes, to resiste
strangling: and is profitable to all poysoned woundes, and
venemous hurtes. 

Howbeit, <term lang="la">Sal</term> by Mesue, is deuided into foure principall
or notable kindes, as 1. <term lang="la">Sal panis</term>, wherin are c<expan type="">on</expan>teined al the
former sores, as <term lang="la">Marinus</term>, <term lang="la">Stagneus</term>, (eche of the which is cal&#173;
led <term lang="la">Factitius</term>) and <term lang="la">Fossitius</term> (called also of <term lang="la">Syluius</term>, <term lang="la">Hammoniacus</term> &#38;
<term lang="la">Communis</term>) which ought to be white, bright, not stony, thick,
and equal. 2. <term lang="la">Sal Gemm&#230;</term> whiche will easely cut or rente, &#38;  is 
lyke to the Cristal. 3. <term lang="la">Sal Naphthicus</term>, of colour blackysh, and
smelleth like <term lang="la">Bitumen</term>: and 4. <term lang="la">Sal Indus</term>. Which is reddysh, or
rather blackish, and is the strongest: next to it is <term lang="la">Naphthi&#173;
cus</term>: Thirdly <term lang="la">Gemmeus</term>, and the weakest is <term lang="la">Fossitius</term>. Salte is
whotte and drie (sayeth he) in the second degree, and howe
bitter soeuer it be, so muche the whotter and drier it is. 

Of <term lang="la">Sal Naphthicus</term>, Dioscorides maketh no mencion, but
Galenus <term lang="la">lib. 4. &#38;. 9. De symp.</term> sayeth <expan type="">that</expan> a certeine salt called <term lang="la">Sodo&#173;
minus</term>, is made in <expan type="">the</expan> sea of palestin of Syria, called <term lang="la">Mare mortu<expan type="">um</expan></term>,
wherin muche <term lang="la">Bitumen</term> is ingendred: and <term lang="la">Naphtha</term> is a kynde
of <term lang="la">Bituminis</term>, wherof <expan type="">the</expan> said salt is called <term lang="la">Sal Naphthicus</term>: wher&#173;
of also in Plinie is almost a like sence. What the Arabians
ment by <term lang="ar">Salem Indum</term>, it is hard to know. But what the Gre&#173;
cians ment therby, is shewed aboue vnder <term lang="la">Saccharum</term>. 

Salte consisteth of an earthye substance, bitter by ad&#173;
ustion, and of an vnsauory watery substance. 



</page>

<page no="104">
Whiche two if they be equall, make a salte sauoure. If the
earthy substance be the stronger, it wyll be bytter: but if
the watery, it wyll haue an easy saltnes. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Hales</term> <term lang="la">ex li&#173;
bris Galeni
De symp. fa&#173;
cult. li. 4. 9. 11. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Sal Mariunus</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Sal Fossitius</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Sal G<expan type="">em</expan>meus</term>
is as you see
a kynde of <term lang="la">Salis Fossitii</term>
or els must 
it varie fr<expan type="">om</expan> 
<term lang="la">Sale Gemmeo
Dioscoridis</term>. </note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Sal Ammoniacus. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="gk">&#x0391;&#x039C;&#x03BC;&#x03C9;&#x03BD;&#x03B9;&#x03B1;&#x03BA;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan> &#x1F05;&#x03BB;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term> <term lang="la">quasi Sal arenarius</term>, so called because
(as some wryte) it is by dygging founde in long pie&#173;
ces vnder sande, or els of his natiue place (as aboue
of the Gumme <term lang="la">Hammoniacum</term>) is more commonly and moste
corruptly called <term lang="la">Sal armoniacus</term>, and is of two sortes. Namely,
the naturall called <term lang="la">Natiuus</term>, whiche is thought to be con&#173;
teyned vnder the sortes of <term lang="la">Salis panis</term>, and shoulde in colour
be lyke <term lang="la">Alumen Siston</term>, and shyning: beyng so salte, that it is of
vnpleasant taste, but profitable in medicine. 

<term lang="la">Sal Ammoniacus</term> is (sayeth Dioscorides) peculiarly praised,
if it may bee easyly cutte or clefte into streighte flakes or
sclyces, with vs bothe out of vse and knowledge, though
in tyme longe synce better knowne. The other whiche 
we haue is artificall, and therfore called <term lang="la">Factitius</term>, and is
chiefly occupied of Goldsmythes: and oughte not to bee v&#173;
sed in steade of the true thynge, otherwyse then in muche
lesse qu<expan type="">an</expan>titie. But Galen biddeth vs substitute in his place
<term lang="la">Salem Capadocum</term>. And dyuers other learned men, <term lang="la">Salem Gem&#173;
meum. </term>. His vertues are like the vertues of salte. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Hammoniacos
Halos. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Sal Ammonia&#173;
cus natiuus</term>. </note>

<note type="m"><term lang="la">Sal Hammonia
cus factitius</term>. </note>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Sal Nitrum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="saltpetre(n)">ALbeit (as moste true it is) that our Salte petre is not 
<term lang="gk">&#x039D;&#x03AF;&#x03C4;&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03BD; &#x03BA;&#x03B1;&#x03B9; &#x03BB;&#x03AF;&#x03C4;&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, of Dioscorides, Galen, Plinie, and
other auncient wryters, whiche is naturall, com&#173;
myng of certeyne waters in lakes: and should be lyght, rose
coloured or whyte, cleauyng or openyng it selfe into many
pores, lyke a spungy substance. But our Salt peter (which
is artificiall) is glisteryng whyte, (if it be good) or els blac&#173;
kyshe, (if it be not made perfecte by boylyng) solide and 
weyghty. 



</page>

<page no="105">
<term lang="la">Nitrum</term> also is taken <term lang="la">cum Lacerpitio</term>, of suche as fele not their
meate (whervnto salt peter is not the metest:) yet surely is
it euident, that Lanfranke and others right learned,
both of his tyme and long since, ment according to the vul&#173;
gar opinion, (though not without great errore,) that which
hath ben comm<expan type="">on</expan>ly called <term lang="la">Sal petr&#230;</term>, When they wrote <term lang="la">Sal
Nitrum</term>. 

But Mesues sayeth, that of <term lang="la">Nitrum</term>, there is one mineral
or naturall, and an other artificial called <term lang="la">Spuma Nitri</term>, white,
lyght, salte, and bityng, and weaker then the naturall <term lang="la">Ni&#173;
trum</term>. An other artificiall <term lang="la">Nitrum</term> also there is sayeth he, str<expan type="">on</expan>&#173;
ger th<expan type="">en</expan> <term lang="la">Nitrum</term>, and called <term lang="la">Flos parietis uel salis</term>. Hetherto Me&#173;
sues. And this doe I with Syluius take to be <term lang="la">Sal petr&#230;</term>, (if it
may in any wyse be founde among the auncientes) better
knowne as it semeth now, to the Apothecaries and to G<expan type="">un</expan>&#173;
ners, then in olde tyme to the auncient wryters. 

<term lang="la">Nitrum</term> sayeth Mesues, is drye in the thyrde degree, and
<emend err="and"></emend> whotte in the begynnyng of the same: But after Aui&#173;
cen only in the seconde. It is (sayth Galen) of power meane
betwene <term lang="la">Aphronitrum</term> &#38;  <term lang="la">Salem</term>, and by burning is more sub&#173;
tyle: and therfore draweth the nygher <term lang="la">Aphronitrum</term>. 

It dryeth and dygesteth, and is put (sayeth Dioscorides)
into emplasters, <expan type="">that</expan> drawe out, discusse, &#38;  extenuate: &#38;  scow&#173;
reth awaye leprosies. Whiche (if it be taken inwardly) cut&#173;
teth, and thinneth, grosse and toughe humores, muche
stronglier then salte: and is taken both rawe and burned,
against the suffocations of Musheromes. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Nitron. 
Litron. </term></note>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Sambucus. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="wallwort(n)" lexeme="Danewort(n)">SAmbucus, which is called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x0391;&#x03BA;&#x03C4;&#x1F74;</term>, semeth sayeth
Fuchsius to be so called of <term lang="la">Sambix</term>, the first fynder therof,
or of the musicall instrument <term lang="la">Sabucus</term>: and therfore (after
Quintus Serenius) ought it rather to be called a Sabucus. There
are of the Elder two kyndes, the one a tree called symply,
by that general name <term lang="la">Sambucus</term>. The other a shrubby herbe



</page>

<page no="106">
called <term lang="la">Ebulus</term>, and in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03C7;&#x03B1;&#x03BC;&#x03B1;&#x03B9;&#x03B1;&#x03BA;&#x03C4;&#x03B7; 	&#x0389;
</term>, <term lang="la">Quasi sambucus hu&#173;
milis</term>, in Englyshe <term lang="la">Walle worte</term>, or <term lang="la">Daneworte</term>. Whiche to
haue both heatyng and driyng facultie, the bitternes and meane bynding therof, doth sufficiently declare, according
to the mynde of Galen: though some wryte that it cooleth. 
It dygesteth also &#38;  glueth together. Dioscorides saieth that
they drye, drawe out water, and hurte the stomache. 

Their leaues sodde and eaten, doe purge choler and
phlegme: and with wyne, doe helpe the byte of the viper. 
It mollyfieth the mother, correctynge the affectes of the 
same. The freshe and tender leaues, applied <term lang="la">cum polenta</term>, mi&#173;
tigateth inflammations, helpeth burnynges, and the by&#173;
tinges of dogges, and glueth together depe and fistulous
vlcers. Two kindes more hereof are of <expan type="">the</expan> late wryters m<expan type="">en</expan>&#173;
cioned as <term lang="la">Syluestris</term>, beyng a more shrubby tree with beries
alwayes redde: and <term lang="la">Palustris</term> growyng alwayes in <expan type="">the</expan> fennes. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Cham&#230;acte</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Sambucus
montana siue
syluestris. </term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Sanguis draconis. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="blood(n)" lexeme="dragon's blood(n)">BLoude sayeth Galen, called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x1F05;&#x03B9;&#x03BC;&#x03B1;</term>, varieth
as the natures of the beast<damage>e</damage>s, from whence it floweth. 
The bloude of one being thinne &#38;  liquide, of another 
dryer, an others bloude is whotter, but no bloud is colde. 

<term lang="la">Sanguis draconis</term>, soundeth <term lang="la">dragons bloud</term>: about the which
there are not a fewe opinions. Some iudge it the iuyce of 
an herbe, others the gumme or liquor of a tree, and Plinie
calleth it <term lang="la">Cinnabarin</term>. who sayeth that the Eleph<expan type="">an</expan>t, beyng in
the conflicte betwene hym and the dragon, (whose mutual
hatred neuer ceaseth,) strangled: falleth with great weight
on the drag<expan type="">on</expan> and dasheth him in sonder. The bloud of both
the whiche running forth together, is after gathered made
in a lumpe, dried and kept for medicine: and this Leonicenus
as well as diuers others, disapproueth not. But that <term lang="la">Cinna&#173;
baris</term>, is <term lang="la">Legitimus sanguis draconis</term>, is farre from truthe, as here
after shall appere. First Dioscorides treateth of <term lang="la">Cinabaris</term>, in 
his fifth booke (as Galen also doth,) among mineralles, (not
speaking a worde therof in his second boke, where he trea&#173;



</page>

<page no="107">
teth of dyuers bloudes) saiyng: that some men estemed it to 
be <term lang="la">Sanguinem draconis</term>, because that suche as then was, would 
scantly serue the painters to varie a colour with. Second&#173;
ly, Theophrastus maketh it a metall, growynge in Hiberia and
Colchis. None otherwyse sayeth Auerois also, of <term lang="la">Cinnabaris</term>. 
But that whiche we haue c<expan type="">om</expan>monly to be solde of the Apo&#173;
thecaries, semeth (not without the authoritie of diuers lear<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
ned men) to be the teares or g<expan type="">um</expan>me of a tre growyng in A&#173;
phrica, whose rynde may be sene in the lumpes of the same:
whiche as Matthiolus iudged, might be <term lang="la">Cinnabaris Dioscoridis</term>. 

Serapio also mencioneth a kinde of <term lang="la">Sanguinis draconis</term>, to bee
the iuice of an herbe, whiche he describeth both in forme &#38;
faculties, as Dioscorides doth <expan type="">the</expan> fourth kinde of <term lang="la">Sideritis</term>, cal&#173;
led <term lang="la">Achillea Sideritis</term>: whiche Brasauolus wysheth to be in vse a&#173;
mong the Apothecaries, rather then the gumme that they
haue. Thus doe you se here three sortes of <term lang="la">Sanguinis draconis</term>,
variyng eche from other as notably as may be. 

<term lang="la">Sanguis draconis</term> sayeth Platearius, hath byndyng and conso&#173;
lydatiue powre, and is profitable for the spyttyng of bloud,
bledyng at the nose or els where. <term lang="la">Vnde, menses cum Corrigiola
sistit. </term></xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="sanies(n)">Sanies. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="la">Sanies</term>, is generally taken for that superfluous liquide
substance that floweth from euery vlcer, regularly or
otherwyse, or what kynde or qualitie soeuer it be, and
as it differeth in coloure, substance, or qualities, so is it kno&#173;
wne and called by seuerall names, (as well as the vlcers
from whence it floweth,) As by destinction a pure, <term lang="la">Sanies
purulens</term>: a sordite, <term lang="la">Sanies sordida</term>, or filthy matter: a <term lang="la">Viro uirulens</term>, 
or <term lang="la">venemous</term>. But thou shalt perceiue Lanfranke alwaies
(for the most part,) to meane by <term lang="la">Saniem</term>, the same quitture,
whyte and thicke to beholde, light to touche, and equall, or
eche waye lyke to it selfe, and not stinkyng: whiche Galen 
calleth <term lang="la">Pus laudabile</term>, and we good or well digested matter. 
But in apostemes otherwyse as before. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Lib. 1. de diffe&#173;
rentiis febri<expan type="">um</expan>
cap. 6. </term></note></wordentry>



</page>

<page no="108">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Santalum siue Sandalum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="sanders(n)" lexeme="gout(n)">OF the wooddes called <term lang="la">Sanders</term> (wrytten commonly
<term lang="la">Sandali</term>,) there is among the olde Grecians no menci<expan type="">on</expan>
founde, vnlesse their opinion should be of force, that
woulde haue <term lang="la">Santalum rubrum</term>, to bee <term lang="la">Aspalathum Dioscoridis</term>: 
whiche Serapio doth manifestly confute. For in wrytyng of 
<term lang="la">Santali</term>, he yeldeth no testimony of Dioscorides (as elswhere
he is wonte,) but the authorities of his owne contrey men:
wryting also of <term lang="la">Aspalathi</term> vnder the Arabik name <term lang="ar">Darsisa<damage>h</damage>an</term>. 
And farther <term lang="la">Santalum</term> is the woodde of a taule steight tree,
but <term lang="la">Aspalathus</term> is a lowe shrubbe, odoriferous, and bitter in
taste: neither of the whiche is founde in <term lang="la">Santalo rubro</term>. Of the
Arabians therfore, was it first founde and described. 

<term lang="la">Santalum</term> (sayeth Matthiolus) groweth in certeyn great and 
thicke wooddes of both the Indies, and is of three kyndes:
wherof <term lang="la">Santalum Pallidum or Citrinum</term> is beste estemed. The
next place hath <term lang="la">Santalum Album</term>. The thyrde and worste,
is <term lang="la">Rubrum</term>, whiche is without odore: but the other doe smell
well. They are colde (by the testymonies of the Arabians)
in the thyrde degree, and drye in the seconde, hauynge byn&#173;
dyng, coolyng, and alteratiue vertue: And therfore are vsed
to mitigate thirste, and the whotte distemperance of the
lyuer, and for choleryke vomites. <term lang="la">Santalum rubrum</term> doth pry&#173;
uatly resiste defluxions, and is profitably applyed <term lang="la">Ex succo so&#173;
lani, uel Sedi, uel portulac&#230;</term>, to vehement inflammations, &#38;  the 
gowte of the feete. <term lang="la">Santalum Candidum atque pallidum</term> layde to
the forehead, <term lang="la">cum aqua Rosarum</term>, doe ease the peynes of the
heade: and beyng drunke, doe merueylously helpe whotte
stomaches. They doe (sayth Auicen) most effectually make
gladde, and strengthen the hearte. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Aspalathus. </term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="soap(n)">Sapo. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="soap(n)"><term lang="la">SOpe</term>, whiche the Arabians cal <term lang="ar">Sabon</term>, is of diuers kindes
<term lang="la">in opere pandactarum</term>. As <term lang="la">Sapo Gallicus</term>, <term lang="la">Sapo muscatus</term>, and <term lang="la">Sapo
Spatharenticus</term>, so called of his excedyng sharpnes and cut&#173;



</page>

<page no="109">
tyng, and is also called <term lang="la">Mollis, niger, &#38;  Iudaicus</term>, wherwith
they washe sylke. The softe or graye sope, is made of the
stronge lye called <term lang="la">Capitellum</term>, and oyle together. Hard sope
hath in composition in the steade of oyle, shepes talowe. 
They are of a hotte and composed exulceratiue operation,
and doe scoure, putrefie, and maturate, harde apostemes. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="sarcocolla(n)">Sarcocolla. </form> 
<xpln lexeme="ophthalmia(n)">IS the Gumme or liquore of a tree growyng in Persia, as 
wytnesseth Dioscorides, Like the poulder of Frankyn&#173;
sence, redde or pale, or as Mesues sayeth whyte, and
bytter: and is called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03B1;&#x03C1;&#x03BA;&#x03BF;&#x03BA;&#x03CC;&#x03BB;&#x03BB;&#x03B1;</term>, <term lang="la">id est glutinum
carnis</term>, because it glueth excellently fleshe together. Galen
sayeth that it dryeth without bytyng, and therfore is of po<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
wre to adglutinate woundes, and to staye the fluxe or run&#173;
nyng of the eyes. It heateth after Mesue, in the seconde
degree, but drieth lesse. It concocteth or maturateth, scou&#173;
reth, openeth, and digesteth. Inwardly, it purgeth rawe
phlegme and other grosse humores, fr<expan type="">om</expan> the brayne chiefly,
from the sinewes, the ioyntes, and the l<expan type="">un</expan>ges: And therfore
is profitable for olde phlegmatike, asthmatike, and coug&#173;
hing persons, and <emend err="p">h</emend>elpeth <term lang="la">Ophthalmia</term>, and other diseases of
the eyes. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De sym. med. 
facultat. lib. 8. </term></note>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Scabiosa pilosa. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="matfellon(n)" lexeme="devil's bit(n)"><term lang="la">SCabiosa</term>, whiche Aetius calleth <term lang="gk">&#x03C8;&#x03CE;&#x03C1;&#x03B1;&#x03BD;</term>, <term lang="la">quasi scabiem Sanant<expan type="">em</expan></term>,
(because it healeth scabbes) is an herbe wherof <expan type="">the</expan> greci<expan type="">an</expan>s
neyther olde nor late writers, haue left any light. Though
Math&#230;us Syluaticus, (as reporteth Iacobus Manlius) affirmeth it
to be <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03C4;&#x03BF;&#x03B9;&#x03B2;&#x03BA;&#x1F74;</term> of the Grecia<expan type="">an</expan>s: whose sentence hath few lear&#173;
ned fautores,) Whervnto there are referred fyue kyndes:
wherof the first two differ not but in greatnes, for the one
is muche lesse then the other. The thyrde is called <term lang="la">Scabiosa
Ouina</term>. The fourth <term lang="la">Iacea negra</term>, and vulgarly <term lang="la">Materfilon</term>. The



</page>

<page no="110">
fifthe whiche Fuchsius (imitating a certeine olde writen her&#173;
ball,) calleth <term lang="la">Succisam</term>. The common sorte (ledde by a moste
vayne superstition, that the diuell of enuye to mankynde &#38;
his felicitie, should haue bytte away, this precious roote:)
haue named <term lang="la">Morsum diaboli</term>, <term lang="la">quasi a diabolo pr&#230;morsa,</term> &#38;  in english
<term lang="la">diuels bytte</term>, of some <term lang="la">Matfelon</term>, but moste aptly <term lang="la">heary sca&#173;
biose</term>. This doth most iustly agree, both in forme &#38;  vertues
with <term lang="la">Scabiosa pilosa</term>, that Lanfranke describeth. For it is vsed
sayeth Fuchsius, as a present remedy to maturate and heale
Carbuncles, eyther brused grene and applyed to the same,
or the wyne drunke wherin it was boyled: and is nowe
founde to haue discussiue, and incisiue facultie, and vsed to
dissolue coagulated bloud. This <term lang="la">Succisa</term>, hath ben thought
of some to bee <term lang="la">Geum Plinii</term>: Whiche with greater reason,
is more aptly aplyed to <term lang="la">Caryophyllata</term>. They are all vnmea&#173;
surable bitter, and therfore must nedes be hotte and drye,
and are in qualities lyke <term lang="la">Succisa</term>. 

Of <term lang="la">Scabiosa</term>, <term lang="la">in opere pandactarum</term> I fynde as foloweth. Men
saye that S. Urban at the peticions of a certeine asthma&#173;
tike sister of his, (that vsed scabiose continually,) sente to hir
these verses, of the vertues therof. 
<term lang="la">
<lemeformat>
Vrbanus pro senescit pretium Scabiosa&#230;. 
Nam purgat pectus quod comprimit &#230;gra senectus. 
Lenit pulmonem purgat laterum regionem. 
Apostema frangit, si locum bibita tangit. 
Tribus uncta foris anthracem liberat horis. 
</lemeformat>
</term>
Whiche may with a fewe moe wordes thus in
verse be englyshed. 

<lemeformat>
To Urbane him selfe, it is vncerteine. 
Howe many vertues, in scabiose reygne:
But excellently it clenseth the breste,
Of sicke aged folke, that there are opreste. 
The pypes of the lunges, if rough they apere,
It maketh them smothe, yea gentle and clere. 
</lemeformat>



</page>

<page no="111">
<lemeformat>
The roumes of the breste, that we the sydes call,
It purgeth well, from incombrances all. 
If it be drunke, so that it touche the place,
Apostemes it breakes, by peculiar grace. 
Without to Carbuncles, if it layde be,
It doth lose and breake them, within houres three. 
</lemeformat>
</xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Psora</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Stoebe</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Succisa</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Morsus Dia&#173;
boli. </term></note>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Scammonium. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="scammony(n)" lexeme="bindweed(n)" lexeme="ivy(n)" lexeme="turbith(n)"><term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03BA;&#x03B1;&#x03BC;&#x03BC;&#x03CE;&#x03BD;&#x03B9;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, so called <term lang="la">Per Antonomasian</term>, is the iuyce of the
herbe <term lang="gk">&#x03C2;&#x03BA;&#x03B1;&#x03BC;&#x03BC;&#x03C9;&#x03BD;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;</term> prepared, &#38;  called the<emend err="m">n</emend> <term lang="la">Diacridium</term>. The
Romains also doe (after the place) name it <term lang="la">Colphonium</term>. 

<term lang="la">Scammonia</term>, saith Dioscorides, putteth forth grosse, fatte, &#38;
many br<expan type="">an</expan>ches, fr<expan type="">om</expan> one roote, of the length of thre cubites,
with the leaues of <term lang="la">Helxin&#230;</term> (whiche we call byndwede,) or of
Iuie: but softer, rough, and triangled, a floure white, ro<expan type="">un</expan>de 
and holowe, lyke a maunde basket, or bell formed cuppe, &#38;
of heauy smell. The roote is very longe, of a cubite thyck&#173;
nes, and whyte, with a heauy sauoure, and a heauy iuyce. 
Whiche is thus gathered. 

The roote (the head therof beyng first cutte of) is made 
holowe with a knife, that the iuice may runne out into the
holownes: whiche is taken in vessells, and dryde. The sc<expan type="">am</expan>&#173;
monie is praised that is light, cleare, thinne, of coloure most
lyke to glewe of lether, called <term lang="la">Glutinum taurinum</term>: Like a mu&#173;
sherum, with fine pores or fistules, suche as is brought fr<expan type="">om</expan>
Mysia, a region of Asia. And trust not it that will only waxe
white, being touched with the tongue: for so will that doe,
that is adulterate with the iuyce of <term lang="la">Tithymali</term>, wherof the
greatest token is, that it burneth not the tongue vehem<expan type="">en</expan>t&#173;
ly, as the right Sc<expan type="">am</expan>monie doth. One drachme of the iuyce
drunke <term lang="la">ex aqua pura uel mulsa</term>, purgeth downwards choler &#38;
phlegme. Mesues writing of fiue kindes of <term lang="la">Volubilis</term> or <term lang="la">Con&#173;
uoluulus</term>, describeth the fifth kynde therof (whiche groweth
in Antiocha, Armenia, Arabia &#38;  Turchia) to haue a roote like to 
<term lang="la">Bryonia</term> or bigger, like a great gourde, a stalke . ii. cubites l<expan type="">on</expan>g 
with little narowe leaues, formed like a fethered arowe,
and falling of at euery light occasion: hauyng more plentie



</page>

<page no="112">
of mylky iuyce then the reste, whereof sc<expan type="">am</expan>monie is made:
and therfore is called the mistres of mylke geuing herbes. 
It is hotte and drye in the seconde degree (or rather with 
Galen in the begynning of the thyrde. ) &#38;c. In the chapiter
of <term lang="la">Scammonium</term> he sayeth, that Sc<expan type="">am</expan>monie is of purgynge me&#173;
dicines the strongest. And after he hath shewed diuers ma&#173;
ners of gathering, drying, workyng it in paste, and sealyng
it: he sayeth that it should be cleare lyke gummes, or whi&#173;
tyshe, or of dyuers colours, chiefly that which floweth first. 
The same touched or rubbed, with spittell or water, ge&#173;
ueth mylke. It ought also to be tender, brittle and easye to
be pouldered, lyght, and referring the propre odoure of the
herbe: but that good, not stynkyng. From the which notes
howe muche the more it differeth, so much the worse it is. 
It may be kept twenty yeares, but the older it is the wea&#173;
ker. It is hotte and drye in the thyrde degree, bytter and
sharpe: but not so sharpe as the herbe. It cutteth, scoureth,
and purgeth yelowe choler, by attraction fr<expan type="">om</expan> out the bloud,
euen from the extreme partes: so muche is his furious ve&#173;
hemencie. It hurteth the harte, the stomache, the liuer, and
the guttes, and troubleth the other bowelles, subuerteth
the stomache, taketh away appetyte, moueth abhorfulnes,
stirreth thyrste, and ought not to be geu<expan type="">en</expan> to cholerick per&#173;
sons, and such as are apte to take feuers. When it is taken,
heate, colde, colde frutes, colde water, long sleape, exercise,
wrath, and other vehement affections of the mynde, ought 
to be aduoyded: and so goeth he forth to the corrections of
the same. 

But nowe whether <term lang="la">Scammonia Dioscoridis</term>, and <term lang="la">Volubilis
quinta Mesu&#230;</term>, be one herbe vnder two names, or not: whose
descriptions, though (beyng precysely examined,) they doe
not exactly agree: yet are they (as you see) of no small affini&#173;
tie. And may haue the more, for that Mesues dispraiseth for
nought, both the herbe and the iuyce of his co<expan type="">un</expan>try: Wher&#173;
of belyke he made his description, or els perhaps of suche a
one, as he knewe not otherwyse then by reporte of others. 
For so are ryghte well approued authores, some tymes 



</page>

<page no="113">
founde to doe. I leaue it to the wise and learned to discusse:
rather then rashly to affirme any thyng, wherof I haue
neyther sensible knowledge, nor iust assurance: and that
the rather because ryght worthy wryters doe leaue it in
doubte. But that <term lang="la">Scammonium Dioscoridis</term> is as vtterly out of
vse as vnknowne, there is no doubte. For we haue none
that maye in any wyse bee compared therto: for our best
may rather be adiudged <term lang="la">Scammonium Iudaicum, uel Syriacum</term>, 
whiche Dioscorides estemeth moste vile. And farther .x. v. 
graynes of the common <term lang="la">Scammonium</term>, hath ben sene to purge
the belly often, or with dyuers stooles: whiche by the testi&#173;
monie of Dioscorides a whole drachme should scarsly doe. 
Wherfore Andreas Marinus, wysheth vs diligently to serche
out, and prudently to vse our owne natiue medicines. For
we haue in our regions (sayeth he) medicines that (if they
were ryghtly knowne and prepared,) would purge muche
more luckily then <term lang="la">Scammonium</term>, or vnknowne Turbith. For
if the best beare with it so many nociue qualities, as aboue
is shewed: what then dothe the worste, wherof we haue the
vse: Whiche truly moued Brasauolus to wyshe, that it had
neuer come to the handes of men: as a thyng more hurtful
then profitable. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Scammonion</term>
<term lang="la">enim patroxsi&#173;
micum nom<expan type="">en</expan>a</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Scammonia</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">ortem est. </term>. </note>
<note type="m">Dioscor. <term lang="la">lib. 4. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Volubilis</term></note>

<form lang="la">Scarificatio. </form> 
<xpln lexeme="scarification(n)"><term lang="la">SCarificatio uel cutis Sculptura</term>, englishly <term lang="la">Scarfication</term>, is cal&#173;
led in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03C7;&#x03B1;&#x03C1;&#x03AC;&#x03BA;&#x03BC;&#x03B1; &#x03B1;&#x03C0;&#x03CC; &#x03C4;&#x03BF;&#x03C5; &#x03C7;&#x03B1;&#x03C1;&#x03AC;&#x03C4;&#x03C4;&#x03C9;</term>, <term lang="la">Hoc est Sculpo,

<wordentry type="h">
<form lexeme="schoenanth(n)">Sch&#339;nanthum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="fen(n)" lexeme="rush(n)" lexeme="camel's straw(n)"><term lang="gk">&#x03A3;&#x03C7;&#x03BF;&#x03AF;&#x03BD;&#x03BF;&#x03C5;&#x03B1;&#x03BD;&#x03B8;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>, <term lang="la">quasi Schoeni, uel Schini flos</term>, It is the floure
of the swete rushe called <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03BA;&#x03CC;&#x03B9;&#x03BD;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan> &#x03B1;&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03BC;&#x03B1;&#x03C4;&#x03B9;&#x03BA;&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</term>, <term lang="la">hoc est iuncus odo
ratus</term>. This rushe sayeth Dioscorides, must be chosen redde
and newe, bearyng fyne flowres with redde fragmentes,
whiche beyng rubbed in the hande smelleth lyke the rose:



</page>

<page no="114">
and byndeth the tongue with a fyery byting. The flowres, 
the stalkes, and the rootes wherof, are in vse. The flowre
sayeth Galen (which we handle,) doth heate and bynde mo&#173;
deratly, neyther lacketh it a subtill nature: and therfore
<term lang="la">Vrinam &#38; menses mouet</term>, geuen in drinke, or in fomentations:
and profiteth also the inflammations of the lyuer, the sto&#173;
mache, and the belly. But the roote is more adstringente,
(though the flowre also doe bynde with all his partes:) and
therfore is myxed with medicines that are geuen for spyt&#173;
ting of bloud. Hitherto Galen. 

And for as muche as these flowres are not now brought
vnto vs, yea and in Venice rarely sene: (wherat our mer&#173;
ueile may be the lesse, synce Galen noteth th<expan type="">em</expan> in his time to
be very rare:) The learned phisiti<expan type="">en</expan>s haue therfore councel&#173;
led to vse, The tenderest or vppermost partes of the herbe
or rushe. But our Apothecaries, (saye they) goynge to Ve&#173;
nise, are deceyued in <term lang="la">Iunco odorato</term>: &#38;  buye eyther in the steade
therof, or mixed therwith, certeyne tender strigges of <term lang="la">Iuncia
palustris</term>, that is the marshe rushe, or rushe of the fennes. 

It is commonly called <term lang="la">Pastum uel palca Camellorum</term>, or <term lang="la">Ca&#173;
melles strawe</term>. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Schoenu<expan type="">an</expan>thos</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Schoenos aro&#173;
maticos</term>. </note>

lib. 8. </term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="scilla(n)">Scilla. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="squill(n)" lexeme="sea onion(n)"><term lang="gk">&#x03A3;&#x03BA;&#x03AF;&#x03BB;&#x03BB;&#x03B1;</term>, called also of some <term lang="la">Scylla</term>, <term lang="la">Cepe muris</term>, &#38;  <term lang="la">Cepa marinsh</term>
of the Apothecaries <term lang="la">Squilla</term>, in englyshe <term lang="la">squille</term> or <term lang="la">sea onyon</term>,
hath sharpe feruent, and cutting power, and heateth in the
second degree. It mollifieth the belly, moueth vrine, is good
for the hydropsie, for the iaundis, for the paynes of the belly
and the olde cough. Whervnto it helpeth much (by <expan type="">the</expan> minde
of Galen) to be rosted or sodde, rather then rawe: for so is 
his vehemencie diminished. Hereof see more in D. Tur&#173;
ners herball. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="scirrhus(n)">Scirrhus. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="gk">&#x03A3;&#x03BA;&#x03AF;&#x03C1;&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03C2;, &#x03BA;&#x03B1;&#x03AF; &#x03C3;&#x03BA;&#x03BB;&#x03B7;&#x03C1;&#x03CC;&#x03C4;&#x03B7;&#x03C2;</term>, <term lang="la">Id est durities</term>, writen of old <term lang="la">Sclirosis</term>,



</page>

<page no="115">
is (as I gather of Galen in diuers places) a tumore against 
nature, and an affecte of harde and thicke partes. (Whiche
yet to take, the lyuer &#38;  the splene are moste readye) harde,
without peyne, and sometyme without sense. Whose fyrste
cause sayeth he, in his booke of tumores against nature, is
double: as of grosse &#38;  toughe phlegme, and of the dregges
of bloud. Whiche also is double, wherof the one doth Hip&#173;
pocrates call blacke: The other also is blacke, but proper&#173;
ly called blacke choler. And in his fifthe booke of symple me&#173;
dicines, he affirmeth it to sprynge of colde and grosse hu&#173;
mores: as of melancholy, or of grosse and viscous phlegme,
(ouermuche dryed, and impacte in the pores of the skynne)
or of bothe: and chanceth often by ignorance, (throughe the
vse of vehement byndyng and coolyng thynges,) to inflam&#173;
mations, and <term lang="la">Ignibus sacris</term>. If it come of phlegme, it hathe
some obscure sense, &#38;  is curable with mollificatiues, though
hardly: but that whiche commeth of melancholyke iuyce,
is cancrous (the partes affected vtterly without sense,) and
vncurable: and is by mollyficatiues exasperate. 

And as the pure <term lang="la">Scirrhus</term>, made of melancholy, maye in
mixtures receyue any of the other three humores: so may
it in name variably ch<expan type="">an</expan>ge, and be called of phlegme <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03BA;&#x03AF;&#x03C1;&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03C2; 
&#x03BF;&#x03B9;&#x03B4;&#x03B7;&#x03BC;&#x03B1;&#x03C4;&#x1F65;&#x03B4;&#x03B5;&#x03C2;</term>, <term lang="la">Id est Scirrhus oedematosus</term>. Of bloude <term lang="gk">&#x03C6;&#x03BB;&#x03B5;&#x03B3;&#x03BC;&#x03BF;&#173;
&#x03BD;&#x1F65;&#x03B4;&#x03B5;&#x03C2;</term>, <term lang="la">Hoc est infl<expan type="">am</expan>mationis consors</term>, or of choler, and is named
<term lang="gk">&#x0395;&#x03C1;&#x03C5;&#x03C3;&#x03B9;&#x03C0;&#x03B5;&#x03BB;&#x03B1;&#x03C4;&#x1F65;&#x03B4;&#x03B5;&#x03C2;</term>, <term lang="la">quasi ignit&#230; rubedinis, uel sacri ignis particeps</term>. </xpln>

<form lang="en" lexeme="scrofula(n)">Scrophula. </form>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Seges syluestris. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="corn(n)"><term lang="la">SEges</term>, signifieth moste commonly standyng Corne. And
where Lanfranke hath <term lang="la">Folia segetis syluestris</term>, I know not
howe to vnderstand hym therin, vnlesse he meane ther&#173;
by some kynde of the wylde cornes, that vsually growe in
Italy: as <term lang="la">Milium</term>, <term lang="la">Milium Indicum</term>, which they call <term lang="it">Sorgos</term>,) <term lang="la">Pani&#173;
cum</term>, <term lang="la">Bromus</term>, &#38;c.  Wherof the poore nedy wretches there,
make their breade, especially of <term lang="it">Sorgos</term>. But whiche of these
(if any) he meaneth, it is harde to gesse. But Galen in glu&#173;
ing together woundes, and healing vlcers, (which is Lan&#173;
franke his purpose) prayseth <term lang="la">Milium</term> for the best. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De symp. li. 7</term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="sempervivum(n)">Semperuiuum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="ay-green(n)"><term lang="la">SEmperuiuum</term>, whiche we may moste aptly call <term lang="la">Aygrene</term>, 
is called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03B1;&#x03B5;&#x03AF;&#x03B6;&#x03C9;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, <term lang="la">id est semperuiuens</term>, because it
lyueth styll and kepeth grene, both wynter &#38; sommer:
neyther dieth it by any iniurious wether. Truly a singuler
miracle of nature, that an herbe so colde and moyste, grow&#173;
ing on the house toppe, (a place moste drie, and subiecte not
only to the parchyng bemes of the Sunne in sommer, but
also to the moste colde frostes, and vehemente stormes of
wynter,) should yet lyue and be grene. Wherof indede,
manye suche as are ready to conuerte those merueylous
workes of God, (that their brutyshe eyes can not otherwise
beholde) into moste horrible supersticiouns: haue conceiued a
moste vayne opinion, that lightninge coulde not hurte the
house on whose toppe it grewe: and haue therfore called it
<term lang="la">Barbam Iouis</term>, and is of three kyndes: as <term lang="la">Sedum maius</term>, wherof
hitherto. 

The second is <term lang="la">Sedum minus</term>, called in Greke <term lang="gk">&#x03A4;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;&#x03B8;&#x03B1;&#x03BB;&#x1F72;&#x03C2;</term>,
for that it bloweth thryce in the yere, and therof is there
<term lang="la">Mas</term> &#38; <term lang="la">foemina</term>. The male hauing a yelowe floure, the floure
of the female is pale or whyte, and is called <term lang="la">Vermicularis</term>, and



</page>

<page no="117">
<term lang="la">Crassula minor</term>. There semeth also (sayeth Dioscorides) to be a
thirde kynde, called of some <term lang="gk">&#x1F00;&#x03BD;&#x03B4;&#x03C1;&#x03AC;&#x03BA;&#x03BD;&#x03B7; &#x1F04;&#x03B3;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;&#x03B1;</term>, <term lang="la">id est portula&#173;
ca syluestris</term>, of other <term lang="gk">&#x03C4;&#x03B5;&#x03BB;&#x03B5;&#x03C6;&#x03B9;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, and of the Romaines <term lang="la">Illecebra</term>,
of nature very hotte, contrary to the reste. 

<term lang="la">Sedum</term>, sayeth Galen, (both <term lang="la">Maius</term> and <term lang="la">minus</term>) dryeth lightly,
and byndeth moderatly: yet cooleth it in the thyrde degree. 
Wherin also a watery essence, beareth the maisterie: and
therfore auayleth <term lang="la">ad erysipelata, Herpetes, &#38;  Phlegmonas</term>, com&#173;
myng of a fluxion: and is an excellent repercussiue. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Aeizoon</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Barba Iouis</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Sedum</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Trithales</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Andrachne a&#173;
gria.</term> <term lang="gk">Telephi<expan type="">on</expan></term>. </note>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="sagapenum(n)" lexeme="ferula(n)">Serapinum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">COruptly so called of Apothecaries, but more trulye 
<term lang="la">Sagapenum</term>, is the iuyce (sayth Dioscorides) of an herbe
lyke to <term lang="la">Ferul&#230;</term>, growyng in Media: wherof the beste is
shyning through, and of a bryght or shining yelow coloure
without, &#38; whyte within: with an odore meane as it were
betwene <term lang="la">Laser</term> and <term lang="la">Galbanum</term>: sharpe in taste, and will (sayth 
Galen) sone resolue in water. 

It profiteth in the peynes of the breste and the sydes, to
olde coughes, conuulsions and ruptures, and expurgeth the
grosse phlegme of the lunges. 

<term lang="la">Sagapenum</term> (sayeth Galen) is whotte and of subtyll partes
as are other liquores: But obteyneth a certeine abstersion,
wherby it purgeth and extenuateth the scarres of the eye:
To the dulnesse also, and the dimnes of the sight, commyng
of the grosnes of humores, It is a good medicine. But the
plante lyke to <term lang="la">Ferula</term> (wherof it commeth) is weake and vn&#173;
profitable. </xpln>
<note type="m">Dioscor. <term lang="la">lib. 3. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De antidotis</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De sym. med. 
facultat. lib. 8. </term>. </note>
<lemenote>
"sagapenum" antedates the earliest OED citation (1597).
</lemenote>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="serpigo(n)">Serpigo. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="alopecia(n)">I C<expan type="">an</expan>n not better expresse Lanfranke his minde of <term lang="la">Serpigo</term>,
then hym selfe hath done, in the thirde treatice and first
doctrine of his greater worke, saiyng. I saie therfore that 
<term lang="la">Serpigo</term> is an exasperation of the skynne, whiche crepeth hi&#173;



</page>

<page no="118">
ther and thither, called in French <term lang="fr">Derbes</term>, in Lumbardy
<term lang="fr">Dembeda</term>, or <term lang="fr">Volatica</term>, of others <term lang="la">Ignis uolaticus</term>, and commeth of
burned humores, assaultyng the skynne with exasperati<expan type="">on</expan>
or roughnes. wherby I iudge him (and others without n<expan type="">um</expan>&#173;
ber) to meane none other thynge, then Galen doth by <term lang="la">Her&#173;
pete symplici</term>. But looke more at <term lang="en">Herpes</term>. 

Howe be it, of <term lang="la">Thiria</term>, (whiche he ioyneth as a partener,
with <term lang="la">Serpigine</term>, and to come of the same causes:) Looke more
at his place in the letter <term lang="la">T</term>. For in his workes I fynd ther&#173;
of no farther explication. 

Howe be it, Guido, Rolandus, and Rogers, wyth dyuers o&#173;
thers liuing about that time, make foure kyndes of lepro&#173;
sies, (a diuision in Galen and the auncientes not founde,)
whiche may come of the foure humores burned, as <term lang="la">Alopecia</term>,
whiche commeth of burned bloud, (though Rolandus saye of
phlegme.) <term lang="la">Leonina</term>, that springeth of burnte choler, infecting
the bloud. <term lang="la">Elephantia</term> commyng of melancholie, (Rolande
sayeth of bloud. ) And <term lang="la">Thiriasis</term>, caused of salt phlegme putre&#173;
fied, after Rolande of melancholie: so called say they <term lang="la">a Thiro</term>,
that is of an adder or viper: because it is scaly &#38; loseth the
skynne, as doth the adder. Wherin there is putrefaction,
both of the skynne and fleshe, and also pustules. Which na&#173;
me (if it may be of the Greke deriued) I iudge to come, <term lang="gk">&#x03B1;&#x03C0;&#x03CC;
&#x03C4;&#x03BF;&#x03C5;&#x03B4;&#x03B7;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;&#x03BF;&#x03C5;</term>, <term lang="la">Hoc est a serpente, uel fera aut noxa belua</term>. That is of a 
serpent or some cruell or hurtfull beaste, <term lang="la">Vt uipera uel lupus</term>. 
<term lang="gk">&#x03B4;&#x03B7;&#x03C1;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;</term> haue <expan type="">the</expan> old greci<expan type="">an</expan>s generally taken <term lang="la">pro bestiis uenenatis</term>,
that is for venemous beastes, &#38; such as leaue venim behind
them, with their stroke or bite: wherof c<expan type="">om</expan>meth <term lang="gk">&#x03B4;&#x03BA;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;&#x03CE;&#x03BC;&#x03B1;&#x03B4;&#x03B1;</term>,
<term lang="la">Id est tetra, uel uenenata ulcera</term>. That is cruell or venemous vl&#173;
cers. And so of <term lang="la">Theriacalia</term>, that is thynges or rather medi&#173;
cines that resiste venim. But at <term lang="la">Thiria</term> you may reade som&#173;
what more. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en">Solatrum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="nightshade(n)" lexeme="petty morel(n)" lexeme="winter cherry(n)" lexeme="alkekengi(n)">OF <term lang="la">Solanum</term>, whiche the Greekes name <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03C4;&#x03C1;&#x03CD;&#x03C7;&#x03BD;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, both
Dioscorides and Galen, doe make foure kindes. The
first is <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03C4;&#x03C1;&#x03CD;&#x03C7;&#x03BD;&#x03BF;&#x03C2; &#x03BA;&#x03B7;&#x03C0;&#x03B1;&#x03B9;&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</term>, <term lang="la">Solan<expan type="">um</expan> hortense</term>, with vs <term lang="la">night
shade</term>, or <term lang="la">petimorell</term>. The seconde <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03C4;&#x03C1;&#x03CD;&#x03C7;&#x03BD;&#x03BF;&#x03C2; &#x1F01;&#x03BB;&#x03B9;&#x03BA;&#x03AC;&#x03BA;&#x03B1;&#x03B2;&#x03BF;&#x03C2; &#x03BA;&#x03B1;&#x03B9;
&#x03A6;&#x03C5;&#x03C3;&#x03B1;&#x03BB;&#x03AF;&#x03C2;</term>, <term lang="la">Id est uesicaria</term>, which we call <term lang="la">wynter cherie</term>, and fol&#173;
lowyng the Apothecaries <term lang="la">Alkakenge</term>. The thirde is called
of Galen <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03C4;&#x03C1;&#x03CD;&#x03C7;&#x03BD;&#x03BF;&#x03C2; &#x03CD;&#x03C0;&#x03BD;&#x03C9;&#x03C4;&#x03B9;&#x03BA;&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</term>, <term lang="la">a consiliando somno, somniferum</term>, of
prouokyng sleape. The fourth (<term lang="la">quod ad insani<expan type="">am</expan> adigat,</term>) they call
<term lang="gk">&#x03BC;&#x03B1;&#x03BD;&#x03B9;&#x03BA;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, <term lang="la">Id est furiosum</term>. A fifth also haue the late wryters
founde and added, calling it <term lang="la">Vesicariam peregrin<expan type="">am</expan></term>. <term lang="la">Solanum hor<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
tense</term>, whiche doubtlesse Lanfranke meaneth by <term lang="la">Solatrum</term>,
doth Galen also call <term lang="la">Esculentum</term>, because in time past it was 
planted in gardyns, and vsually eaten, but we vse it rarely,
sayeth he as an aliment, (because it nourisheth litle,) but as
a medicine often. It cooleth and byndeth in the seconde de&#173;
gree: outwardly applied, they heale maligne vlcers. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="spica(n)">Spica. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="la">Spicanardi</term>, for so sayth Nicolaus in his recepte of <term lang="la">Diazingiber</term>,
or as some haue it <term lang="la">Spica indica</term>, or <term lang="la">Nardus indica</term>, is so called
sayth Galen, in his first booke, <term lang="la">De antidotis</term>: because it in
forme resembleth an eare of corne, for it is in deede a roote,
and is called also of lyke reason in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x039D;&#x03AC;&#x03C1;&#x03B4;&#x03BF; &#x03C5; <expan type="">&#x03C3;&#x03C4;</expan>&#x03AC;&#x03C7;&#x03C5;&#x03C2;</term>. 



</page>

<page no="120">
It is hotte in the thyrde degree, and drye in the ende of
the second, consisting of a sharpe adstringent, and light bit&#173;
ter substance: Therfore is the roote bothe drunke and out&#173;
wardly applyed, conuenient for the lyuer and the stomache. 
It moueth vryne, healeth the gnawinge of the stomache, &#38;
drieth the fluxions of the head, breste, and belly. Whervn&#173;
to <term lang="la">Nardus Indica</term>, whiche is the blacker, is stronger then
<term lang="la">Syriaca</term>. 

Other two kyndes there are, <term lang="gk">&#x039D;&#x03AC;&#x03C1;&#x03B4;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan> &#x03BA;&#x03B5;&#x03BB;&#x03C4;&#x03BF;&#x03BA;&#x1F70;</term>, <term lang="la">Hoc est
Gallica</term>, &#38; <term lang="gk">&#x039D;&#x03AC;&#x03C1;&#x03B4;&#x03BF;&#x03C2; &#x03BF;&#x03C1;&#x03B5;&#x03B9;&#x1F70;</term>. <term lang="la">id est Montana</term>. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Nardu Sta&#173;
chys</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De sym. me. 
facult. li. 8. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Nardos Celti&#173;
ca</term>. <term lang="gk">Nardos
orceia</term>. </note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="spodium(n)">Spodium. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="la">POmpholix</term> sayeth Dioscorides, differeth from <term lang="la">Spodio</term>, on&#173;
ly in <term lang="la">Specie</term>, not in kynde. And as the twoo kyndes of 
<term lang="gk">&#x03BA;&#x03B1;&#x03B4;&#x03BC;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;</term>, are the earthy or stony partes of the oure of
brasse, dryuen vp from the fornace, when the brasse is in
melting or purifying, as ashes or soote: and cleaueth to the
sydes, vaultes, and walles, of the fornace or melting house. 
Some for the lyghtnes therof rysing, and cleauynge in the
hygher partes, and called <term lang="gk">&#x0392;&#x03BF;&#x03C4;&#x03C1;&#x03C5;&#x03AF;&#x03C4;&#x03B9;&#x03C2;</term>: The other weyghtier
partes, that cleaue therfore in the lower places of the wal&#173;
les, they call <term lang="gk">&#x03C0;&#x03BB;&#x03B1;&#x03BA;&#x03AF;&#x03C4;&#x03B9;&#x03BD;</term>: So not only of brasse, but also of <term lang="la">Cad&#173;
mia</term> broken, and by the industrie of the founder or worke&#173;
man, sprinkled in the fornace, and at the last burned, com&#173;
meth both <term lang="en">Spodium</term> and <term lang="la">Pompholyx</term>. Whiche by the cunnyng
blowyng of the belowes, doe rise from the fornace. Wher&#173;
of some ryseth, and hangeth to the walles and couerynges
of the house, and is <term lang="gk">&#x03C0;&#x03BF;&#x03BC;&#x03C6;&#x03CC;&#x03BB;&#x03C5;&#x03BE;</term>: white, fatty, and so light, that
it may flie away in the ayer: and parte, whiche is <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03C0;&#x03CC;&#x03B4;&#x03B9;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, 
so called <term lang="gk">&#x03B1;&#x03C0;&#x03CC;&#x03C4;&#x03BF;&#x03C5;&#x03C3;&#x03C0;&#x03BF;&#x03B4;&#x03BF;&#x03C5;</term>, <term lang="la">id est a cinere sordido</term>, That is of ashes
myxt with dead coales, and rubbyshe: falleth on the flower:
and is of colour black, weyghtie, and ful of heares, &#38;  chaffe,
as the swepyng of the brasyers flowre. Whervnto sayeth 
Gallen, that seameth to be lyke in facultie, whiche is cal&#173;



</page>

<page no="121">
led <term lang="la">Antispodium</term>, whiche is not to be vsed where <term lang="la">Spodium</term> may
be had: neyther yet <term lang="la">Spodi<expan type="">um</expan></term>, if you may get <term lang="la">Pompholyx</term>. Which
if it be washed, is well nere the beste of all other medicines
that drye without bytyng. And therfore is a mete medicine
for cancrous and maligne vlcers: and is also put into col&#173;
lyries that heale the webbes of the eyes, and staye the 
fluxions of the same. And farther is a very good remedy, <term lang="la">ad
pudendorum, ac sedis ulcera</term>. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Cadmia</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Cadmia botry&#173;
itis quasi asi&#173;
nosa. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Cadmia placi&#173;

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la" lexeme="sea-girdle(n)">Spuma maris. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="gk">&#x0391;&#x039B;&#x03BA;&#x03C5;&#x03CC;&#x03BD;&#x03B9;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term> is called of the Apothecaries <term lang="la">Spuma maris</term>,
that is the spume or fome of the sea, wheron the bir&#173;
des called <term lang="la">Alcyones</term>, are sayde to make their neste, &#38;
is therfore called <term lang="la">Alcyonium</term>: though Plinie (whose sentence
therin is lesse allowed) affirme it to come of <expan type="">the</expan> Alci<expan type="">on</expan>s nests. 
It is after Dioscorides and Galen, of fyue kyndes, (wherof 
Plinie doth mencion but foure. ) The first is grosse, weight&#173;
tie, sharpe in taste, without lyke a spunge, of heauy smell
lyke rotten fyshe: and is founde chiefly on the shores. An o&#173;
ther is lyke the pin in the eye, or a spunge: lyght, <damage>f</damage>ull of ho&#173;
les, of forme somwhat longe, and smelleth lyke the sea we&#173;
des, called <term lang="la">Alga</term> and <term lang="gk">&#x03C6;&#x03CD;&#x03B7;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>. The thyrde is in forme lyke a
worme, softe and of purple colour: whiche they call <term lang="la">Milesum</term>. 
The fourth is light and ful of pores like the second, and re&#173;
sembleth the moyste woulle, called <term lang="la">Lana succida</term>. The fifth
is fashioned lyke a mosherome, without smoothe, within
rough, lyke the pumyse stone, of no sauoure, but of taste
sharpe. They doe all scoure awaye and dygeste, hauyng all
a whotte and sharpe qualitie. The firste twoo doe helpe
scabbes, skuruynes, leprosies, and take awaye spottes and
morphewes, and scoure and bewtifie the skynne: whiche
the fifte can not doe. The thyrde is of all the reste the sub&#173;
tillest, and is good for the affectes of the reynes, and blad&#173;
der, and for difficille pissynge: And beynge burned, dothe
cure with wyne <term lang="la">Alopecias</term>. The fourth hath also the same



</page>

<page no="122">
vertues, but is muche weaker. The fifte is of all the reste
the whoteste: in so muche that it burneth the heare, and ex&#173;
coriateth and exulcerateth the skynne. 
</xpln>
<note type="m">Galen. <term lang="la">de sym. 
Lib. 11. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Phycos</term>, <term lang="la">id est
Alga</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Sea girdle</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Milesum</term>. </note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="styrax(n)">Styrax. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="myrrh(n)"><term lang="gk">&#x03A3;&#x03C4;&#x03CD;&#x03C1;&#x03B1;&#x03BE;</term> or <term lang="la">Styrax calamita</term> a moste pleasant gumme, is the
teares of a tree, sayth Dioscorides, lyke to the quynce tree:
wherof the beste is fatty, pale or yelowe, and gummy, with
whyte lumpes, yeldyng when it is melted a liquor lyke ho&#173;
ny: and kepeth long his good sauour. That whiche is drie, is 
of Galen dispraysed: who sayth that it doth heate, mollifie,
and concoct. And therfore helpeth the cough, catarrhes, di&#173;
stillations, and murres. <term lang="la">Menses prolicit</term>, eyther dr<expan type="">un</expan>ke or layd
to. That whiche is commonly called <term lang="la">Storax liquida</term>, is the fat&#173;
ty substance, called <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03C4;&#x03B1;&#x03BA;&#x1F74;</term>, pressed out of Myrrhe: and chiefly
of that kynde that is called <term lang="la">Myrrha gabirea</term>. Wherfore looke
more at <term lang="en">Myrrha</term>. </xpln>
<note type="m">Dioscor. <term lang="la">lib. 1. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De sym. med. 
facultat. lib. 8. </term>
<term lang="la">De com. med. 
secundum ge&#173;
nera. lig. 7. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Stacte</term>. </note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="sulphur(n)">Sulphur. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">OR <term lang="la">Sulfur</term> is named in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03B8;&#x03B5;&#x1F33;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, &#38; is of two kindes,
(vnder <expan type="">the</expan> which Plinie reconeth. 4) The one (which is
the beste) is called <term lang="gk">&#x1F04;&#x03C0;&#x03C5;&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03C5;</term>, <term lang="la">Hoc est uiuum, uel ignis non ex&#173;
expers</term>, &#38; is taken out of the veyne without any boylyng. 
The other <term lang="gk">&#x03C0;&#x03B5;&#x03C0;&#x03C5;&#x03C1;&#x03C9;&#x03BC;&#x03AD;&#x03BD;&#x03C9;&#x03BD;</term>, <term lang="la">id est igne incoctum</term>, that is boiled <term lang="la">sul&#173;
fur</term>. The best <term lang="la">Sulfur uiuum</term>, ought by Dioscorides to be fattye,
greene, not stony: but bright, and shyninge lyke the glowe
worme. Euery <term lang="la">Sulfur</term> (sayeth Galen) hath drawyng power,
is whotte of temperament of nature discussiue, also swiftly
concoctiue, and of so subtile essens, that it layde to, resisteth
the peryls of many venemous woundes: and is a medicine
approued, to heale scabbes, leprosies, scuruines, &#38;  itche. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Theion</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Apyron</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Theion pepy&#173;
romenon</term>. </note>
<note type="m">Dioscor. <term lang="la">lib. 5. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De symplici. 
faculta. lib. 9. </term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="syncope(n)">Syncope. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="paroxysm(n)"><term lang="gk">&#x03A3;&#x03C5;&#x03B3;&#x03BB;&#x03BF;&#x03C0;&#x1F74;</term>, <term lang="la">Id est animi deliquium, uel pr&#230;seps uirium lapsus</term>, that



</page>

<page no="123">
is the defecte of the mynde, or a sodeine slyding away of the
strengthe of the body, and commonly called <term lang="la">swoundynge</term>,
and is caused dyuersly. Somtyme of euel &#38; sharpe iuyce, or
of raw humores, about <expan type="">the</expan> mouth of the stomache: of vnme&#173;
surable bledyng, swetyng, &#38; fluxe of the belly, great peyne,
vehem<expan type="">en</expan>t mouing, immoderate watche, or emptines: of ter&#173;
ror also, and feare, and of suche lyke perturbations of the
mynde, and conuersation in stynkyng ayre. Whose sygnes
are rare and obscure pulse, coldnes of the extreme partes,
sweat of the face, the skynne of the same losynge his bewy&#173;
tie, and almoste a resolution of the whole body: which must
be cured as variably as the causes efficient are dyuerse. 

Howe be it, the paroxisme presente, is after the mynde of
the learned, (led by reason and knowledge) Thus to be suc&#173;
curred. First throw into his face violently rose water, with
vineger, or cold fountayne water: wherby the astonied spi&#173;
rites maye be reuyued: And that in Sommer, a burnynge
feuer or the cause whotte. The extreame partes ought also 
in eruptions to be bounde. And rubbe the legges, if the e&#173;
ruption issue aboue: the armes if beneathe. But principal&#173;
ly and with spede, are the nosethrilles, and the mouthe to
be stopped (and as some wyll also the eares:) That the ayer
alreddy drawne in, and passinge into the inwarde partes,
and by and by is ready to issue out, So made whotte by of&#173;
ten mouyng to &#38; fro, may goe agayne vnto the harte: that
by heatyng the same, the spirites, may be quickned and re&#173;
uiued. And then forthwith is it expedient, to reuocate the
mynde with thynges odoriferous. For nothyng by the te&#173;
stimony of Hypocrates, <term lang="la">lib. de alimento</term>, doth sooner call againe 
the spirites. &#38;c. By the whiche, and by bowying moderatly
the body: I haue often brought again, euen such as all men
presente haue thought dead. And thus much, because Lan&#173;
franke appointeth th<expan type="">em</expan> to be reuiued, by the noyses of Tr<expan type="">um</expan>&#173;
pettes &#38; tympanies: Which (if they were necessary) yet are
they instrum<expan type="">en</expan>tes, at so sodeine exigentes to vnredy, &#38; to call
the person loude by his owne name. Whiche truly I iudge



</page>

<page no="124">
as vayne and rydiculous, (or worse) as the strokes and buf&#173;
fettes, that the rude multitude vse. For if it be (as by the
authoritie of Galen aboue, and as we see it is) the defecte of 
the mynde, a losse of strengthe, and a generall depriuation
of sense: what can the eares doe more by hearyng, then the
eyes by seyng. Or rather, howe can the eares heare more
then the eyes see: or the handes and other partes of the bo&#173;
dy feele. But this notwithstanding, I denie it not, but that
the sodeyne noyse of trumpettes or drummes, may in the 
tymes of great incisions, and suche like daungerous enter&#173;
pryses, both reteyne and quicken so the spirites (not yet
loste,) that swoundyng may therby be euited, wherof also I
haue had the experience. </xpln>
<note type="m">Galenus <term lang="la">de me
thodo me. li. 12</term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="synochus(n)">Synochus. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="fever(n)">THose feuers (sayeth Galen) that haue one only acces&#173;
sion or fitte, continuyng from the beginnynge to the
ende, and that many daies, are called <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03CD;&#x03BD;&#x03BF;&#x03C7;&#x03BF;&#x03B9;</term>, <term lang="la">id est co<damage>n</damage>&#173;
tinentes, seu continu&#230;</term>, whose cause efficient is yelowe choler,
and is of nature double. The one hauyng a manifest putre&#173;
faction, and is therfore called <term lang="la">Synochus putrida</term>: and the other
vtterly without <expan type="">the</expan> same, called <term lang="la">Synochus non putrida</term>, whych is
a kynde of diarie feuer: (and are of Lanfranke, and dyuers
other for distinction sake, called <term lang="la">Synoch&#230;</term>. ) And because the
remission of the fitte is wonte to chance, by the transpira&#173;
tion or breathyng out of feruent humores, (for they neuer
breathe out at all, in vehement stipations,) A fytte of many
dayes must nedes be made. When in suche feuers there is 
no occasion, that may rayse the begynnynge of an other ac&#173;
cession: It persisteth a feuer, both of many daies, and of one
accession, without the inuasion of an other: neyther refer&#173;
ryng quotidian, tertian, nor quartan. For as much therfor,
as of these twoo, a feuer may be maynteyned: both that all
the vapor may not passe out, and that the same may heate
whatsoeuer it toucheth: There must nedes be three diffe&#173;



</page>

<page no="125">
rences of <term lang="la">Synochus</term>. For some continue from the begynnyng
to the ende, of one greatnes: and are called in Greke <term lang="gk">&#x03C0;&#x03C5;&#x03C1;&#x03B5;&#x03C4;&#x03BF;&#x03B9; 
&#x03BF;&#x03BC;&#x03BF;&#x03C4;&#x03CC;&#x03BD;&#x03BF;&#x03B9; &#x03BA;&#x03B1;&#x03B9; &#x1F00;&#x03BA;&#x03BC;&#x03B1;<expan type="">&#x03C3;&#x03C4;</expan>&#x03B9;&#x03BA;&#x03BF;&#x03AF;</term>, <term lang="la">hoc est eodem uigore permanentes</term>. Others
doe augmente and still adde to somwhat, called <term lang="gk">&#x1F00;&#x03BD;&#x03B1;&#x03B2;&#x03B1;&#x03C4;&#x03B9;&#x03BA;&#x03BF;&#x1F76;
&#x03AE;&#x03B5;&#x03C0;&#x03B1;&#x03BA;&#x03BC;&#x03B1;<expan type="">&#x03C3;&#x03C4;</expan>&#x03B9;&#x03BA;&#x03BF;&#x03B9;</term>, <term lang="la">quasi incrememtum sucipientes</term>. And some demi&#173;
nishe by a litle and litle, whiche they call <term lang="gk">&#x03C0;&#x03B1;&#x03C1;&#x03B1;&#x03BA;&#x03BC;&#x03B1;&#x03C3;&#x03C4;&#x03B9;&#x03BA;&#x03BF;&#x03C5;&#x03C2;</term>, <term lang="la">Id
est decrescentes</term>. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De methodo
medendi . li. 9. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Synochos</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Synochus pu&#173;
trida</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Synochus non
putrida</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De differ<expan type="">en</expan>tiis
febrium. lib. 2. 
cap. 2. &#38;  ulti. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Pyreti. 
Homotom</term>, <term lang="la">uel
Acmastici</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Anabatici</term> <term lang="la">siue
epacmastici. </term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Paracmastici. </term>. </note>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Tamar indus. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="date tree(n)"><term lang="gk">&#x039F;&#x039E;&#x03C5;&#x03C6;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;&#x03B9;&#x03BE;</term>, is called in Latin <term lang="la">Syluestris palma</term>, that is
the wylde or sower Date tree, whose fruite is
called of the Arabians <term lang="ar">Tamarindus</term>, and maye bee
called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x039F;&#x03BE;&#x03C5;&#x03C6;&#x03BF;&#x03B9;&#x03BD;&#x03AF;&#x03BE;</term>, and of some <term lang="gk">&#x039F;&#x03BE;&#x03C5;&#x03C6;&#x03BF;&#x03B9;&#x03BD;&#x03B9;&#173;
&#x03B7;&#x03BF;&#x03BF;&#x03B2;&#x03AC;&#x03BB;&#x03B1;&#x03BD;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>, <term lang="la">quasi fructus uel dactilus palm&#230; acetos&#230;, uel syluestris</term>. 
Whiche last hath not withstanding some learned aduersa&#173;
ries. But their opinion semeth moste false, that thynke <term lang="la">Ta&#173;
mar indum</term> and <term lang="la">Myrobalanon</term> to be all one: for <term lang="la">Tamar Indus</term> hath a 
curnell or stone, whiche <term lang="la">Myrabolani</term> haue not. 

They are sayth Mesues, sharpe or sower dates (like saith
Siluius, vnto the sede of <term lang="la">Cassia</term>) and the fruytes of the wild
Indian date tree: A medicine excellent and hurtlesse. Of 
the olde aunci<expan type="">en</expan>t writers eyther vnknowne, or vnder these
names vnmensioned, colde and drie in the seconde degree:
After Auerois in the thirde. By the whiche reason, it repres&#173;
seth the sharpnes of humores, purgeth choler, and mitiga&#173;
teth the feruoure therof, and also of the bloude: &#38; healeth
the iaundies, and sharpe continuall feuers. It stayeth vo&#173;
miting, quencheth thyrste, and euery burnyng of the lyuer
and stomache. But to colde stomaches, it must not be geu<expan type="">en</expan>
alone. The beste <term lang="la">Tamar indi</term> should be freshe, fatty, syncere,
and tender, in taste sharpe swete, of colour blackyshe, and
shynynge, myxed with heares, as it ware with certeyne
rootes. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Oxyphoenix</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Oxyphoenico&#173;
balanos</term>. </note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la" lexeme="terebinthia(n)">Terebinthina. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="mastic(n)" lexeme="terebinthine(n)"><term lang="gk">&#x03A4;&#x0395;&#x03C1;&#x03BC;&#x03B9;&#x03BD;&#x03B8;&#x03AF;&#x03BD;&#x03B7; &#x03C1;&#x03AE;&#x03C4;&#x03AF;&#x03BD;&#x03B7;</term>, <term lang="la">Resina terebinthina</term>, is the liquide g<expan type="">um</expan>me
of the tree <term lang="la">Terebinthus</term>, among the Greekes called <term lang="gk">&#x03C4;&#x03AD;&#x03C1;&#173;
&#x03BC;&#x03B9;&#x03BD;&#x03B8;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>, and sometyme <term lang="gk">&#x03C4;&#x03B5;&#x03C1;&#x03AD;&#x03B2;&#x03B9;&#x03BD;&#x03B8;&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</term>: The leaues and
flowres wherof are drye, and bynde and heate in the sec<expan type="">on</expan>de 
degree. Terebinthin sayth Galen, is preferred am<expan type="">on</expan>g other
gummes, hauyng a certeyne adstriction: but not so mani&#173;
fest as hath <term lang="la">Mastiche</term>. But it hath a certein bytternes, wher&#173;
by it digesteth and scoureth, more then <term lang="la">Mastiche</term>. It purgeth
strayte passages, and draweth from depthe more then the
reste: and because it is of qualities moste moderate, it is
moste safely admyxed with medicines that heale vlcers,
whiche should be without byting. But neyther is this our
Terebynthine, whiche is the gumme of the tree <term lang="la">Laryx</term>, and
called <term lang="la">Larigna</term>, (not without his specyall vertues,) nor yet as
some suspect the finest that we haue, callyng it <term lang="la">Terebinthinam
Venetam</term>: For it is (as many learned affirme) A resine ta&#173;
ken out of the rynde of the younge whyte fyrre tree, called
<term lang="la">Abies</term>, and was of the auncientes called <term lang="la">Resina oleosa</term>. The de&#173;
termination wherof I referre vnto them againe. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="terra sigillata(n)">Terra sigillata. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="earth(n)">Earth called of the Latins <term lang="la">Terra</term>, in Greke <term lang="gk">&#x0393;&#x1F23;&#x03BA;&#x03B9; &#x03C7;&#x03B8;&#x03CE;&#x03BD;</term>, is
vsually that, whiche beinge put in water dissolueth: and is 
diuers kyndes. For so muche as the body of the earthe is of 
nature drie, &#38; vtterly voyde of fyrie substance, euery earth
drieth without bityng: Wherof many, (namely such as are 
mete for medicine) are in Dioscorides treated of at large. 
Among the which <term lang="gk">&#x0393;&#x03BD;&#x03BB;&#x03B7;&#x03BC;&#x03BD;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;</term>, <term lang="la">Id est terra lemnia</term>, (sayth he) gro&#173;
weth in holes, lyke cunny borowes, and is broughte from
a fenny place out of the Isle Lemno. which the inhabitantes
gather, and myxing it with goates bloude, they make it in
lytle cakes or loues, and signe it with the image of a gote: &#38;
therfore call it <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03C6;&#x03C1;&#x03B1;&#x03B3;&#x03AF;&#x03B4;&#x03B1; &#x1F00;&#x03B9;&#x03B3;&#x1F78;&#x03C2;</term>, <term lang="la">Hoc est sigill<expan type="">um</expan> capr&#230;</term>, or rather



</page>

<page no="127">
as Galen wil, (who of purpose to se it, visited the place abo&#173;
ue sayde) because it is impressed with the sacred signet of Di
ana: but nowe is it marked with the Turkyshe sygnet. 
He maketh therof three differences. The firste he also cal&#173;
leth <term lang="la">Lemniam phragidon</term>, whiche it was not lawfull for any
to touche, but their women priestes, and differeth from <term lang="la">L<expan type="">em</expan>&#173;
nia rubrica</term>, called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03BB;&#x03B7;&#x03BC;&#x03BD;&#x03AF;&#x03B1; &#x03BC;&#x03AF;&#x03BB;&#x03C4; <expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>, <term lang="la">It est terra sigillata
rubea</term>,) in that it defileth not the fingers when it is touched,
as <term lang="la">Lemnia rubrica</term> doth: and is of colour yelowe, lyke the hyll
in Lemno. wheron there is neither stones, trees, or plantes,
growyng: So muche sayeth he is this earth visited. To the
thyrd, he geueth neither name nor description, but sayeth
that it hath scouring faculties. The other two ar of driyng
facultie, and are vsed for the <term lang="la">Dysenteria</term>, against the venime
of beastes, or hurtfull medicines, and also for all olde and
malignant vlcers. </xpln>

<form lang="la">Tertiana febris. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="tertian(n)" lexeme="quartan(n)" lexeme="paroxysm(n)">THe tertian feuer, is eyther continuall, and called <term lang="la">Ter&#173;

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="thapsia(n)">Thapsia. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="gk">&#x03B4;&#x03B1;&#x03C8;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;</term>, so called (sayeth Dioscorides) because it was firste
founde in the Isle Thapso, is an herbe lyke to <term lang="la">Ferula</term>, in his
whole nature, hauyng a sclenderer stalke, with leues lyke
fenel. And on euery branche a spokye toppe lyke to Dylle,
with yellowe flowers, and a sede lyke <term lang="la">Ferula</term>, but broder
somwhat, and lesse: hauyng also a long roote, blacke with&#173;
out, and whyte within: sharp also, and couered with a thick
rynde. The rynde and the milky iuyce therof, dooe purge
choler, but is perylous to take without great prouydence. 
It is by the testimonie of Galen, of a sharpe, and a myghty
heatyng facultie ioyned with moisture: And therfore dra&#173;
weth violently from the depth, and that which it draweth
it digesteth. The iuyce hereof, (whiche is by arte gathered
from the roote) is so vehem<expan type="">en</expan>t sharpe, that the very breathe
of the same, maketh the face and other naked partes of th<expan type="">em</expan>
that gather it, to swell excedyngly, and to ryse in pustules. </xpln>
<note type="m">Dioscor. <term lang="la">lib. 4</term></note>

stris nominatur</term></note>

causis. lib. 6. </term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="thus(n)">Thus. </form> 
<xpln lexeme="incense(n)" lexeme="mastic(n)">INcence (called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x03BB;&#x03AF;&#x03B2;&#x03B1;&#x03BD;&#x03C9;&#x03C4;&#x03CC;&#x03C2;</term>) as well as the tree,
wherof it commeth (called <term lang="gk">&#x03BB;&#x03AF;&#x03B2;&#x03B1;&#x03BD;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>,) is called in Latyne

foure sortes. The best sayeth he, is <term lang="la">Thus masculinum</term>, of his

&#x03BD;&#x03BF;&#x03C2; <expan type="">&#x03C3;&#x03C4;</expan>&#x03B1;&#x03B3;&#x03C9;&#x03BD;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;&#x03C2;</term>, whyte, vndeuisyble, fatte within when it is
broken, and burneth spedely: hereof at <term lang="la">Oliban<expan type="">um</expan></term>. That of In&#173;
dia is of swarte and reddishe colour, and is made rounde by
industrie: as first by cutting it in square peces, &#38; then tour&#173;
nyng it in vesselles till it waxe rounde, but in tyme it wax&#173;

The second place hath <term lang="la">Thus arabic<expan type="">um</expan></term>, growing in Smilo: which
some call <term lang="la">Copiscum</term>, of colour darke yellowe. An other kinde

to the fyngers, as doth masticke. 

<term lang="la">Cortex thuris</term>, whiche is the rynde of the Incense tree, is
here also to be remembred: which ought to be new, thicke, 
fatty, swete smellyng, not rough, &#38;  without skinnes. Here&#173;

place. <term lang="la">Thus</term> heateth (sayeth Galen) in the second degree, dri&#173;

in the seconde degree, and byndeth euidently. It is of gros&#173;
ser partes then <term lang="la">Thure</term>, hauing litle sharpnes: and therfore is
vsed for the griefes of the stomache, spitting of bloud, fluxe
of the belly, and <term lang="la">Dysenteria</term>. <term lang="la">Thus</term> mollifieth, swageth peyne, 
c<expan type="">on</expan>cocteth, and bredeth quitture. But that in temperate na&#173;
tures: for in moyste bodies it ingendreth fleshe. It putteth 
away the dymnesse of syghte, purgeth, filleth, and healeth,
the vlcers of the eyes, and all other hollowe vlcers: glueth



</page>

<page no="130">
bloudy woundes, and stayeth all eruptions of bloud. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Thiria. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="gk">&#x039F;&#x03C6;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;&#x03C3;&#x03B9;&#x03C2;</term> is a disease of the head, and hath sayeth Fuchsius
(in his first boke <term lang="la">De medendis morbis</term>) no latin name, but is cal&#173;
led of the Arabians and barbarous phisiciens <term lang="ar">Tyria</term>. By the
whiche worde they vnderstande all serpentes, but chiefly
the Viper: whiche therfore I thynke to come of this greke
worde <term lang="gk">&#x03B8;&#x03B5;&#x03C1;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;</term>, and so of that kynde of leprosie, whiche is a&#173;
mong that sorte called <term lang="la">Theriasis</term> as aboue at <term lang="la">Serpigo</term>. 

Euen as plantes sayeth Galen are of twoo causes vitiate,
namely some by the vtter lacke of nuryshyng iuyce, dried &#38;
withered: other by the same c<expan type="">on</expan>trarie to their own natures,
are infected: so are the beares through the vtter defecte of
their slymie nutritiue iuyce, and also by the same vitiate
corrupted. For through the vtter lacke therof commeth
<term lang="la">Caluities</term>, but of the same corrupted, riseth <term lang="gk">&#x03B1;&#x03BB;&#x03C9;&#x03C0;&#x03B5;&#x03BA;&#x1F35;&#x03B1;</term>, and
<term lang="gk">&#x039F;&#x03C6;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;&#x03C3;&#x03B9;&#x03C2;</term>. And as in <term lang="la">Alopecia</term> the falling of the heare is circu&#173;
lare, changing into a golden colour or like the foxe, (wherof
it hath that name, <term lang="gk">&#x03AC;&#x03BB;&#x03CE;&#x03C0;&#x03B7;&#x03BE;</term>, <term lang="la">quid<expan type="">em</expan> uulpes est:</term> so in <term lang="la">Ophiasi</term>, <expan type="">the</expan> af&#173;
fected parte of the head is pilde, after the maner of Ser&#173;
pentes, receyuing therof also his name: <term lang="la">Orphis enim serpens est. </term></xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Ophiasis</term> <term lang="la">uel de med<expan type="">en</expan>di 
methodo</term>. </note>
