<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="tragacanth(n)">Tragacantha. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="gk">&#x03A4;&#x03C1;&#x03B1;&#x03B3;&#x03B1;&#x03BA;&#x03AC;&#x03BD;&#x03B8;&#x03B1;</term>, is a bright shyning gumme, leane, lyght,
sincere, and swetishe: whiche runneth out of the roote of a
pricky shrubbe, called also <term lang="la">Tragacantha</term>. whose roote is broad,
wooddy, &#38;  stiffe in the toppe of the turfe: From the which
doe strong lowe branches spreade very broade: wheron do
growe many smale thinne leaues, hydinge vnder them
selues whyte, stiffe, and streight thornes. The gumme,
whiche we call commonly <term lang="la">Dragagantum</term>, stoppeth the poores
of the skinne, as gumme doth: whose most vse hath been in
medicines for the eies, for the cough, for horsenes, &#38;  rough&#173;



</page>

<page no="131">
nes of the pypes and yawes, and other distillations against
peyne of the reines, and corrosions of the bladder. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Triticum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="wheat(n)"><term lang="la">WHeate</term> called in Greeke <term lang="gk"> &#x0393;&#x03C5;&#x03C1;&#x03CC;&#x03C2;</term>, is after Galen, of fyrme
nurishment, and bredeth a iuyce grosse and tough, and 
therfore is <term lang="la">Alica</term> (whiche also nurisheth strongly) forbidden 
in them that are apte to breede the stone, or to haue obstru&#173;
ctions in the liuer: exterially vsed, it heateth in the first de&#173;
gree, but neither can it drie nor moiste manifestly. What&#173;
soeuer is made of <term lang="la">Amylum</term>, is then it, both colder and drier. 
The <term lang="la">Cataplasma</term> that is made with bread, digesteth more th<expan type="">en</expan> 
with wheate, by reason of the salte and leuen: whiche hath
power to drawe vp and digeste, thinges that lye depe. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Pyros</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De attenu<expan type="">an</expan>te
uictus ratione</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De symp. li. 8. </term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="turbith(n)">Turbith. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="date tree(n)"><term lang="la">TVrbith</term> is estemed a barbarous name, geu<expan type="">en</expan> to a roote,
whiche is called <term lang="la">Trupetum</term>, wherof among all menne,
there is no small ambiguitie, nor a fewe opinions. 

For Syluius and Manardus, doe make therof a destinction,
in . iiii. sortes, whiche I thoughte good thus to gather. 

<term lang="la">Turbith siue
Turpetum. </term>
The one, whiche some 
call <term lang="la">Turbith Dioscoridis</term>. 
<term lang="la">Radix pityus&#230;</term>, whiche hath the leaues
of <term lang="la">Pinus</term>. 
<term lang="la">Serapionis. i. Radix Tripolii Dioscoridis. </term>
<term lang="la">Vulgare Radix Tithymali foemin&#230; siue myrtites</term>, so called be&#173;
cause it hath leaues like to <term lang="la">Myrtus</term>. Or <term lang="la">Radix Alipi</term>, 
after Matthiolus under the autorities of Actuarius,
who calleth it <term lang="la">Turpetum album</term>. 
<term lang="la">Mesu&#230;</term> Is the roote of an herbe, whose leaues are like <term lang="la">Fe<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
rula</term>: wherof thei dare not geue sentence. Howbeit
Brasauolus iudgeth it the roote of <term lang="la">Tithymali myrsmites</term>
but that would Matthiolus refute: who semeth by
reasons to proue that <term lang="la">Turbith Mesu&#230;</term> &#38;  <term lang="la">actuarii</term> is
none other thynge then the roote of <term lang="la">Alipi&#230;</term>. And
so of our common <term lang="la">Turpetum</term>. 



</page>

<page no="132">
Wherfore knowyng that there are at this daie, not a fewe
diligent men in this kinde of studie: namely in the serching
out of suche vnknowne symples, &#38; the certeintie of thinges
so vncerteyne and doubtful: (least they should wante a iust
occasion, to examine suche conditions of plantes or rootes,
as shall in anywyse be lyke to any of these. ) I haue set here 
(for the sakes chiefly of the meanly learned) the seuerall de&#173;
scriptions of the kindes of Turbith here m<expan type="">en</expan>cioned: Namly
suche as haue not before in the Englyshe tongue been pu&#173;
blyshed. For <term lang="la">Pytiusa</term> and <term lang="la">Tithymalus myrtites</term>, are of D. Tur&#173;
ner right well described. Whiche therfore I touche not. 

<term lang="la">Turbith Serapionis siue Tripolium, Gr&#230;ce</term> <term lang="gk">&#x03C4;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;&#x03C0;&#x03CC;&#x03BB;&#x03B9;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, groweth by
the sea sides, in places that are by the tydes washed, and by
the ebbes left againe: so that it groweth neither in the sea,
nor on the drie grounde: with a lefe like to <term lang="la">Glastum</term>, but thic&#173;
ker, and a stalke lyke the Date tree, deuided in the toppe. 
whose floures (as men saye,) doe thrice in one daye change
their colours: beynge whyte in the morninge, purple at
noone, and crimson at nyght: whose rote is whyte, odor<emend err="f">i</emend>fe&#173;
rous, and whotte in taste. 

Of the whiche, two dragmes drunke in wyne, draweth
out water and vrine by the belly, and is put into medicines
that resiste venym. 

<term lang="gk">&#x1F04;&#x03BB;&#x03C5;&#x03C0;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, in Latin <term lang="la">Alypum</term> or <term lang="la">Alypia</term>, is a bushie and reddish
herbe with sclender stalkes and thinne leaues: hauinge a
softe thinne flowre, and of them plentie, &#38; a sclender roote
lyke the roote of <term lang="la">Beta</term>, full of sharpe iuyce, with seede lyke
the sedes of <term lang="la">Epithymi</term>: whiche purgeth blacke choler, if it be
taken with an equall portion of <term lang="la">Epithymum</term>, with salte and
vineger: but it doth a litle exulcerate the intraelles. 

It groweth in places nighe the sea, chiefly and most pl<expan type="">en</expan>&#173;
tifully in Libya: though much of it doe also grow els where. 
<term lang="la">Turbith</term> sayeth Mesues, is a mylky herbe, hauynge leaues
lyke <term lang="la">Ferula</term> but lesse: wherof there is <term lang="la">Hortense</term> and <term lang="la">Syluestre</term>, of
the whiche also some is great, and some little: whyte (also)
yelowe, ashe coloured or blacke. Praysinge that whiche is 



</page>

<page no="133">
Gummie, white, empty, and like a reede, with a plaine and
ashy rynde, or as it weare baked vnder ashes, beyng mean&#173;
ly newe and easy to be broken: but that saieth he, whiche is
not gummie, is weake and troubleth the belly: dyspraysing
also the yelowe and the blacke , the grosse and the leane. 

The curled or wrynkled rynded also, whiche is as it
were sinewy within when it is broken, which is the wilde. 
The olde is weake: The newe dothe lesse trouble and o&#173;
uerturne the bowelles. Some doe adulterate it, by anoyn&#173;
ting the rynde outwardly with melted oyle: but being bro&#173;
ken, it is not gummy within. 

And where as the roote of <term lang="la">Thapsia</term> is numbered amonge
the sortes of <term lang="la">Turbith</term>: whiche the Apothecaries thinke to be 
theyrs. Andreas Marinus, calleth it <term lang="la">Turbith apulum</term>, affirming it
to be that whiche mesues calleth <term lang="la">Crassum</term>. The fraude wher
of beynge at the laste discouered, it was reiected &#38; put oute
of vse: for though it purge phlegme as doth the right <term lang="la">Tur&#173;
bith</term>, yet that doth it violently, and with the great damage
of the principall members. Whose faculties you may far&#173;
ther reade before vnder the propre title therof. 

Mesues of his <term lang="la">Turbith</term> sayeth, it is whotte in the thirde
degree, purgeth meanly by attraction, rayseth wyndes,
that ouerturnethe stomache <term lang="la">ad nauseam</term>: and dryeth, being
purged from the thinne excrementes therof. <term lang="la">Turbith</term> cor&#173;
rected draweth out grosse, toughe, and putrefied phlegme,
from the breste, the stomache, and the sinewes: Euen from 
the ioyntes and farthest partes. 
</xpln>
<note type="m">Dioscor. <term lang="la">lib. 4. </term></note>
<note type="m">Dioscor. <term lang="la">lib. 4</term></note></wordentry>

<form lang="en" lexeme="verbena(n)">Verbena. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="la">VErbena</term> or <term lang="la">Verbenaca</term>, is of two kindes, described of Dios&#173;
corides in two seuerall chapiters, callyng them both 



</page>

<page no="134">
<term lang="gk">&#x03C0;&#x03B5;&#x03C1;&#x03B9;<expan type="">&#x03C3;&#x03C4;</expan>&#x03B5;&#x03C1;&#x03B5;&#x03C9;&#x03BD;</term>, but the seconde <term lang="gk">&#x03B9;&#x03B5;&#x03C1;&#x03B1;&#x03BD; &#x03B2;&#x03BF;&#x03C4;&#x03AC;&#x03BD;&#x03B7;&#x03BD;</term>, <term lang="la">Hoc est sacram her&#173;

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Vermes terrestres. </form> 
<xpln lang="en"><term lang="la">VErmes terreni</term>, called also <term lang="la">Lumbrici</term> &#38; <term lang="la">Intestin&#230; terr&#230;</term>, beyng
drunke in <term lang="la">passo</term>, doe prouoke vrine, heale the ia<expan type="">un</expan>dise,
and driue away tertian feuers: brused and layde to
cutte synewew, they glewe them together merueylouslye,
and are good for the crampe. Being boiled <term lang="la">cum adipe anserino,</term>
and poured in, they heale the peynes of the eares: If they
be boyled in oyle, and put into the contrarie eare, they help
the peyne of the teethe. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Vernix. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="juniper(n)" lexeme="tree(n)" lexeme="sandarac(n)">OVt of the Iuniper tree sweateth certeine teares, in
the spring tyme chiefly: and therfore called <term lang="la">Vernix, 
quasi Vernus ros</term>, beyng of the colour and smell of fran&#173;
kinsence: Whiche hardened by heate, bec<expan type="">om</expan>meth a gumme. 
Whiche sayth Fuchsius, the Arabians, not without a great
confusion of names,) haue called <term lang="ar">Sandaracham</term>, whiche sayeth Serapio, is whotte and drie, harde, and somewhat bitter: and
is brought from the lande of the Christians. And the Apo&#173;
thecaries cal it <term lang="la">Vernicem</term>. But farre is it from <term lang="gk">&#x03A3;&#x03B1;&#x03BD;&#x03B4;&#x03B1;&#x03C1;&#x03AC;&#x03C7;&#x03BA;</term> of 
the Grecians and Latines: whiche is a stone, founde sayeth
Plinie, in the mynes of golde and syluer. 

Brasauolus sayeth, that <term lang="la">Sandaracha</term> is nothynge els but the
redde partes of <term lang="la">Auripigmentum</term>, so made by the more natural
boylyng: For there may a kinde of <term lang="en">Sandaracha</term> be made, by the
artificiall boylyng of <term lang="la">Auripigmentum</term>. And therfore may the
one, (namely <term lang="la">Sandaracha</term>,) be called <term lang="la">Arsenicum rubrum</term>, and the
other (that is <term lang="la">Auripigmentum</term>) <term lang="la">Arsenicum Croceum</term>. 

Matthiolus also sayeth, that <term lang="la">Sandaracha</term> and <term lang="la">Auripigmentum</term>,



</page>

<page no="135">
are both of one kynde and facultie, neyther differ they in 
any thynge, but only that the one, (to wytte <term lang="la">Sandaracha</term>) is
more concocted in the bowelles of the earthe then the o&#173;
ther, whiche is <term lang="la">Auripigmentum</term>: and are therfore by the same 
reason in subtilties of vertues, more excellent the one then
the other. 

Dioscorides the surest grounde herein) sayeth, that <term lang="la">Auri&#173;
pigmentum</term> is founde in the same mines that <term lang="la">Sandaracha</term> is, a&#173;
lowyng that whiche is fyerie redde, or lyke to <term lang="la">Cinnaban</term>:
playne, pure, brittle, and smellynge lyke <term lang="la">Sulphur</term>. 

It is geuen <term lang="la">cum mulso</term>, to them that spytte rott<expan type="">en</expan> phlegme. 
The smoke hereof is taken in, agaynste the olde coughe. 
Lycked in with hony, it cleareth the voyce: and in pylles
is geuen, to harde breathyng. Vnto whiche vertues, not
only Plinie, and other Latines doe agree of the same: But
also the Arabians, the more to augmente their error, of
their gummy <term lang="la">Sandarach&#230;</term>: twoo thynges bothe in substance
and nature, (as you see) moste contrarie. 

Wherfore, when you shall among the Greeke authors
fynde <term lang="la">Sandaracha</term>, you muste vnderstande the same that Di&#173;
oscorides wryteth of, called afore <term lang="la">Arsenicum rubrum</term>: But if
among the Arabian wryters, you must take <term lang="la">Gummi iuniperi</term>. 

An other kynde of artificiall or adulterate <term lang="la">Sandaracha</term>
there is, made of <term lang="la">Seruffa</term> burned in the fyre, tyll it haue ch<expan type="">an</expan>&#173;
ged his colour, and is called <term lang="la">Sandix</term>: wherof Plinie maketh
mencion. 

<term lang="la">Vernix</term> out of the later wryters hath the facultie to staie
the fluxe of bloode at the nose: if it be layde to the forehead,
<term lang="la">cum Candido oui</term>. And taken with the poulder of Frankin&#173;
sence, or the poulder therof supped in a rere egge, suppres&#173;
seth vomityng: and layde to, or taken with the same, fir&#173;
meth the laxatyue bellye, defendeth the fallynge of destil&#173;
lacions, dygesteth the phlegme in the bowelles, and kyl&#173;
leth wormes, and other lyuynge thynges in the same. 



</page>

<page no="136">
<term lang="la">Mensium fluxus continet</term>: beyng put in, it dryeth the moiste ho&#173;
lownesses of fistules: and helpeth the ryftes, cones, or 
chappinges, of the handes and feete. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Sandarache</term>. </note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Vinum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="wine(n)"><term lang="la">OVlde wyne</term> hurteth the synewes, and other wynes
the senses: though they be in taste swete. And ther&#173;
fore ought they to be ware therof, that fele weake&#173;
nes in any of their bowelles: But beyng taken in smal qu<expan type="">an</expan>&#173;
titie accordyng to strengthe and delayed, it is vnhurtfull. 
Newe wyne inflateth, doth hardly concocte, and breedeth
many vayne dreames: amd moueth vrine. That whiche is
of meane age, wanteth both the fourmer faultes: and ther&#173;
fore is vsed in the diet, both of sicke and whole. The white
and thynne wyne, is to the stomache profitable, and easily
dispersed into the members. Black wyne is grosse, hard to
concocte, nourisheth fleshe, and causeth dronkennes. Yel&#173;
low wyne, as it holdeth a middle colour: so hath it faculties
meane betwene both. But when health quaileth, or is cra&#173;
sed, the whyte wyne hath moste prayse. Wynes also differ
in sauoure. For swete wyne as it c<expan type="">on</expan>sisteth of grosse partes,
so doth it difficilly breath out of the body, inflateth the sto&#173;
mache, and troubleth the belly and bowelles, as doth newe
wyne: but moueth dronkennes lesse, and is moste apte for
the reynes &#38;  bladder. Tarte wyne doth more swiftly passe
through by the vryne: but maketh peyne in the heade, and
bredeth dronkennes. Sharpe wyne is for the digestyng of 
meates into the membres, moste commodious: restreyneth
the belly and other fluxes, &#38;c. Wyne saieth Galen, gleweth
together holownesses, and is a conuenient medicine for all
vlcers. The Greekes doe call it <term lang="gk">&#x039F;&#x03B9;&#x03BD;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term> </xpln>
<note type="m">Dioscor. <term lang="la">li. 4. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De methodo
medendi. li. 3. </term>
<term lang="la">De arte cura&#173;
tiua. lib. 2</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Oenon uel
Inon</term>. </note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="viola(n)">Viola. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="violet(n)"><term lang="la">Viola</term>, so writt<expan type="">en</expan> symplie without an adiectiue, is alwayes 



</page>

<page no="137">
taken for the blewe or purple violet, called in Greke <term lang="gk">&#x0399;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;
&#x03C0;&#x03BF;&#x03C1;&#x03C6;&#x03C5;&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, <term lang="la">Id est Viola nigra</term> or <term lang="la">pupurea</term>: and of some <term lang="la">Viola mura&#173;
ria</term>. Some holde that it is called <term lang="gk">&#x0399;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, because the nymphes
called <term lang="la">Ion&#230;</term>, gaue first the floure therof vnto Iupiter as a 
gyfte. Others because when Iupiter had turned the Nym&#173;
phe Io into a cowe, the earth gaue furth violet floures, as a 
pasture or foode for the same. But many other herbes ther
are, that are called <term lang="la">Violae</term>, wherof here to speake weare as
nedelesse as superfluous. Mesues geueth to violettes, cold&#173;
nes and moystnes in the first degree. They are somnife&#173;
rous, purge choler, staye thyrst, and quenche all inflamma&#173;
tions: and therfore are applyed, for the burnynge heate of 
the mouth of the stomache and the eyes. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Viride &#230;s. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="brass(n)">IS called also <term lang="la">Aerugo</term>, that is the ruste of brasse. Of this 
Dioscorides maketh two kyndes. The one is called <term lang="la">Aeru&#173;
go rasa</term> or <term lang="la">rasilis</term>, and is made sayeth he, by coueryng a ves&#173;
sell of moste strong vineger, with a cauldern or other lyke
vessell, or <expan type="w+c">with</expan> plates of brasse, so close that no ayre may passe
between: whiche after the tenth daye beyng opened, that <term lang="la">Aerugo</term> that is, must be scraped or rubbed of, &#38;c. Otherwyse
dyuersly. The other he calleth <term lang="la">Aeruginem scoletiam</term>, and no&#173;
teth therof two sortes. The one he called <term lang="la">Fossil<expan type="">em</expan></term>, that is dig<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
ged, for it is taken out of the earth: The other <term lang="la">Factitiam</term>, 
which is made by beatyng or labouring the sharpest white
vineger, in a brasen morter, with a copper pestell, till it re&#173;
ceyue a thicknes of filthe: Then must it bee beaten toge&#173;
ther in the Sunne in the canicular dayes, <term lang="la">cum alumine rotun&#173;
do, &#38;  sale fossili, uel marino, uel nitro</term>, tyll it growe to a thicknes,
and receyue the colour of <term lang="la">Aeruginis</term>: and when it becommeth
lyke wormes, it must be kepte. This therfore hath been
called of some <term lang="la">Vernicularis</term>. A fourth kynde also of <term lang="la">Aeruginis</term>,
sayeth he, is made by the goldsmythes, of the vryne of chil&#173;
dren, in a brasen morter, with a pestell of the same: 



</page>

<page no="138">
wherwith they soulder golde. Of the which kyndes of <term lang="la">Aeru
genis scoleti&#230;</term>, <term lang="la">Fossilis</term> is the beste: nexte to that is <term lang="la">Aerugo Derasu</term>:
the thirde place hath <term lang="la">Factitia</term>, whiche yet is moste adstrin&#173;
gent and bytinge <term lang="la">Aerugo aurificum</term>, answereth to <term lang="la">Aerugini ras&#230;</term>. 
Here mayst thou see good reader, the difference betwene
<term lang="la">Aeruginem</term> &#38; <term lang="la">florem &#230;ris</term>, whiche is in his place described. All
the <term lang="la">Aerugines</term>, doe bynde, extenuate, and heate. They amend
the scarres or blottes of the eyes, cause teares, staie creping 
and eatyng sores, and defende woundes from inflammati&#173;
ons. They purge filthie vlcers, &#38;  <term lang="la">cum cera &#38;  oleo</term>, they heale
the same. Put into fistules with <term lang="la">Ammoniaco</term>, in <expan type="">the</expan> maner of a
collyrie, they errode the callous substance of them: and are
profitable for the swellynges, and excressentes of the gum&#173;
mes. The poulder of <term lang="la">Aeruginis</term> (sayeth Galen) put alone into 
vlcers, suffereth them not to putrefie: but it bringeth pein,
and byting, not a little: gnaweth, and maketh infl<expan type="">am</expan>mations. </xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Virulentia. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="ulcer(n)"><term lang="la">VIrulentia uulnera, uel ulcera</term>, virulent or venemous wo<expan type="">un</expan>des 
or vlcers: <term lang="la">a uiro</term>, whiche is venyme or poyson. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Viticella. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="bryony(n)" lexeme="white vine(n)">THe herbe vulgarly called <term lang="la">Viticella</term>, is called in Greke <term lang="gk">&#x03B2;&#x03C1;&#x03C5;&#x03C9;&#x03BD;&#x03AF;&#x03B1; &#x1F24; &#x03C8;&#x03AF;&#x03BB;&#x03C9;&#x03B8;&#x03C1;&#x03BF;&#x03BD; &#x1F24; &#x1F04;&#x03BC;&#x03C0;&#x03B5;&#x03BB;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan> &#x03BB;&#x03B5;&#x03C5;&#x03BA;&#x1F20;</term>, in Latin <term lang="la">Vitis
alba psilothrum</term>, and of the Apothecaries <term lang="la">Brionia</term>: in En&#173;
lyshe <term lang="la">whyte vyne</term> or <term lang="la">brionie</term>. The roote and the iuyce of 
Briony sayeth Mesues) purgeth the brayne, the synewes,
and the breste, of rotten phlegme: moueth vryne, and ope&#173;
neth the obstructions of <expan type="">the</expan> bowelles. But he appointeth the
t<expan type="">em</expan>peram<expan type="">en</expan>t out of Dioscorides, (who yet leaueth all thinges
without degree of temperament,) to heate and drie in the 
thyrde degree. 

They vse sayeth Galen, to eate the braunches therof in
the spryng tyme, as a meate (for the adstriction therof,) ac&#173;



</page>

<page no="139">
ceptable for the stomache. But to that adstriction it is
sharpe, and somewhat bitter: and therby moueth vryne
moderatly. The roote hath a scouring, driyng, and moderat
heating power: and therfore drunke, softneth the harde 
splene, and layde to <term lang="la">cum ficubus</term>, healeth <term lang="la">psoram</term> &#38; <term lang="la">lepram</term>. &#38;c. 
It is somtymes mixed with corrosiue medicines. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Bryonia. 
Psilothron. 
Ampelos leuce</term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De symp. li. 6. </term></note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h"><form lang="la">Vitriolum Romanum. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="unperfect(a)" lexeme="vitriol(n)" lexeme="chalcanthum(n)" lexeme="sory(n)" lexeme="misy(n)">DIoscorides wryting of <term lang="la">Chalcanthum</term>, (which the Grekes
call <term lang="gk">&#x039A;&#x03AC;&#x03BB;&#x03BA;&#x03B1;&#x03BD;&#x03B8;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>, the Arabians <term lang="ar">Zeg</term>, the Latines <term lang="la">Vitrio&#173;
lum</term>, because it shineth lyke glasse,) and <term lang="la">Atramentum sutorium,
ab atro colore</term>: <emend err="">(</emend>because it was vsed to dye leather black,) saith
that in kynde it is but one thynge: but deuydeth it into 
three differences. As one, which congeileth of certeyne hu&#173;
mores, gathered together drop by droppe into certeyne 
holes of the earth: and therfore is called <term lang="gk">&#x03C3;&#x03C4;&#x03B1;&#x03BB;&#x03B1;&#x03BA;&#x03C4;&#x03B9;&#x03BA;&#x03CC;&#x03BC;</term>, <term lang="la">hoc est
Stillatitium</term>. An other is made by a symple reason in caues,
beyng after poured out into certeyne diches that are made
holowe: whiche is properly called <term lang="gk">&#x03C0;&#x03BA;&#x03C5;&#x03C4;&#x03CC;&#x03BD;</term>, <term lang="la">Id est Concreti<expan type="">um</expan></term>. 
The thyrde is called <term lang="la">Coctile</term>, and is wonte to bee made in
Spayne: beyng moste weake and vnprofitable. But a&#173;
mong the late wryters there is not a little confusion of
names therin. For Matthiolus speakyng of the time present,
sayeth, that of <term lang="la">Calcanthum</term> there are twoo kyndes in Hetru&#173;
ria. The one growyng of it selfe in the earth by concretion,
and is vulgarly called <term lang="la">Coppa rosa</term>. The other is arteficiall:
whose chiefest vse is for dyers. whiche is either the better
or the worse, as well for the dispositions of regions and ce&#173;
lestiall climates, as for the mater wherof it is made: But it 
is proued that <term lang="la">Vitriolum Romanum</term>, excelleth the reste of the 
arteficiall kyndes, though it be of weaker or vnperfecter co&#173;
lour. The seconde place hath <term lang="la">Cyprium</term>, but was of the aunci&#173;
entes praysed for the beste. That whiche is brought from
Germanie, is weaker then any that is in Italy. &#38; for that it
hath a blewe coloure, it deceyueth many. 



</page>

<page no="140">
Among the Apothecaries there are twoo kynds: na<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
mely <term lang="la">Vitriolum</term> symply so called, (which sayeth Brasauolus,
some haue falsely called <term lang="la">Cholcothar</term>,) and <term lang="la">Vitriolum Romanum</term>:
(whiche is sayth he, <term lang="la">Antiquorum misy</term>. ) And is harde, lyke vn&#173;
to gould, shyning lyke the starres, and beyng broken, gli&#173;
stereth as with golden sparkes: All the whiche notes he
affirmeth exactly to agree <expan type="w+c">with</expan> <term lang="la">Vitriolo romano</term> of Germanie. 

And for the better vnderstandyng of his mynde therin,
take these notes, as he for the same reason, drew and gaue
forth the same. 

<term lang="la">Vitriolum</term>, <term lang="la">Atramentum</term>, and the Arabike worde <term lang="ar">Zeg</term>, are 
sayeth he, c<expan type="">om</expan>mon or generall names, to all the sortes therof
as they folowe. 

<term lang="gk">&#x03A3;&#x1F63;&#x03C1;&#x03C5;</term> <term lang="la">Antiquorum. i. Zeg rubeum Serapionis.</term> 

<term lang="gk">&#x03C7;&#x03B1;&#x03BB;&#x03BA;&#x03AF;&#x03C4;&#x03B9;&#x03C2;</term> <term lang="la">Antiquorum,
i. Colcothar Serapionis.</term> 
For <expan type="">the</expan> late wryters erre saith he
that cal <term lang="la">Vitriolum</term> <term lang="la">Vstum Colcothar</term>,
whiche ought rather to haue ben 
called <term lang="la">Chalcitis</term>. 

<term lang="gk">&#x039C;&#x03AF;&#x03C3;&#x03C5;</term>
<term lang="la">i. Zeg uiride, Vitriolum
Romanum</term>
But out of Germanie saieth he
ther is brought romain vitriole
whiche is more harde &#38; of ye&#173;
lowe coloure. 

<term lang="gk">&#x039C;&#x03B5;&#x03BB;&#x03B1;&#x03BD;&#x03C4;&#x03B5;&#x03C1;&#x03AF; &#x03B1;</term>. <term lang="la">i. Bities, atramentum metallicum. 
Chalcanthum, atramentum Sutorium, Vitriolum. </term>

Herevnto it is to be noted, that <term lang="la">Chalcanth<expan type="">um</expan></term> doth by l<expan type="">en</expan>gthe
of tyme, or by present byrnyng the same, degenerate into
<term lang="la">Chalcitin</term>: which yet is suche as beyng disolued in water,
wyll agayne become <term lang="la">Chalcanthum</term>. And these names sayeth
he, doe so mutually answere to them selues, that hereby
thou canst not erre. 

Howe be it, (as Matthiolus moste truly sayeth) <term lang="la">Calcitis</term> My&#173;
sy &#38;  Sory, haue ben long vnknowne, neither is ther almost
any that can saye that he hath sene those very thynges: yet
doth the sayde Matthiolus reporte, that it chanced him, (after
long diligent serche but al in vayne,) to come at the laste to
the sight of them, by the meanes of a certein Apothecarie



</page>

<page no="141">
Whose diligence God graunte ours to folowe. They were
founde sayeth he, about Trident. 

But in that Musa Brasauolus, affirmeth that kinde of <term lang="la">Chal&#173;
canthum</term>, whiche is called <term lang="la">Roman<expan type="">um</expan></term>, to be the true <term lang="en">Misy</term>, semeth
to stande saieth he, both against truthe and reason: both be&#173;
cause <term lang="la">Vitriolum Romanum</term>, is neyther of a golden coloure, nor
glistereth as with sparkes when it is broken, but is more 
lyke the substance of glasse: And also that <term lang="en">Misy</term> is no medi&#173;
cine arteficiall, but is without arte made in the bowelles
of the earth. 

<term lang="la">Chalcanthum</term> hath power to bynde, to heate, and to induce
eschares. <term lang="la">Latas uentris tineas necat, drachm&#230; pondere deuoratum, aut
cum melle linctum</term>, and moueth vomite. Beynge drunke with
water, it helpeth against the venim of Musheroms. It pur<emend err="">&#173;</emend>
geth the head beyng watered, &#38; dropped into <expan type="">the</expan> nosethrilles
with woulle. 

Of <term lang="la">Chalcanthum</term>, that is estemed beste whiche is blewe,
rare, faste, and shyning: such is <term lang="la">Stillatium</term>, called of some
<term lang="la">Lonchoton</term>. The next is <term lang="la">Concretitium</term>, but the arteficiall is ap&#173;
ter for dyers, &#38; to make blewe colours with, then the reste:
but for the vse of medicine weaker. 
</xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Vlceratiua medicamina. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">OR <term lang="la">exulceratiua medicamina</term>, are suche medicines as by
Corrosion doe excoriate or exulcerate: and c<expan type="">om</expan>meth of
<term lang="la">Vlcerare</term>, or <term lang="la">exulcerare</term>, to blister or make vlcers. </xpln></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Vlcus. </form> 
<xpln lang="en">Galen in his fourth booke <term lang="la">De methodo medendi</term>, sayth: <term lang="la">Est
itaque morborum genus, diuortium continui vocatum. Quod qui&#173;
dem in carnosa parte, ulcus: in osse fractura: in neruo conuulsio
appellatur. &#38;c. </term>

That is. There is one kynde of diseases, called the sun&#173;
dryng of vnittie. whiche in a fleshie parte, is called <term lang="la">Vlcus</term>, In
the bone <term lang="la">Fractura</term>, and in the synewe, <term lang="la">conuulsio</term>. &#38;c. Whervn&#173;



</page>

<page no="142">
to quitture is alwayes incident: and maketh an exacte di&#173;
stinction, between it and <term lang="la">Vulnus</term>. More of it, and the deui&#173;
sions therof, you may see in maister Gales institution. It is
called in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x1F14;&#x03BB;&#x03BA;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan></term>, wherof commeth <term lang="gk">&#x1F14;&#x03BB;&#x03BA;<expan type="">&#x03BF;&#x03C2;</expan> &#x1F00;&#x03BD;&#x03AF;&#x03B1;&#x03C4;&#x03BF;&#x03BD;</term>,
<term lang="la">Hoc est lethale, seu insanabile ulcus</term>, a mortal or vncurable vlcer. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Elcos</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk">Elcos aniaton</term>. </note></wordentry>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="la">Vndimia. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="oedema(n)"><term lang="la">ORYdema</term>, or after others <term lang="la">Zimia</term>, but more rightly <term lang="la">Oede&#173;
ma</term>, is after Guido, and diuerse others of that age, of . ii. 
sortes: naturall and vnnaturall: the naturall caused
of naturall phlegme, or thinner then the same. The vnna&#173;
turall is none other wyse made, but either by the admixti<expan type="">on</expan>
of other humores: as choler, phlegme, &#38;c. Or by their affe&#173;
ctes in <expan type="">the</expan> same, as by heating driyng &#38;c. Loke more at <term lang="la">Oedema</term></xpln>

<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="ungula(n)">Vngula. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="web in the eye(n)">OR <term lang="la">Vnguis in oculo</term>, is that whiche we call the webbe in
the eye: Whiche groweth sayeth Galen, to the pan&#173;
nycle that couereth the eye outwardly, <term lang="la">Vsque ad Iridis
coronam</term>, called <term lang="la">agnata</term>, or <term lang="la">coh&#230;rens</term>. Whiche pannycle (sayth he)
beyng compased with inflammation, maye by accidens im&#173;
pedyte the syght of the eye: but <term lang="la">Chymosis</term> and <term lang="la">Vnguis</term>, doe by
a chiefe and principall reason, hyde and darken the pupill:
euen as the greate vnnaturall tumores of the cheake. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Vnguis</term>. </note>

tii causis. lib. 1</term>

pi. con. &#38; ri&#173;
gore</term>. </note>

<wordgroup1 type="a" object="Z">
<wordentry type="h">
<form lang="en" lexeme="zingiber(n)">Zingiber. </form> 
<xpln lang="en" lexeme="pepper(n)" lexeme="long pepper(n)" lexeme="ginger(n)">OR <term lang="la">Gingiber</term>, in Greeke <term lang="gk">&#x0396;&#x03B9;&#x03B3;&#x03B3;&#x03AF;&#x03B2;&#x03B5;&#x03C1;</term>, hath of an olde opini<expan type="">on</expan>
been iudged the roote of pepper: but that doth Pliny
denie. And Dioscorides describeth ginger in a seue&#173;
rall chapiter, after he hath spoken of pepper, saiynge. <term lang="la">Gingi&#173;



</page>

<page no="143">
beris</term>, is a plante of his owne kynde, growyng in Arabia Tro&#173;
glodytica, which they vse grene for many thinges, as we doe
<term lang="la">Ruta</term>, mixing it with their first meates and drynkes. The
rootes of this are little, lyke the rootes of <term lang="la">Cyperus</term>, whyte,
and odoriferus, and in taste lyke pepper: they oughte to be
chosen that are without woodwormes. They ar kept with
salte, because they will otherwyse soone putrefie: and are
brought into Italie, in earthen vesselles. Ginger is con&#173;
uenient in meates and in sauces, hauyng healyng and con&#173;
coctiue power. It gently mollifieth the belly, is vtile for the
stomache, And efficatious for the dymnesse, or dulnesse of
sight. It is put in medicines against venym, and answereth
in summe, to the vertues of pepper. Hitherto Dioscorides. 
Galen sayeth it heateth, but not at the first as pepper, &#38;
therfore is to be estemed of subtiller partes: and semeth to
retayne in it selfe a certeyne grosse and vnlaboured humi&#173;
ditie, lyke <term lang="la">Piperi longo</term>: And therfore doth the heate continue
longer, that is made by ginger or longe pepper, then of the
whyte, or blacke. They that haue seen Ginger in India,
(saith Ruellius,) affirme it to haue a creping roote, with
knottes and ioyntes: From the whiche, and from
the stalke, commeth often new rootes, geuing
twise or thryse in the yeare leaues lyke a
reede, but not so long: and that it re&#173;
sembleth wholy <term lang="la">Gramen</term>, neither
is there any thing in that 
region more com&#173;
mon. </xpln>
<note type="m"><term lang="gk"></term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Cyperus</term>. </note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">De sym. lib. 6. </term></note>
<note type="m"><term lang="la">Piper longum</term>. </note>
<lemenote>
"Zingiber" antedates the earliest OED citation (1902). 
</lemenote>
